### [1] [The Randomness Floor: Measuring Intrinsic Non-Randomness in Language Model Token Distributions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22771)
*Jarosław Hryszko*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They measure how non-random language models are, showing there is a built-in minimum level of structure in their outputs that depends on architecture and language, not just prompts or tokenization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how much of a language model’s apparent structure comes from its internal training vs. being driven by prompts, and whether different architectures and languages change this intrinsic non-randomness.

Method: They define Entropic Deviation (ED) as the normalized KL divergence between a model’s token distribution and a uniform distribution, then systematically measure ED across 31,200 generations varying model families (transformer vs. state space), prompt types, temperatures, and languages.

Result: Transformers show a consistent ED ≈ 0.30 even for semantically empty prompts, indicating most non-randomness is baked into the weights. Different transformer families converge on nearly identical ED, while a state space model exhibits much higher ED, lower within-sequence variance, and strong temperature sensitivity. Cross-lingual tests reveal a stable ED gradient across languages that is not explained by tokenization differences.

Conclusion: Language models have a structural lower bound on randomness determined largely by architecture and language rather than context, with transformers sharing similar bounds and state space models showing a distinct regime; language-specific effects persist beyond tokenization, suggesting deeper linguistic modulation of this intrinsic non-randomness.

Abstract: Language models cannot be random. This paper introduces Entropic Deviation (ED), the normalised KL divergence between a model's token distribution and the uniform distribution, and measures it systematically across 31,200 generations spanning seven models, two architectures (transformer and state space), nine prompt categories, three temperatures, and five languages. Under semantically neutral prompts (empty strings, random characters, nonsense syllables) transformers still exhibit ED of approximately 0.30, meaning that 88-93% of the non-randomness observed under semantic prompts is intrinsic to the learned weights rather than induced by context. Three transformer families (Gemma, Llama, Qwen) converge on nearly identical ED values despite different training data and vocabularies. A state space model (Mamba2) reveals a qualitatively different regime: twice the ED, three times lower within-sequence variance, and massive sensitivity to temperature (r = -0.78) where transformers are nearly immune (r < 0.05). Cross-lingual experiments with Qwen-32B show a stable gradient across five languages (English, Japanese, Chinese, Polish, Arabic) that does not correlate with token fertility and persists when two languages sharing an identical tokeniser subset are compared. These findings establish a structural lower bound on randomness in pretrained language models, characterise how this bound differs across architectures, and demonstrate that language itself modulates the bound independently of tokenisation.

</details>


### [2] [TexOCR: Advancing Document OCR Models for Compilable Page-to-LaTeX Reconstruction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22880)
*Chengye Wang,Lin Fu,Zexi Kuang,Yilun Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces TexOCR, a system and benchmark for reconstructing scientific PDFs into compilable LaTeX, emphasizing structural and compilation correctness beyond plain OCR.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most OCR systems focus on extracting plain text or Markdown from documents, ignoring the rich structure, executable nature, and compilation constraints of LaTeX that are crucial for scientific publishing and downstream reuse. There is a lack of benchmarks and training corpora that evaluate and train models on preserving LaTeX structure, compilability, and document invariants rather than just text transcription accuracy.

Method: The authors define a new task: page-level reconstruction of scientific PDFs into compilable LaTeX. They create TexOCR-Bench, a benchmark with multi-dimensional metrics that jointly measure transcription fidelity, structural faithfulness, and end-to-end compilability. They also build TexOCR-Train, a large-scale training corpus for this task. Using this corpus, they train a 2B-parameter model (TexOCR) via supervised fine-tuning to learn LaTeX reconstruction, and further apply reinforcement learning with verifiable, programmatic rewards based on LaTeX unit tests that check compilability and referential integrity (e.g., label-reference consistency, section structure, float placement).

Result: On TexOCR-Bench, evaluation of 21 frontier models shows that existing OCR or vision-language systems often break critical LaTeX document invariants, such as consistent section hierarchies, correct placement of figures/tables (floats), and valid label-reference links, leading to unreliable compilation and reduced usability. TexOCR, especially when trained with RL and verifiable rewards, improves these aspects over SFT-only training and outperforms baselines on structural and compilation-related metrics while maintaining transcription quality.

Conclusion: Reconstructing scientific PDFs into compilable LaTeX requires going beyond text accuracy to enforce structural and executable constraints. The TexOCR-Bench benchmark and TexOCR-Train corpus enable systematic evaluation and training for this goal. Incorporating reinforcement learning with verifiable, LaTeX-based rewards substantially improves structural faithfulness and compilability over supervised learning alone, highlighting the value of executable, programmatic feedback for document OCR models.

Abstract: Existing document OCR largely targets plain text or Markdown, discarding the structural and executable properties that make LaTeX essential for scientific publishing. We study page-level reconstruction of scientific PDFs into compilable LaTeX and introduce TexOCR-Bench, a benchmark, and TexOCR-Train, a large-scale training corpus, for this task. TexOCR-Bench features a multi-dimensional evaluation suite that jointly assesses transcription fidelity, structural faithfulness, and end-to-end compilability. Leveraging TexOCR-Train, we train a 2B-parameter model, TexOCR, using supervised fine-tuning (SFT) and reinforcement learning (RL) with verifiable rewards derived from LaTeX unit tests that directly enforce compilability and referential integrity. Experiments across 21 frontier models on TexOCR-Bench show that existing systems frequently violate key document invariants, including consistent section structure, correct float placement, and valid label-reference links, which undermines compilation reliability and downstream usability. Our analysis further reveals that RL with verifiable rewards yields consistent improvements over SFT alone, particularly on structural and compilation metrics.

</details>


### [3] [AutoPyVerifier: Learning Compact Executable Verifiers for Large Language Model Outputs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22937)
*Pouya Pezeshkpour,Estevam Hruschka*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces AutoPyVerifier, a system that automatically learns small sets of deterministic Python verifier functions that closely approximate desired evaluation objectives for LLM outputs, improving verification quality and downstream task performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing verification approaches for LLMs trade off between expressivity and reliability: LLM-based verifiers can represent complex criteria but are error-prone and hard to control, whereas deterministic executable verifiers are reliable and interpretable but tend to be narrow and manually engineered. There is a need for an automatic way to derive strong, deterministic verifiers that match a target objective such as output correctness, using only labeled examples of LLM outputs.

Method: The authors propose AutoPyVerifier, which starts from a development set of LLM outputs labeled according to a target objective (e.g., correctness). An LLM is used to synthesize candidate Python verifier functions. These candidates are organized into a directed acyclic graph (DAG) representing combinations or refinements of verifiers. A search over this DAG then selects a minimal subset of verifiers whose joint satisfaction best matches the target objective on the dev set. The process iteratively refines and prunes verifiers to find a compact, high-performing verifier set that is fully deterministic and executable.

Result: On benchmarks spanning mathematical reasoning, coding, function calling, and instruction following for multiple state-of-the-art LLMs, AutoPyVerifier significantly improves prediction of the target objective compared to initial, raw LLM-generated verifier sets, achieving up to a 55.0 F1 score improvement. Analyses reveal that which verification targets are most effective depends on the specific benchmark and model, and that the DAG-based search tends to favor more structural and semantically grounded checks. When the resulting verifier sets are exposed to an LLM as external tools, downstream task accuracy improves by up to 17.0 points.

Conclusion: Automatically inducing compact sets of deterministic Python verifiers from labeled LLM outputs is both feasible and beneficial. AutoPyVerifier leverages LLMs for initial verifier synthesis and a DAG-based search for refinement, yielding verifiers that better approximate evaluation objectives and enhance both verification quality and LLM performance when used as tools. This suggests a promising direction for building robust, interpretable verification layers around LLM systems.

Abstract: Verification is becoming central to both reinforcement-learning-based training and inference-time control of large language models (LLMs). Yet current verifiers face a fundamental trade-off: LLM-based verifiers are expressive but hard to control and prone to error, while deterministic executable verifiers are reliable and interpretable but often limited in capability. We study the following question: given a development set of LLM outputs and labels for a target objective, such as correctness, can we automatically induce a minimal set of Python verifiers whose joint satisfaction closely matches that objective? We propose AutoPyVerifier, a framework that uses an LLM to synthesize candidate verifier functions and then refines them through search over a directed acyclic graph (DAG). By navigating the DAG, AutoPyVerifier systematically explores the space of deterministic executable verifiers and selects a compact verifier set whose joint satisfaction best approximates the target objective. Across mathematical reasoning, coding, function calling, and instruction-following benchmarks for several state-of-the-art LLMs, AutoPyVerifier improves target-objective prediction by up to 55.0 F1 points over the initial LLM-generated verifier sets. Additional analyses show that the most useful verification targets vary by benchmark and model, and that the DAG-based search shifts the learned verifier sets toward more structural and semantically grounded checks. We further show that exposing the discovered verifier set to an LLM as an external tool improves downstream accuracy by up to 17.0 points. We release our code

</details>


### [4] [Self Knowledge Re-expression: A Fully Local Method for Adapting LLMs to Tasks Using Intrinsic Knowledge](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22939)
*Mengyu Wang,Xiaoying Zhi,Zhiyi Li,Robin Schmucker,Shay B. Cohen,Tiejun Ma,Fran Silavong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Self-Knowledge Re-expression (SKR), a task-agnostic adaptation method that repurposes LLM next-token prediction outputs into efficient, task-specific representations without supervision or distillation, yielding large gains on financial IR, object detection, and anomaly detection tasks, and outperforming strong retrieval baselines on MMDocRAG.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although LLMs trained with next-token prediction contain rich internal knowledge, their sequential generative interface is poorly matched to specialized, non-generative tasks (e.g., retrieval, detection), creating a performance bottleneck. The authors argue that the limitation lies not in knowledge acquisition but in how that knowledge is expressed at inference time, motivating a new mechanism to re-express internal knowledge into task-effective forms without costly supervision or model changes.

Method: They propose Self-Knowledge Re-expression (SKR), a fully local, task-agnostic adaptation method that operates on unannotated data. Instead of using raw next-token generation for every task, SKR transforms the LLM’s generic output into compact, task-specific expressions. The method does not rely on human labels or distillation from external models; it locally adapts how the model expresses what it already knows so that downstream components (e.g., retrievers, detectors) can use it more effectively.

Result: On a large financial document benchmark, SKR yields over 40% improvement in Recall@1 for information retrieval, more than 76% reduction in object detection latency, and above 33% increase in anomaly detection AUPRC. On the MMDocRAG dataset, their SKR-enhanced system surpasses strong retrieval baselines by at least 12.6%, showing that re-expressing model knowledge can materially improve diverse downstream tasks.

Conclusion: Reformulating how LLMs express their internal knowledge—rather than further training them to acquire new knowledge—can substantially improve performance on non-generative, specialized tasks. SKR offers a simple, local, and task-agnostic mechanism that exploits unannotated data to convert generic next-token outputs into efficient task-specific representations, achieving significant gains in retrieval, detection, and anomaly detection without supervision or distillation.

Abstract: While the next-token prediction (NTP) paradigm enables large language models (LLMs) to express their intrinsic knowledge, its sequential nature constrains performance on specialized, non-generative tasks. We attribute this performance bottleneck to the LLMs' knowledge expression mechanism, rather than to deficiencies in knowledge acquisition. To address this, we propose Self-Knowledge Re-expression (SKR), a novel, task-agnostic adaptation method. SKR transforms the LLM's output from generic token generation to highly efficient, task-specific expression. SKR is a fully local method that uses only unannotated data, requiring neither human supervision nor model distillation. Experiments on a large financial document dataset demonstrate substantial improvements: over 40% in Recall@1 for information retrieval tasks, over 76% reduction in object detection latency, and over 33% increase in anomaly detection AUPRC. Our results on the MMDocRAG dataset surpass those of leading retrieval models by at least 12.6%.

</details>


### [5] [Uncertainty Quantification for LLM Function-Calling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22985)
*Zihuiwen Ye,Lukas Aichberger,Michael Kirchhof,Sinead Williamson,Luca Zappella,Yarin Gal,Arno Blaas,Adam Golinski*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates how well different uncertainty quantification (UQ) methods work for large language models when they call external functions, and proposes FC-specific tweaks that significantly improve both multi-sample and single-sample UQ.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Function-calling LLMs are increasingly used to autonomously perform high-stakes real-world actions like transactions or data modification. Incorrect function calls can be irreversible and harmful, so it is crucial to estimate the model’s confidence before executing them. Existing UQ methods were mostly evaluated on standard NLP tasks (e.g., QA) and not on the more structured function-calling setting, leaving a gap in understanding how suitable those UQ methods are and how they might be improved for FC.

Method: The authors conduct what they claim is the first systematic evaluation of uncertainty quantification methods specifically for LLM function-calling. They compare multi-sample UQ approaches (e.g., Semantic Entropy, which uses multiple model samples) with simpler single-sample, logit-based UQ methods. They then propose FC-specific enhancements: for multi-sample methods, they cluster different function-call outputs according to their abstract syntax trees (ASTs); for single-sample methods, they compute uncertainty only over semantically important tokens in the function call rather than all tokens.

Result: They find that, unlike in natural language QA tasks where Semantic Entropy and other multi-sample UQ methods perform strongly, in the function-calling setting these methods do not outperform simple single-sample UQ. However, when they exploit structural properties of function calls—AST-based clustering for multi-sample methods and token selection for single-sample methods—both types of UQ methods perform better at estimating correctness/uncertainty of function calls.

Conclusion: Uncertainty quantification for LLM function-calling behaves differently than in free-form NLP tasks: sophisticated multi-sample methods offer no inherent advantage over basic single-sample approaches unless adapted to the structured nature of function calls. By leveraging function-call syntax (ASTs) and focusing on semantically meaningful tokens, existing UQ techniques can be significantly improved in this domain, highlighting the importance of task- and format-aware UQ design for safe, autonomous LLM tool use.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed to autonomously solve real-world tasks. A key ingredient for this is the LLM Function-Calling paradigm, a widely used approach for equipping LLMs with tool-use capabilities. However, an LLM calling functions incorrectly can have severe implications, especially when their effects are irreversible, e.g., transferring money or deleting data. Hence, it is of paramount importance to consider the LLM's confidence that a function call solves the task correctly prior to executing it. Uncertainty Quantification (UQ) methods can be used to quantify this confidence and prevent potentially incorrect function calls. In this work, we present what is, to our knowledge, the first evaluation of UQ methods for LLM Function-Calling (FC). While multi-sample UQ methods, such as Semantic Entropy, show strong performance for natural language Q&A tasks, we find that in the FC setting, it offers no clear advantage over simple single-sample UQ methods. Additionally, we find that the particularities of FC outputs can be leveraged to improve the performance of existing UQ methods in this setting. Specifically, multi-sample UQ methods benefit from clustering FC outputs based on their abstract syntax tree parsing, while single-sample UQ methods can be improved by selecting only semantically meaningful tokens when calculating logit-based uncertainty scores.

</details>


### [6] [Chinese-SkillSpan: A Span-Level Dataset for ESCO-Aligned Competency Extraction from Chinese Job Ads](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23009)
*Guojing Li,Zichuan Fu,Junyi Li,Wenxia Zhou,Xinyang Wu,Jinning Yang,Jingtong Gao,Feng Huang,Xiangyu Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Chinese-SkillSpan, the first large-scale, ESCO-aligned Chinese Job Skill Named Entity Recognition (NER) dataset created via an LLM-assisted macro-micro collaborative annotation pipeline, enabling effective training and evaluation of JobSkillNER models for intelligent recruitment.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a lack of high-quality, large-scale, and standardized Chinese datasets for Job Skill Named Entity Recognition in recruitment texts, which limits the development of intelligent recruitment systems, job–candidate matching, and personalized employment services in the Chinese labor market. Existing resources do not align well with international occupational skill standards and are costly to create purely by manual annotation.

Method: The authors design Chinese-specific annotation guidelines for job postings and develop an LLM-empowered Macro–Micro collaborative annotation pipeline. First, large language models perform macro-level, context-aware automatic initial annotations of skill entities from raw job postings. Then human experts conduct micro-level, sentence-wise adjudication and refinement of these annotations. Using this pipeline, they collect job postings from four major recruitment platforms (2014–2025), extract and normalize skill spans, and align them with the ESCO skill taxonomy along four dimensions (knowledge, skill, transversal competence, language competence). They finally use the dataset to train and evaluate JobSkillNER models.

Result: The pipeline yields more than 20,000 annotated instances of job skill entities from Chinese recruitment texts, structured and labeled according to ESCO’s four LSKT dimensions. Experiments demonstrate that models trained on Chinese-SkillSpan achieve strong performance on JobSkillNER tasks and that the dataset serves as an effective benchmark for evaluation, confirming its utility for downstream intelligent recruitment applications.

Conclusion: Chinese-SkillSpan is the first ESCO-aligned, large-scale Chinese JobSkillNER dataset and successfully supports effective model training and fair evaluation. The LLM-assisted macro–micro annotation pipeline proves practical for high-quality, cost-efficient dataset construction. This resource fills a key gap in Chinese JobSkillNER and offers a benchmark that can accelerate research and application development in intelligent recruitment and labor-market analytics.

Abstract: Job Skill Named Entity Recognition (JobSkillNER) aims to automatically extract key skill information from large-scale job posting data, which is important for improving talent-market matching efficiency and supporting personalized employment services. To the best of our knowledge, this work presents the first Chinese JobSkillNER dataset for recruitment texts. We propose annotation guidelines tailored to Chinese job postings and an LLM-empowered Macro-Micro collaborative annotation pipeline. The pipeline leverages the contextual understanding ability of large language models (LLMs) for initial annotation and then refines the results through expert sentence-level adjudication. Using this pipeline, we annotate more than 20,000 instances collected from four major recruitment platforms over the period 2014-2025. Based on these efforts, we release Chinese-SkillSpan, the first Chinese JobSkillNER dataset aligned with the ESCO occupational skill standard across four dimensions: knowledge, skill, transversal competence, and language competence (LSKT). Experimental results show that the dataset supports effective model training and evaluation, indicating that Chinese-SkillSpan helps fill a major gap in Chinese JobSkillNER resources and provides a useful benchmark for intelligent recruitment research. Code and data are available at https://sites.google.com/view/cn-skillspan-resources .

</details>


### [7] [Evaluating Temporal Consistency in Multi-Turn Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23051)
*Yash Kumar Atri,Steven L. Johnson,Tom Hartvigsen*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces ChronoScope, a large-scale benchmark to test how well language models maintain and update time-specific factual assumptions across multi-turn dialogues, revealing that current models often fail to preserve correct temporal scope and drift to present-day assumptions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations mostly test language models on single-turn factual accuracy, but real interactions are multi-turn and require models to remember and correctly update temporal context (e.g., which facts were true in which year). Models often implicitly assume the present tense and can lose track of earlier time-scoped information. The authors aim to systematically diagnose and quantify this temporal scope stability problem in a controlled way.

Method: The authors construct ChronoScope, a large-scale diagnostic benchmark derived from Wikidata, consisting of over one million deterministically generated multi-turn question chains. These chains are designed to probe whether models can infer and maintain temporal scope across turns when explicit time expressions may be omitted. The benchmark covers several behaviors: implicit temporal carryover, explicit scope switching between times, transferring temporal scope across related entities, and reasoning over longer temporal trajectories. They then evaluate state-of-the-art language models on these chains, including conditions where the relevant prior context is perfectly provided (oracle context), to isolate temporal reasoning from knowledge gaps.

Result: Across extensive evaluations, state-of-the-art language models frequently violate temporal scope stability. Even when models possess the correct underlying factual knowledge, they often revert to default present-day assumptions in follow-up questions, failing to respect previously inferred time scopes. The error rates grow as interaction length increases, and the failures remain significant even under oracle context conditions where all necessary prior turns are supplied verbatim.

Conclusion: There is a substantial gap between achieving high single-turn factual accuracy and maintaining coherent temporal reasoning in multi-turn dialogue. Current language models struggle to preserve, override, and transfer temporal scope information reliably over sequences of turns, especially as conversations grow longer. ChronoScope provides a public benchmark and evaluation suite to systematically study and improve temporal scope stability in interactive language model systems.

Abstract: Language models are increasingly deployed in interactive settings where users reason about facts over time rather than in isolation. In such scenarios, correct behavior requires models to maintain and update implicit temporal assumptions established earlier in a conversation. We study this challenge through the lens of temporal scope stability: the ability to preserve, override, or transfer time-scoped factual context across dialogue turns. We introduce ChronoScope, a large-scale diagnostic benchmark designed to isolate temporal scope behavior in controlled multi-turn interactions, comprising over one million deterministically generated question chains grounded in Wikidata. ChronoScope evaluates whether models can correctly retain inferred temporal scope when follow-up questions omit explicit time references, spanning implicit carryover, explicit scope switching, cross-entity transfer, and longer temporal trajectories. Through extensive evaluation of state-of-the-art language models, we find that temporal scope stability is frequently violated in controlled multi-turn settings, with models often drifting toward present-day assumptions despite correct underlying knowledge. These failures intensify with interaction length and persist even under oracle context conditions, revealing a gap between single-turn factual accuracy and coherent temporal reasoning under sequential interaction. We make our dataset and evaluation suite publicly available at https://github.com/yashkumaratri/ChronoScope

</details>


### [8] [DeepImagine: Learning Biomedical Reasoning via Successive Counterfactual Imagining](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23054)
*Youze Zheng,Jianyou Wang,Yuhan Chen,Matthew Feng,Longtian Bao,Hanyuan Zhang,Maxim Khan,Aditya K. Sehgal,Christopher D. Rosin,Umber Dube,Ramamohan Paturi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DeepImagine is a framework that teaches LLMs to reason about clinical trial outcomes via counterfactual imagination, improving prediction performance and interpretability over standard models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLMs and traditional models perform poorly at predicting outcomes of future clinical trials because they mainly learn correlational patterns and lack explicit representations of underlying biomedical mechanisms. There is a need for methods that can move models toward mechanistic, causal-style reasoning so they can better generalize to new trial settings and offer interpretable insights.

Method: DeepImagine approximates hidden causal mechanisms by training LLMs to predict how clinical trial results would change under controlled perturbations of trial attributes (dose, endpoints, arms, geography, etc.). The authors construct two kinds of counterfactual supervision from real trials: (1) strict counterfactuals, such as different doses or outcome measures within the same trial, used for supervised fine-tuning; and (2) approximate counterfactual pairs retrieved from similar-but-not-identical trials, optimized via reinforcement learning using verifiable benchmark-based rewards. Training is further enhanced with synthetic reasoning traces that explain local counterfactual transitions in causally plausible ways. Models under 10B parameters (e.g., Qwen3.5-9B) are trained within this framework.

Result: The framework yields LLMs that outperform untuned language models and traditional correlational baselines on clinical trial outcome prediction benchmarks. The learned counterfactual reasoning trajectories also encode structure that correlates with trial-level mechanisms, making model behavior more transparent and interpretable.

Conclusion: Training LLMs through successive counterfactual imagining of clinical trial variations can improve both predictive accuracy and mechanistic interpretability. DeepImagine offers a practical path for building sub-10B biomedical LLMs that reason more like causal models over trial attributes, providing more scientifically useful and explainable predictions for clinical research.

Abstract: Predicting the outcomes of prospective clinical trials remains a major challenge for large language models. Prior work has shown that both traditional correlational predictors, such as random forests and logistic regression, and strong commercial LLMs achieve limited performance on this task. In this paper, we propose DeepImagine, a framework for teaching LLMs biomedical reasoning through successive counterfactual imagining. The central idea is to approximate hidden causal mechanisms of clinical trials by training models to infer how observed trial results would change under controlled perturbations of experimental conditions, such as dosage, outcome measures, study arms, geography, and other trial attributes. To support this objective, we construct both natural and approximate counterfactual pairs from real clinical trials with reported outcomes. For settings where strict counterfactual supervision is available, such as paired outcome measures or dose-ranging study arms within the same trial, we train models with supervised fine-tuning. For broader settings where only approximate counterfactual pairs can be retrieved, we optimize models with reinforcement learning using verifiable rewards based on downstream benchmark correctness. We further augment training with synthetic reasoning traces that provide causally plausible explanations for local counterfactual transitions. Using this pipeline, we train language models under 10B parameters, including Qwen3.5-9B, and evaluate them on clinical trial outcome prediction. We aim to show that DeepImagine consistently improves over untuned language models and traditional correlational baselines. Finally, we aim to show that the learned reasoning trajectories provide interpretable signals about how models represent trial-level mechanisms, suggesting a practical path toward more mechanistic and scientifically useful biomedical language models.

</details>


### [9] [Implicit Framing in Obstetric Counseling Notes: A Grounded LLM Pipeline on a VBAC-Eligible Cohort](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23059)
*Baris Karacan,Barbara Di Eugenio,Patrick Thornton,Joanna Tess,Subhash Kumar Kolar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how doctors’ wording when counseling about delivery options (VBAC vs repeat C‑section) differs, using large language models to extract and categorize counseling language from obstetric notes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical outcomes can be affected not only by medical facts but by how these facts are linguistically framed to patients. In obstetrics, counseling about delivery mode (VBAC vs repeat C-section) is high stakes, yet there has been little large-scale, systematic study of the language clinicians use. Existing analyses are limited by confounding from clinical contraindications and lack of scalable tools to analyze narrative notes. The authors aim to fill this gap by creating a rigorously defined VBAC-eligible cohort and using LLMs to characterize counseling frames in real-world documentation.

Method: 1) Build a VBAC-eligible cohort by combining structured EHR data with an LLM-based extraction pipeline that only uses grounded, verbatim evidence from free-text notes to exclude contraindications. 2) From 2,024 obstetric history and physical narratives, identify counseling segments related to delivery options. 3) Apply a zero-shot LLM framework to assign these segments to predefined framing categories (e.g., risk-focused vs other frames) that describe how VBAC and repeat C-section options are linguistically presented. 4) Compare framing distributions between VBAC and RCS notes and perform statistical tests on category-level differences.

Result: The analysis finds a significant difference in how counseling is framed between VBAC and repeat C-section documentation. In particular, risk-focused language constitutes a much larger proportion of counseling segments in RCS notes than in VBAC notes. These differences in framing distributions are statistically validated, indicating systematic variation in counseling language depending on the delivery mode documented.

Conclusion: Controlled, LLM-based analysis of counseling language can reveal systematic framing differences in obstetric care. In this VBAC-eligible cohort, RCS counseling is more heavily framed around risks compared with VBAC counseling. This demonstrates both the influence of clinical framing on documented counseling and the utility of constrained, evidence-grounded LLM pipelines for large-scale analysis of narrative clinical text in high-stakes decision contexts.

Abstract: Clinical framing -- the linguistic manner in which clinical information is presented -- can influence patient understanding and decision-making, with important implications for healthcare outcomes. Obstetrics is a high-stakes domain in which physicians counsel patients on delivery mode choices such as vaginal birth after cesarean (VBAC) and repeat cesarean section (RCS), yet counseling language remains underexplored in large-scale clinical text analysis. In this work, we analyze physician counseling language in 2,024 obstetric history and physical narratives for a rigorously defined cohort of patients for whom both VBAC and RCS were clinically viable options. To control for confounding due to medical contraindications, we first construct a VBAC-eligible cohort using structured clinical data supplemented by a large language model (LLM)-based extraction pipeline constrained to grounded, verbatim evidence from free-text narratives. We then apply a zero-shot LLM framework to categorize counseling segments into predefined framing categories capturing how physicians linguistically present delivery options. Our analysis reveals a significant difference in counseling framing distributions between VBAC and RCS notes; risk-focused language accounts for a substantially larger share of counseling segments in RCS documentation than in VBAC, with category-level differences confirmed by statistical testing, highlighting the value of controlled LLM-based framing analysis in obstetric care.

</details>


### [10] [ContextWeaver: Selective and Dependency-Structured Memory Construction for LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23069)
*Yating Wu,Yuhao Zhang,Sayan Ghosh,Sourya Basu,Anoop Deoras,Jun Huan,Gaurav Gupta*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes ContextWeaver, a dependency-structured memory framework that organizes LLM agent interaction history into a graph of reasoning steps to manage long context more effectively than sliding windows or simple retrieval.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents degrade on long, multi-step tasks because standard context management (sliding windows, prompt compression, retrieval) either drops earlier information or ignores the causal/logical structure of reasoning, which is crucial for tool-using agents solving complex problems.

Method: Model the agent’s interaction trace as a dependency graph where each step is linked to the earlier steps it relies on; build mechanisms to (1) construct and traverse this dependency graph, (2) summarize root-to-step reasoning paths into compact, reusable dependency summaries, and (3) validate and update the memory using execution feedback through a lightweight validation layer.

Result: On SWE-Bench Verified and SWE-Bench Lite, ContextWeaver outperforms a sliding-window baseline on pass@1 while using fewer reasoning steps and fewer tokens, showing both accuracy and efficiency gains.

Conclusion: Explicitly modeling logical dependencies in the interaction history yields a more stable and scalable memory mechanism for tool-using LLM agents, enabling better performance on long-context, multi-step reasoning tasks compared to traditional context management approaches.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents often struggle in long-context interactions. As the agent accumulates more interaction history, context management approaches such as sliding window and prompt compression may omit earlier structured information that later steps rely on. Recent retrieval-based memory systems surface relevant content but still overlook the causal and logical structure needed for multi-step reasoning. We introduce ContextWeaver, a selective and dependency-structured memory framework that organizes an agent's interaction trace into a graph of reasoning steps and selects the relevant context for future actions. Unlike prior context management approaches, ContextWeaver supports: (1) dependency-based construction and traversal that link each step to the earlier steps it relies on; (2) compact dependency summarization that condenses root-to-step reasoning paths into reusable units; and (3) a lightweight validation layer that incorporates execution feedback. On the SWE-Bench Verified and Lite benchmarks, ContextWeaver improves performance over a sliding-window baseline in pass@1, while reducing reasoning steps and token usage. Our observations suggest that modeling logical dependencies provides a stable and scalable memory mechanism for LLM agents that use tools.

</details>


### [11] [Mixture of Heterogeneous Grouped Experts for Language Modeling](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23108)
*Zhicheng Ma,Xiang Liu,Zhaoxiang Liu,Ning Wang,Yi Shen,Kai Wang,Shuming Shi,Shiguo Lian*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes MoHGE, a new Mixture-of-Experts framework that uses heterogeneous grouped experts and two-level routing to achieve MoE-level performance with ~20% fewer parameters and balanced GPU utilization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard MoE models use uniformly sized experts, which misaligns computation with token difficulty. Heterogeneous experts can, in theory, allocate more capacity to hard tokens and less to easy ones, improving efficiency. However, existing heterogeneous MoE designs cause system problems: severe GPU load imbalance, inefficient parameter utilization, and difficulty in practical large-scale deployment. The paper aims to design a heterogeneous MoE architecture that is both compute-efficient and system-friendly for real-world LLM inference.

Method: The authors propose Mixture of Heterogeneous Grouped Experts (MoHGE). Experts are organized into groups of different sizes, and a two-level routing mechanism first selects an expert group and then selects experts within that group. They introduce: (1) a Group-Wise Auxiliary Loss that encourages tokens of different difficulty levels to be routed to groups that are most parameter-efficient for them, improving inference efficiency; (2) an All-size Group-decoupling Allocation strategy that assigns groups across GPUs to avoid load imbalance when group sizes differ; and (3) an Intra-Group Experts Auxiliary Loss to encourage balanced routing among experts within each group. Together, these mechanisms maintain both computational and load balance while exploiting heterogeneity in expert capacity.

Result: Experiments show that MoHGE achieves performance comparable to standard homogeneous MoE architectures while using about 20% fewer parameters. At the same time, it preserves balanced GPU utilization despite having heterogeneous expert sizes. This indicates that the proposed grouping, routing, and auxiliary loss strategies successfully reconcile heterogeneity with system-level efficiency.

Conclusion: MoHGE demonstrates that carefully structured heterogeneity in MoE—via grouped experts, two-level routing, and tailored auxiliary losses—can reduce parameters and inference cost without sacrificing accuracy or GPU balance. The approach provides a practical, scalable blueprint for deploying resource-efficient heterogeneous MoE-based LLMs in industrial scenarios, overcoming the usual system bottlenecks of heterogeneous experts.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) based on Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) are pivotal in industrial applications for their ability to scale performance efficiently. However, standard MoEs enforce uniform expert sizes,creating a rigidity that fails to align computational costs with varying token-level complexity. While heterogeneous expert architectures attempt to address this by diversifying expert sizes, they often suffer from significant system-level challenges, specifically unbalanced GPU utilization and inefficient parameter utilization, which hinder practical deployment. To bridge the gap between theoretical heterogeneity and robust industrial application, we propose Mixture of Heterogeneous Grouped Experts (MoHGE) which introduces a two-level routing mechanism to enable flexible, resource-aware expert combinations. To optimize inference efficiency, we propose a Group-Wise Auxiliary Loss, which dynamically steers tokens to the most parameter-efficient expert groups based on task difficulty. To address the critical deployment challenge of GPU load balancing, we introduce an All-size Group-decoupling Allocation strategy coupled with an Intra-Group Experts Auxiliary Loss. These mechanisms collectively ensure uniform computation distribution across GPUs. Extensive evaluations demonstrate that MoHGE matches the performance of MoE architectures while reducing the total parameters by approximately 20% and maintaining balanced GPU utilization. Our work establishes a scalable paradigm for resource-efficient MoE design, offering a practical solution for optimizing inference costs in real-world scenarios.

### [12] [Mechanistic Steering of LLMs Reveals Layer-wise Feature Vulnerabilities in Adversarial Settings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23130)
*Nilanjana Das,Manas Gaur*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates why large language models can still be jailbroken despite safety alignment, showing that specific internal feature subgroups in mid-to-late layers are disproportionately responsible for harmful outputs, and that steering these features significantly changes model harmfulness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although large language models are safety-aligned, they remain vulnerable to jailbreak prompts that elicit harmful responses. Existing work focuses on designing attacks or defenses at the prompt level, without clarifying what internal mechanisms in the model drive jailbreak success. The authors aim to determine whether jailbreak vulnerability is associated with identifiable internal features and specific layers, so that safety interventions can move from ad-hoc prompting tricks to principled, mechanistic approaches.

Method: Using Gemma-2-2B and the BeaverTails adversarial dataset, the authors build a three-stage analysis and steering pipeline. (1) They compute a subspace similarity measure to extract tokens in adversarial responses that are strongly aligned with harmful concepts. (2) For these concept-aligned tokens, they identify sparse autoencoder (SAE) features across all 26 layers and group them into subgroups using three strategies: clustering-based grouping, hierarchical-linkage grouping, and a single-token-driven grouping method. (3) They perform feature-level steering by amplifying the top-ranked features in each subgroup during generation and then quantify how this manipulation affects output harmfulness using a standardized LLM-judge scoring protocol.

Result: Across all three feature-grouping strategies, feature subgroups in mid-to-late layers (specifically layers 16–25) show the highest vulnerability to steering: amplifying these features causes the strongest increases in harmfulness scores. Earlier layer features are comparatively less influential in producing unsafe content. The convergence of results across all three independent grouping methods strengthens the finding that these later-layer subgroups are systematically tied to the model’s harmful behaviors under jailbreak prompts.

Conclusion: Jailbreak vulnerability in Gemma-2-2B is not just a surface-level prompt phenomenon but is localized in specific internal feature subgroups, primarily in mid-to-late layers. Because steering these features significantly modulates harmfulness, targeted feature-level interventions (e.g., editing or suppressing these features) may offer a more robust and principled route to adversarial safety than relying solely on prompt-level defenses.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) can still be jailbroken into producing harmful outputs despite safety alignment. Existing attacks show this vulnerability, but not the internal mechanisms that cause it. This study asks whether jailbreak success is driven by identifiable internal features rather than prompts alone. We propose a three-stage pipeline for Gemma-2-2B using the BeaverTails dataset. First, we extract concept-aligned tokens from adversarial responses via subspace similarity. Second, we apply three feature-grouping strategies (cluster, hierarchical-linkage, and single-token-driven) to identify SAE feature subgroups for the aligned tokens across all 26 model layers. Third, we steer the model by amplifying the top features from each identified subgroup and measure the change in harmfulness score using a standardized LLM-judge scoring protocol. In all three approaches, the features in the layers [16-25] were relatively more vulnerable to steering. All three methods confirmed that mid to later layer feature subgroups are more responsible for unsafe outputs. These results provide evidence that the jailbreak vulnerability in Gemma-2-2B is localized to feature subgroups of mid to later layers, suggesting that targeted feature-level interventions may offer a more principled path to adversarial robustness than current prompt-level defenses.

</details>

Detecting harmful or sensitive content in memes requires accurate modeling of these multimodal cues. Existing CLIP-based approaches rely on static fusion, which struggles to capture fine grained dependencies between modalities. We propose DARC-CLIP, a CLIP-based framework for adaptive multimodal fusion with a hierarchical refinement stack. DARC-CLIP introduces Adaptive Cross-Attention Refiners to for bidirectional information alignment and Dynamic Feature Adapters for task-sensitive signal adaptation. We evaluate DARC-CLIP on the PrideMM benchmark, which includes hate, target, stance, and humor classification, and further test generalization on the CrisisHateMM dataset. DARC-CLIP achieves highly competitive classification accuracy across tasks, with significant gains of +4.18 AUROC and +6.84 F1 in hate detection over the strongest baseline. Ablation studies confirm that ACAR and DFA are the main contributors to these gains. These results show that adaptive cross-signal refinement is an effective strategy for multimodal content analysis in socially sensitive classification.

### [14] [Measuring Temporal Linguistic Emergence in Diffusion Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23235)
*Harry Lu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Study of how information emerges over denoising time in diffusion language models (LLaDA-8B) on WikiText-103.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To exploit the explicit denoising trajectory in diffusion language models to understand when different linguistic and semantic properties become measurable and how model uncertainty and sensitivity evolve during generation.

Method: Run three independent 32-step denoising trajectories of LLaDA-8B-Base on masked WikiText-103. Save intermediate states and train linear probes on 1,000 sequences (with 200 for evaluation) to measure: (1) token commitment timing; (2) linear recoverability of POS tags, coarse semantic categories, and token identity; (3) evolution of confidence and entropy; (4) sensitivity to mid-trajectory re-masking, decomposed into direct vs collateral effects.

Result: Consistent patterns across random seeds: (1) Content-word categories stabilize earlier than function-heavy categories. (2) POS and coarse semantic labels stay more linearly recoverable than exact lexical identity across the trajectory. (3) Tokens that end up wrong retain higher uncertainty; late confidence becomes less well calibrated. (4) Perturbation sensitivity peaks mid-trajectory and is highly localized to re-masked positions according to the direct/collateral decomposition.

Conclusion: Denoising time provides a useful axis for analyzing diffusion language models. In this LLaDA+WikiText setup, models recover coarse linguistic and semantic information earlier and more robustly than exact word identity, trajectory-level uncertainty is predictive of final correctness, and mid-trajectory states are the most sensitive to interventions, primarily at directly perturbed positions.

Abstract: Diffusion language models expose an explicit denoising trajectory, making it possible to ask when different kinds of information become measurable during generation. We study three independent 32-step runs of LLaDA-8B-Base on masked WikiText-103 text, each with 1{,}000 probe-training sequences and 200 held-out evaluation sequences. From saved trajectories, we derive four temporal measurements: token commitment; linear recoverability of part-of-speech (POS), coarse semantic category, and token identity; confidence and entropy dynamics; and sensitivity under mid-trajectory re-masking. Across seeds, the same ordering recurs: content categories stabilize earlier than function-heavy categories, POS and coarse semantic labels remain substantially more linearly recoverable than exact lexical identity under our probe setup, uncertainty remains higher for tokens that ultimately resolve incorrectly even though late confidence becomes less calibrated, and perturbation sensitivity peaks in the middle of the trajectory. A direct/collateral decomposition shows that this peak is overwhelmingly local to the perturbed positions themselves. In this LLaDA+WikiText setting, denoising time is therefore a useful analysis axis: under our measurements, coarse labels are recovered earlier and more robustly than lexical identity, trajectory-level uncertainty tracks eventual correctness, and mid-trajectory states are the most intervention-sensitive.

### [15] [Small Language Model Helps Resolve Semantic Ambiguity of LLM Prompt](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23263)
*Zhenzhen Huang,Chaoning Zhang,Fachrina Dewi Puspitasari,Jiaquan Zhang,Yitian Zhou,Shuxu Chen,Yang Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes an explicit prompt disambiguation step, performed by small language models before LLM inference, to detect and resolve semantic ambiguities in user prompts and thereby improve complex reasoning performance at low cost.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are strong at complex reasoning but are highly sensitive to how user prompts are written. Real user prompts are often informal, syntactically loose, and semantically ambiguous. These ambiguities create multiple plausible interpretations and reasoning paths, which can mislead the LLM and degrade performance. Existing methods mainly tweak or edit queries during LLM inference, without directly resolving the underlying semantic ambiguity in the prompt itself. The authors aim to directly tackle this root cause by systematically disambiguating prompts before they are fed to the LLM.

Method: They introduce a pre-inference prompt optimization pipeline based on explicit prompt disambiguation, primarily executed by small language models (SLMs) for efficiency. The pipeline (1) detects semantic risks or potentially ambiguous parts in the prompt, (2) examines these from multiple perspectives to test consistency, (3) identifies and resolves semantic conflicts, and (4) restructures the cleaned, disambiguated content into a logically organized form that is then passed as input to the main LLM. This process is designed to sharpen the LLM’s attention on semantically essential tokens without modifying the LLM’s internal architecture or inference mechanism.

Result: Across multiple reasoning benchmarks, the proposed disambiguation step yields an average improvement of about 2.5 points in reasoning performance. This gain is achieved at very low computational and monetary cost—reported as roughly $0.02—because the main work is offloaded to efficient SLMs rather than expensive large models.

Conclusion: Explicit prompt disambiguation, carried out as a pre-inference optimization step by small language models, is an effective way to reduce semantic ambiguity in user prompts and improve LLM reasoning. The approach enhances attention to important prompt tokens, yields measurable performance gains at minimal cost, and does so without modifying or interfering with the internal inference mechanisms of large language models. The authors advocate treating explicit disambiguation as a practical and scalable form of prompt optimization.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly utilized in various complex reasoning tasks due to their excellent instruction following capability. However, the model's performance is highly dependent on the open-ended characteristics of the users' input prompt. Natural prompts often do not follow proper syntactic rules, which creates ambiguous queries that yield multiple interpretations. Such ambiguous prompts confuse the model in choosing the correct reasoning paths to answer questions. Prior works address this challenge by applying query editing during the LLM inference process without explicitly solving the root cause of the ambiguity. To address this limitation, we propose a pre-inference prompt optimization mechanism via explicit prompt disambiguation. Particularly, we identify semantic risks in the prompt, check their multi-perspective consistency, and resolve any semantic conflicts that arise. Finally, we organize the resolved ambiguities in a logically structured manner as a clean input to the LLM. By explicitly resolving semantic ambiguity, our method can produce a more focused attention distribution to the semantically essential tokens. We also leverage small language models (SLMs) as the main executor of prompt disambiguation to benefit from their efficient computation. Through comprehensive experiments on multiple benchmarks, we demonstrate that our method improves reasoning performance by 2.5 points at a cost of only \$0.02. Our study promotes explicit prompt disambiguation as an effective prompt optimization method without disturbing the internal mechanism of LLM inference.

### [16] [Fine-tuning vs. In-context Learning in Large Language Models: A Formal Language Learning Perspective](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23267)
*Bishwamittra Ghosh,Soumi Das,Till Speicher,Qinyuan Wu,Mohammad Aflah Khan,Deepak Garg,Krishna P. Gummadi,Evimaria Terzi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper compares fine-tuning (FT) and in-context learning (ICL) for LLMs using formal language learning as a clean testbed, showing FT achieves higher in-distribution proficiency while both are similar out-of-distribution, and revealing nuanced differences in their inductive biases and sensitivities.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior comparisons between fine-tuning and in-context learning have been inconsistent and inconclusive because they used different tasks, datasets, and evaluation protocols, often with data contamination and unclear language boundaries. The authors want a rigorous, controlled way to compare these two fundamental learning modes of LLMs and to understand their relative language proficiency and inductive biases.

Method: They design a formal language learning benchmark where languages have precise, well-defined boundaries and samples of strings can be tightly controlled, avoiding data contamination. They introduce a discriminative evaluation: an LLM is judged by whether it assigns higher generation probability to strings that belong to a target formal language than to strings that do not. Using this setup, they systematically train and test LLMs under both fine-tuning and in-context learning, measuring proficiency and analyzing correlations in string probabilities to probe inductive biases, across model sizes, families, and different token vocabularies.

Result: (a) Fine-tuning yields better language proficiency than in-context learning on in-distribution generalization, while both modes perform similarly well on out-of-distribution generalization. (b) The inductive biases of FT and ICL, quantified via correlations in string generation probabilities, are similar when the language is only partially learned, but they diverge as proficiency increases. (c) In-context learning is much more heterogeneous across model sizes, architectures, and language tokenizations, whereas FT is relatively stable, and ICL performance is notably sensitive to the token vocabulary used to represent the formal language.

Conclusion: Formal languages provide a powerful and clean testbed for dissecting LLM behavior, allowing a more rigorous comparison between fine-tuning and in-context learning than natural language tasks typically permit. Fine-tuning generally achieves higher in-distribution proficiency and exhibits more stable behavior across models, while in-context learning is more fragile and vocabulary-sensitive, with inductive biases that diverge from those of fine-tuning at higher proficiency levels. These insights help clarify when and how to use FT vs. ICL and suggest that formal-language-based benchmarks are valuable tools for understanding and evaluating LLM learning modes.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) operate in two fundamental learning modes - fine-tuning (FT) and in-context learning (ICL) - raising key questions about which mode yields greater language proficiency and whether they differ in their inductive biases. Prior studies comparing FT and ICL have yielded mixed and inconclusive results due to inconsistent experimental setups. To enable a rigorous comparison, we propose a formal language learning task - offering precise language boundaries, controlled string sampling, and no data contamination - and introduce a discriminative test for language proficiency, where an LLM succeeds if it assigns higher generation probability to in-language strings than to out-of-language strings.
  Empirically, we find that: (a) FT has greater language proficiency than ICL on in-distribution generalization, but both perform equally well on out-of-distribution generalization. (b) Their inductive biases, measured by the correlation in string generation probabilities, are similar when both modes partially learn the language but diverge at higher proficiency levels. (c) Unlike FT, ICL performance differs substantially across models of varying sizes and families and is sensitive to the token vocabulary of the language. Thus, our work demonstrates the promise of formal languages as a controlled testbed for evaluating LLMs, behaviors that are difficult to isolate in natural language datasets. Our source code is available at https://github.com/bishwamittra/formallm.

</details>


### [17] [From Similarity to Structure: Training-free LLM Context Compression with Hybrid Graph Priors](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23277)
*Yitian Zhou,Chaoning Zhang,Jiaquan Zhang,Zhenzhen Huang,Jinyu Guo,Sung-Ho Bae,Lik-Hang Lee,Caiyan Qin,Yang Yang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces a training-free, model-agnostic method to compress long contexts for large language models by selecting a compact, coherent subset of sentences using a graph-based framework.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context large language models are costly and unreliable on very long inputs, so systems often need to compress context. Existing methods either require training, rely on dense retrieval or simple heuristics, and struggle to jointly maintain task relevance, broad topic coverage, and coherent text under tight token budgets.

Method: The authors build a sparse hybrid graph over sentences, combining semantic mutual k-NN edges with local sequential edges. They cluster this graph to obtain a topic skeleton, then compute an interpretable importance score for each sentence that blends task relevance, how representative it is of its cluster, its bridge centrality between topics, and a signal related to cycle coverage in the graph. A budgeted greedy algorithm with redundancy suppression selects sentences according to this score and outputs them in original order to form a compressed context.

Result: On four datasets, the proposed method performs competitively with strong extractive and abstractive baselines, with particularly strong improvements on long-document tasks, indicating it effectively compresses context while preserving important information.

Conclusion: A structural, graph-based, training-free compression framework can effectively produce compact, coherent, and task-relevant contexts for large language models, matching or outperforming learned and heuristic baselines, especially for very long documents.

Abstract: Long-context large language models remain computationally expensive to run and often fail to reliably process very long inputs, which makes context compression an important component of many systems. Existing compression approaches typically rely on trained compressors, dense retrieval-style selection, or heuristic trimming, and they often struggle to jointly preserve task relevance, topic coverage, and cross-sentence coherence under a strict token budget. To address this, we propose a training-free and model-agnostic compression framework that selects a compact set of sentences guided by structural graph priors. Our method constructs a sparse hybrid sentence graph that combines mutual k-NN semantic edges with short-range sequential edges, extracts a topic skeleton via clustering, and ranks sentences using an interpretable score that integrates task relevance, cluster representativeness, bridge centrality, and a cycle coverage cue. A budgeted greedy selection with redundancy suppression then produces a readable compressed context in original order. Experimental results on four datasets show that our approach is competitive with strong extractive and abstractive baselines, demonstrating larger gains on long-document benchmarks.

We propose a two-stage training recipe -- large-scale pre-training followed by fine-tuning on 1,000 hours of conversational data. ### [20] [$\mathcal{S}^2$IT: Stepwise Syntax Integration Tuning for Large Language Models in Aspect Sentiment Quad Prediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23296)
*Bingfeng Chen,Chenjie Qiu,Yifeng Xie,Boyan Xu,Ruichu Cai,Zhifeng Hao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces S^2IT, a multi-step tuning framework that injects syntactic structure knowledge into large language models to improve Aspect Sentiment Quad Prediction, achieving state-of-the-art results.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing Aspect Sentiment Quad Prediction methods with large language models underuse syntactic structure information, despite prior evidence from extractive approaches that syntax is beneficial. The authors aim to bridge this gap by effectively integrating global and local syntactic cues into the generative paradigm of LLMs, overcoming their limited reasoning over structure.

Method: The authors propose S^2IT, a Stepwise Syntax Integration Tuning framework with a three-step training pipeline. First, they decompose the ASQP task into two stages: (1) Global Syntax-guided Extraction, where global syntactic structure guides the extraction of aspect-related elements, and (2) Local Syntax-guided Classification, where local syntactic cues aid in classifying extracted elements. Finally, they introduce Fine-grained Structural Tuning, which further aligns the model with syntactic structures through element-link prediction and node classification tasks, progressively injecting syntactic knowledge into an LLM.

Result: Experiments on multiple ASQP benchmark datasets show that S^2IT considerably boosts performance compared to previous state-of-the-art approaches. The method yields consistent improvements, indicating that stepwise integration of syntax is effective for generative LLM-based ASQP.

Conclusion: The study concludes that systematically integrating syntactic structure information into LLMs via a stepwise tuning process significantly enhances their capability for Aspect Sentiment Quad Prediction. The combination of global and local syntax guidance, plus fine-grained structural objectives, allows LLMs to better capture complex sentiment structures, setting a new state of the art and motivating further research on structure-aware tuning for generative models.

Abstract: Aspect Sentiment Quad Prediction (ASQP) has seen significant advancements, largely driven by the powerful semantic understanding and generative capabilities of large language models (LLMs). However, while syntactic structure information has been proven effective in previous extractive paradigms, it remains underutilized in the generative paradigm of LLMs due to their limited reasoning capabilities. In this paper, we propose S^2IT, a novel Stepwise Syntax Integration Tuning framework that progressively integrates syntactic structure knowledge into LLMs through a multi-step tuning process. The training process is divided into three steps. S^2IT decomposes the quadruple generation task into two stages: 1) Global Syntax-guided Extraction and 2) Local Syntax-guided Classification, integrating both global and local syntactic structure information. Finally, Fine-grained Structural Tuning enhances the model's understanding of syntactic structures through the prediction of element links and node classification. Experiments demonstrate that S^2IT significantly improves state-of-the-art performance across multiple datasets. Our implementation will be open-sourced at https://github.com/DMIRLAB-Group/S2IT.

</details>


### [21] [Hidden States Know Where Reasoning Diverges: Credit Assignment via Span-Level Wasserstein Distance](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23318)
*Xinzhu Chen,Wei He,Huichuan Fan,Wenzhe Niu,Zhongxiang Sun,Xuanru Wang,Jiuchong Gao,Jinghua Hao,Renqing He,Weijie Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose SHEAR, a method that uses differences in hidden-state distributions between correct and incorrect rollouts to provide fine-grained credit assignment in RL with verifiable rewards, improving reasoning and code generation without extra annotations or reward models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In RL with verifiable rewards like GRPO, all tokens in a rollout share the same advantage, leading to coarse credit assignment: good and bad steps are treated equally if the final outcome is correct or incorrect. Process reward models can fix this but are costly, needing step-level labels or extra reward modeling. The authors seek a way to get fine-grained supervision using only outcome-level correctness labels, without extra models or annotations.

Method: They empirically study hidden-state distributions of language models across GRPO groups of correct vs. incorrect rollouts, measuring span-level Wasserstein distances between these distributions. They observe that divergence in reasoning quality corresponds to increased Wasserstein distance in certain spans. They formalize this via a separation theorem: under mild assumptions, spans after a divergence in reasoning quality exhibit larger Wasserstein distances than earlier spans when population-level gaps exceed noise. Based on this, they introduce SHEAR, which modifies GRPO by computing span-level Wasserstein distances between correct and incorrect rollouts and using these distances to reweight token-level advantages, amplifying updates where hidden states are more separated. The approach does not introduce new models and minimally alters the training loop.

Result: On five mathematical reasoning benchmarks and five code generation benchmarks, SHEAR outperforms standard GRPO and approaches or matches the performance of systems that use supervised process reward models, despite not requiring any step-level labels or additional reward model training.

Conclusion: Hidden-state distributional divergence, measured via Wasserstein distance between correct and incorrect rollouts, provides a viable self-supervision signal for fine-grained credit assignment in RLVR. SHEAR operationalizes this insight, enhancing GRPO with span-level advantage reweighting that improves reasoning and coding performance while remaining annotation- and model-efficient.

Abstract: Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO) performs coarse-grained credit assignment in reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) by assigning the same advantage to all tokens in a rollout. Process reward models can provide finer-grained supervision, but they require step-level annotation or additional reward modeling. We show that hidden-state distributions contain a useful signal for local reasoning quality that can be extracted using only outcome-level correctness labels available in RLVR. Specifically, within each GRPO group, the Wasserstein distance between span-level hidden state distributions of correct and incorrect rollouts increases around regions where their local reasoning quality diverges. This association holds both across examples and within individual trajectories, suggesting that hidden-state distributional divergence can serve as a self-supervision signal for fine-grained credit assignment. We formalize this observation with a separation theorem showing that, under mild structural assumptions, post-divergence spans have larger Wasserstein distances than pre-divergence spans whenever the population-level distributional gap exceeds finite-sample noise. Motivated by this result, we propose \textbf{S}pan-level \textbf{H}idden state \textbf{E}nabled \textbf{A}dvantage \textbf{R}eweighting (SHEAR), which modifies GRPO by using span-level Wasserstein distances to scale token-level advantages, amplifying updates on tokens whose hidden states are more separated from the opposing group. The method requires no additional model and only minimal changes to the training pipeline. Experiments on five mathematical reasoning benchmarks and five code generation benchmarks show improvements over standard GRPO and strong performance relative to supervised process reward models, while requiring no additional annotation or reward model training.

However, current state-of-the-art approaches struggle with long, noisy, and weakly labeled audio due to their reliance on contrastive learning and large-batch training. We propose a novel multimodal retrieval framework that refines audio and text embeddings using a cross-modal embedding refinement module combining transformer-based projection, linear mapping, and bidirectional attention. To further improve robustness, we introduce a hybrid loss function blending cosine similarity, $\mathcal{L}_{1}$, and contrastive objectives, enabling stable training even under small-batch constraints. Our approach efficiently handles long-form and noisy audio (SNR 5 to 15) via silence-aware chunking and attention-based pooling. Experiments on benchmark datasets demonstrate improvements over prior methods.

Result: Across several benchmarks for task-oriented dialogue, VLK-RL yields better generalization and robustness compared to strong single-model baselines, especially on long-horizon tasks. The framework shows that verified LLM-derived constraints can significantly enhance RL policy performance.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that carefully verified and structured use of LLM-derived constraint reasoning can make RL more effective for complex, long-horizon dialogue tasks. Abstract: Cross-domain task-oriented dialogue requires reasoning over implicit and explicit feasibility constraints while planning long-horizon, multi-turn actions. Large language models (LLMs) can infer such constraints but are unreliable over long horizons, while Reinforcement learning (RL) optimizes long-horizon behavior yet cannot recover constraints from raw dialogue. Naively coupling LLMs with RL is therefore brittle: unverified or unstructured LLM outputs can corrupt state representations and misguide policy learning. Motivated by this, we propose Verified LLM-Knowledge empowered RL (VLK-RL), a hybrid framework that makes LLM-derived constraint reasoning usable for RL. VLK-RL first elicits candidate constraints with an LLM and then verifies them via a dual-role cross-examination procedure to suppress hallucinations and cross-turn inconsistencies. The verified constraints are mapped into ontology-aligned slot-value representations, yielding a structured, constraint-aware state for RL policy optimization. Experiments across multiple benchmarks demonstrate that VLK-RL significantly improves generalization and robustness, outperforming strong single-model baselines on long-horizon tasks.

First, developers often lack sufficient medical domain expertise to interpret model errors in clinically meaningful terms. Second, models can fail across a large and diverse set of instances involving different input types, tasks, and reasoning steps, making it challenging for developers to prioritize which errors deserve focused inspection. Third, developers struggle to identify recurring error patterns across cases, as existing debugging practices are largely instance-centric and rely on manual inspection of isolated failures. To address these challenges, we present VeriLLMed, a visual analytics system that integrates external biomedical knowledge to audit and debug medical LLM diagnostic reasoning. ### [26] [Overcoming Copyright Barriers in Corpus Distribution Through Non-Reversible Hashing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23412)
*Arthur Amalvy,Vincent Labatut,Xavier Bost,Hen-Hsen Huang*

Main category: cs.CL

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Annotated corpora are essential for NLP, but copyright prevents freely sharing many real-world texts. Researchers need ways to collaborate on such data without illegally distributing copyrighted material, while still capturing real-world diversity in language.

Method: The corpus creator releases two things: (1) annotations in clear text and (2) a non-reversible hashed representation of the source tokens. Users who legally own the copyrighted texts run the same tokenization and hashing on their local copies, then align their hashes with the shared ones to obtain the annotations. The method is designed to tolerate reasonable differences between editions (e.g., small textual variations).

Result: Through alignment experiments on several editions of novels, the method achieves 98.7–99.79% correct token alignment across novels, assuming the user’s version is sufficiently similar to the creator’s version.

Conclusion: It is feasible to lawfully share rich NLP annotations for copyrighted literary texts without distributing the texts themselves by using hash-based token alignment, and the authors provide a public Python implementation, novelshare, to facilitate adoption.

Abstract: While annotated corpora are crucial in the field of natural language processing (NLP), those containing copyrighted material are difficult to exchange among researchers. Yet, such corpora are necessary to fully represent the diversity of data found in the wild in the context of NLP tasks. We tackle this issue by proposing a method to lawfully and publicly share the annotations of copyrighted literary texts. The corpus creator shares the annotations in clear, along with a non-reversible hashed version of the source material. The corpus user must own the source material, and apply the same hash function to their own tokens, in order to match them to the shared annotations. Crucially, our method is robust to reasonable divergences in the version of the copyrighted data owned by the user. As an illustration, we present alignment experiments on different editions of novels. Our results show that our method is able to correctly align 98.7 to 99.79% of tokens depending on the novel, provided the user version is sufficiently close to the corpus creator's version. We publicly release novelshare, a Python implementation of our method.
