### [27] [Beyond Local vs. External: A Game-Theoretic Framework for Trustworthy Knowledge Acquisition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23413)
*Rujing Yao,Yufei Shi,Yang Wu,Ang Li,Zhuoren Jiang,XiaoFeng Wang,Haixu Tang,Xiaozhong Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: GTKA is a game-theoretic framework that splits sensitive user queries into low-risk sub-queries for cloud LLMs, training a generator and attacker adversarially to balance privacy and answer quality.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Cloud LLMs provide strong reasoning and up-to-date knowledge but require sending them raw user queries, which can reveal sensitive intent. Purely local models protect privacy but lack scale and coverage, especially in sensitive domains like biomedicine and law. There is a need for a principled way to trade off knowledge utility from powerful external models against the privacy risk of intent leakage, instead of using ad-hoc redaction or full local processing.

Method: The paper formulates the interaction between a query-decomposing mechanism and a potential attacker as a strategic game. It introduces GTKA with three main components: (1) a privacy-aware sub-query generator that decomposes a sensitive user query into generalized, lower-risk fragments to be sent to external LLMs; (2) an adversarial reconstruction attacker that, given these fragments, attempts to infer the original sensitive intent, thereby estimating leakage; and (3) a trusted local integrator that combines the returned answers inside a secure environment. The generator and attacker are trained in an alternating adversarial fashion, similar to GANs, where the generator learns a policy that maximizes task performance (knowledge acquisition accuracy) while minimizing the attacker’s ability to reconstruct the original intent.

Result: On two constructed sensitive-domain benchmarks in biomedical and legal settings, GTKA is evaluated against state-of-the-art privacy-preserving and query-transformation baselines. The experiments show that GTKA significantly lowers the amount of recoverable user intent (i.e., reduces intent leakage) while preserving or closely matching the answer quality of systems that directly use cloud LLMs without such protections.

Conclusion: Modeling privacy–utility trade-offs as a game between a sub-query generator and a reconstruction attacker enables principled optimization of query decomposition strategies. GTKA demonstrates that it is possible to substantially mitigate sensitive intent leakage to cloud-hosted LLMs without incurring a large degradation in answer quality, especially in high-stakes domains like biomedicine and law. This suggests a promising direction for practical, privacy-aware use of external LLM services.

Abstract: Cloud-hosted Large Language Models (LLMs) offer unmatched reasoning capabilities and dynamic knowledge, yet submitting raw queries to these external services risks exposing sensitive user intent. Conversely, relying exclusively on trusted local models preserves privacy but often compromises answer quality due to limited parameter scale and knowledge. To resolve this dilemma, we propose Game-theoretic Trustworthy Knowledge Acquisition (GTKA), a framework that formulates the trade-off between knowledge utility and privacy as a strategic game. GTKA consists of three components: (i) a privacy-aware sub-query generator that decomposes sensitive intent into generalized, low-risk fragments; (ii) an adversarial reconstruction attacker that attempts to infer the original query from these fragments, providing adaptive leakage signals; and (iii) a trusted local integrator that synthesizes external responses within a secure boundary. By training the generator and attacker in an alternating adversarial manner, GTKA optimizes the sub-query generation policy to maximize knowledge acquisition accuracy while minimizing the reconstructability of the original sensitive intent. To validate our approach, we construct two sensitive-domain benchmarks in the biomedical and legal fields. Extensive experiments demonstrate that GTKA significantly reduces intent leakage compared to state-of-the-art baselines while maintaining high-fidelity answer quality.

Result: The experiments consistently show that greedy decoding outperforms standard stochastic sampling methods on multiple VQA benchmarks, supporting the theoretical argument. The proposed Greedy Decoding for Reasoning Models further improves performance over both conventional greedy decoding and stochastic approaches on multimodal reasoning tasks, indicating that careful deterministic decoding can leverage reasoning models more effectively.

Conclusion: Stochastic sampling, inherited from text-only LLM practices, is not necessarily suitable for VQA with multimodal LLMs. For closed-ended, head-heavy answer distributions where uncertainty is mainly epistemic, greedy decoding is often a better choice. The paper recommends greedy decoding as a strong and efficient default for VQA and shows that specialized deterministic schemes for reasoning models can surpass both standard greedy and stochastic decoding, urging the community to reconsider default decoding heuristics in MLLMs.

Abstract: Stochastic sampling strategies are widely adopted in large language models (LLMs) to balance output coherence and diversity. These heuristics are often inherited in Multimodal LLMs (MLLMs) without task-specific justification. However, we contend that stochastic decoding can be suboptimal for Visual Question Answering (VQA). VQA is a closed-ended task with head-heavy answer distributions where uncertainty is usually epistemic, arising from missing or ambiguous visual evidence rather than plausible continuations. In this work, we provide a theoretical formalization of the relationship between model calibration and predictive accuracy, and derive the sufficient conditions for greedy decoding optimality. Extensive experiments provide empirical evidence for the superiority of greedy decoding over stochastic sampling across multiple benchmarks. Furthermore, we propose Greedy Decoding for Reasoning Models, which outperforms both stochastic sampling and standard greedy decoding in multimodal reasoning scenarios. Overall, our results caution against naively inheriting LLMs decoding heuristics in MLLMs and demonstrate that greedy decoding can be an efficient yet strong default for VQA.

Abstract: Educational NLP systems are typically evaluated using engagement metrics and satisfaction surveys, which are at best a proxy for meeting pedagogical goals. We introduce six computational metrics for automated evaluation of pedagogical alignment in student-AI dialogue. We validate our metrics through analysis of 12,650 messages across 500 conversations from four courses. Using our metrics, we identify a fundamental misalignment: educators design conversational tutors for sustained learning dialogue, but students mainly use them for answer-extraction. Deployment context is the strongest predictor of usage patterns, outweighing student preference or system design: when AI tools are optional, usage concentrates around deadlines; when integrated into course structure, students ask for solutions to verbatim assignment questions. Whole-dialogue evaluation misses these turn-by-turn patterns. Our metrics will enable researchers building educational dialogue systems to measure whether they are achieving their pedagogical goals.

### [31] [K-SENSE: A Knowledge-Guided Self-Augmented Encoder for Neuro-Semantic Evaluation of Mental Health Conditions on Social Media](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23493)
*Vijay Yadav*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces K-SENSE, a knowledge-guided, contrastive-learning framework to better detect stress and depression from noisy social media text.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Detecting mental health conditions like stress and depression from social media is difficult due to figurative language, implicit emotions, and noisy user-generated text. Existing methods either use external commonsense knowledge or self-augmentation/contrastive learning, but rarely integrate both systematically. The paper aims to create a unified model that leverages psychological commonsense while making text representations robust and discriminative.

Method: K-SENSE uses a three-stage encoding pipeline. (1) It extracts inferential commonsense knowledge along five mental state dimensions from the COMET commonsense reasoning model for each input. (2) It builds a semantic anchor by encoding text with two parallel streams, then projecting their hidden representations into a shared space and fusing them. (3) It trains with a supervised contrastive learning objective that pulls together representations of the same mental health class and pushes apart different classes, while guiding attention to downweight noisy or irrelevant knowledge. The knowledge encoder is kept frozen and a temporal integration strategy is applied to incorporate knowledge over the sequence.

Result: On two datasets—Dreaddit for stress detection and Depression_Mixed for depression detection—K-SENSE achieves mean F1 scores of 86.1 and 94.3 across five runs, improving over the strongest prior baselines by about 2.6 and 1.5 F1 points, respectively. Ablation studies show that removing any key component (knowledge integration, semantic anchor, contrastive loss, temporal strategy, or frozen-encoder setup) degrades performance, validating each part’s utility.

Conclusion: Integrating external psychological commonsense knowledge with robust, contrastive representation learning in a single framework significantly improves automatic detection of stress and depression from social media text. The design choices of temporally-aware knowledge integration and freezing the knowledge encoder are empirically important, suggesting a general recipe for combining knowledge models with downstream mental health NLP tasks.

Abstract: Early detection of mental health conditions, particularly stress and depression, from social media text remains a challenging open problem in computational psychiatry and natural language processing. Automated systems must contend with figurative language, implicit emotional expression, and the high noise inherent in user-generated content. Existing approaches either leverage external commonsense knowledge to model mental states explicitly, or apply self-augmentation and contrastive training to improve generalization, but seldom do both in a principled, unified framework. We propose K-SENSE (Knowledge-guided Self-augmented Encoder for Neuro-Semantic Evaluation of Mental Health), a framework that jointly exploits external psychological reasoning and internal representation robustness. K-SENSE adopts a three-stage encoding pipeline: (1) inferential commonsense knowledge is extracted from the COMET model across five mental state dimensions; (2) a semantic anchor is constructed by combining hidden representations from two parallel encoding streams, projected into a shared space before fusion; and (3) a supervised contrastive learning objective aligns same-class representations while encouraging the attention mechanism to suppress irrelevant knowledge noise. We evaluate K-SENSE on Dreaddit (stress detection) and Depression_Mixed (depression detection), achieving mean F1-scores of 86.1 (0.6%) and 94.3 (0.8%), respectively, over five independent runs. These represent improvements of approximately 2.6 and 1.5 percentage points over the strongest prior baselines. Ablation experiments confirm the contribution of each architectural component, including the temporal knowledge integration strategy and the choice to keep the knowledge encoder frozen during fine-tuning.

</details>

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: MTRouter encodes both dialogue history and candidate models into joint embeddings and learns, from logged trajectories, an estimator that predicts each model’s utility at every turn, then uses these predictions to choose which model to invoke under a cost budget.

Here, we study cost-aware multi-turn LLM routing: selecting which model to invoke at each turn from a model pool, given a fixed cost budget. We propose MTRouter, which encodes the interaction history and candidate models into joint history-model embeddings, and learns an outcome estimator from logged trajectories to predict turn-level model utility. Experiments show that MTRouter improves the performance-cost trade-off: on ScienceWorld, it surpasses GPT-5 while reducing total cost by 58.7%; on Humanity's Last Exam (HLE), it achieves competitive accuracy while reducing total cost by 43.4% relative to GPT-5, and these gains even carry over to held-out tasks. Further analyses reveal several mechanisms underlying its effectiveness: relative to prior multi-turn routers, MTRouter makes fewer model switches, is more tolerant to transient errors, and exhibits emergent specialization across models.   <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing test-time alignment methods like RE-Control use an external value function trained on hidden states to guide LLM outputs, but they do not explicitly model how alignment data are collected—namely as human preferences between candidate responses. Method: The authors propose Pref-CTRL, a preference-based test-time control framework. Instead of a single scalar value function, they train a multi-objective value function on human preference data defined over LLM hidden representations. This value function is then used at inference time to perform gradient-based editing of internal states, steering generation toward responses that are preferred according to human preference signals while respecting multiple alignment objectives.

Result: On two standard alignment benchmarks, Pref-CTRL surpasses the performance of RE-Control, a strong prior test-time control method. It also demonstrates stronger robustness and generalization when evaluated on out-of-domain datasets, indicating that modeling the preference structure improves transfer beyond the training distribution.

Conclusion: Incorporating the structure of human preference data into the value function used for test-time control leads to more effective LLM alignment than previous scalar-value approaches like RE-Control. Pref-CTRL’s multi-objective preference-based value function enables better in-domain performance and improved out-of-domain generalization, making it a promising alternative to full model fine-tuning for alignment.

Abstract: Test-time alignment methods offer a promising alternative to fine-tuning by steering the outputs of large language models (LLMs) at inference time with lightweight interventions on their internal representations. Recently, a prominent and effective approach, RE-Control (Kong et al., 2024), has proposed leveraging an external value function trained over the LLM's hidden states to guide generation via gradient-based editing. While effective, this method overlooks a key characteristic of alignment tasks, i.e. that they are typically formulated as learning from human preferences between candidate responses. To address this, in this paper we propose a novel preference-based training framework, Pref-CTRL, that uses a multi-objective value function to better reflect the structure of preference data. Our approach has outperformed RE-Control on two benchmark datasets and showed greater generalization on out-of-domain datasets. Our source code is available at https://github.com/UTS-nlPUG/pref-ctrl.

### [34] [RouteNLP: Closed-Loop LLM Routing with Conformal Cascading and Distillation Co-Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23577)
*Dongxin Guo,Jikun Wu,Siu Ming Yiu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: RouteNLP is a closed-loop system that routes queries across multiple LLMs to cut cost and latency while meeting per-task quality guarantees.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Enterprises running diverse NLP workloads with large LLMs face very high inference costs and latency, even though most queries are easy and can be served by smaller, cheaper models without quality loss. There is a need for a principled way to decide, per query and per task, which model to use so that cost is minimized while meeting explicit quality requirements.

Method: RouteNLP introduces a tiered model portfolio and a closed-loop routing framework with three main components: (1) a difficulty-aware router that learns shared task-conditioned representations from preference data and quality signals to predict which model tier is sufficient; (2) confidence-calibrated cascading using conformal prediction to set quality/uncertainty thresholds in a distribution-free way so the system knows when to escalate to a stronger model; and (3) a distillation–routing co-optimization loop that clusters failures where queries had to escalate, performs targeted knowledge distillation from strong to cheaper models on those hard clusters, and then automatically retrains the router so that more future queries can be safely routed to cheaper models.

Result: In an 8‑week production pilot handling about 5K queries per day in an enterprise customer-service setting, RouteNLP cut LLM inference cost by 58%, maintained 91% response acceptance, and reduced p99 latency from 1,847 ms to 387 ms. On a controlled six-task benchmark across finance, customer service, and legal domains, it achieved 40–85% cost savings while preserving 96–100% quality on structured tasks and 96–98% on generation tasks, with human raters judging that 74.5% of responses routed to cheaper models matched or exceeded the quality of a frontier model.

Conclusion: A closed-loop, difficulty-aware routing and targeted distillation framework can substantially reduce the cost and latency of serving enterprise NLP workloads while maintaining high task-specific quality. By using calibrated cascading and iteratively improving cheaper models on real escalation failures, RouteNLP outperforms naive or untargeted distillation strategies in both economic efficiency and end-to-end user-perceived quality.

Abstract: Serving diverse NLP workloads with large language models is costly: at one enterprise partner, inference costs exceeded $200K/month despite over 70% of queries being routine tasks well within the capability of smaller models. We present RouteNLP, a closed-loop framework that routes queries across a tiered model portfolio to minimize cost while satisfying per-task quality constraints. The framework integrates three components: a difficulty-aware router with shared task-conditioned representations trained on preference data and quality signals; confidence-calibrated cascading that uses conformal prediction for distribution-free threshold initialization; and a distillation-routing co-optimization loop that clusters escalation failures, applies targeted knowledge distillation to cheaper models, and automatically retrains the router, yielding over twice the cost improvement of untargeted distillation. In an 8-week pilot deployment processing ~5K queries/day at an enterprise customer-service division, RouteNLP reduced inference costs by 58% while maintaining 91% response acceptance and reducing p99 latency from 1,847 ms to 387 ms. On a six-task benchmark spanning finance, customer service, and legal domains, the framework achieves 40-85% cost reduction while retaining 96-100% quality on structured tasks and 96-98% on generation tasks, with human evaluation confirming that 74.5% of routed generation outputs match or exceed frontier-model quality.

### [36] [ComplianceNLP: Knowledge-Graph-Augmented RAG for Multi-Framework Regulatory Gap Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23585)
*Dongxin Guo,Jikun Wu,Siu Ming Yiu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: ComplianceNLP is an end-to-end NLP system that monitors regulatory changes, extracts machine-readable obligations, and detects gaps between regulations and a bank’s internal policies, achieving strong accuracy and large efficiency gains over manual review and GPT-4o+RAG baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Financial institutions face an unmanageable volume of regulatory changes—over 60,000 events per year—and have incurred over USD 300B in fines since 2008, indicating that manual compliance processes are both costly and ineffective. There is a need for automated, reliable, and explainable systems that can continuously track new regulations, interpret them as concrete obligations, and compare them against existing internal policies to prevent non-compliance. Existing generic LLM+RAG setups are not optimized for regulatory structure, cross-references, and institutional integration, motivating a specialized system.

Method: The paper proposes ComplianceNLP, which comprises three main components integrated into an end-to-end pipeline. (1) A knowledge-graph-augmented RAG system grounds model generations in a curated regulatory knowledge graph consisting of 12,847 provisions from major frameworks (SEC, MiFID II, Basel III), with KG-based re-ranking to prioritize structurally relevant passages and cross-references. (2) A multi-task obligation extraction module based on a shared LEGAL-BERT encoder performs named-entity recognition, deontic (obligation vs. permission, etc.) classification, and cross-reference resolution jointly, producing structured obligation representations. (3) A compliance gap analysis component maps extracted obligations to an institution’s internal policies and scores potential gaps by severity to prioritize remediation. The authors further apply domain-specific knowledge distillation from a 70B to an 8B model plus Medusa speculative decoding to accelerate inference, leveraging the relatively low-entropy nature of regulatory text for high draft-token acceptance.

Result: On a dedicated benchmark, ComplianceNLP achieves 87.7 F1 on detecting compliance gaps, outperforming a GPT-4o+RAG baseline by 3.5 F1 points. It attains 94.2% grounding accuracy with a correlation of r=0.83 against human judgments and 83.4 F1 in an end-to-end setting that includes upstream extraction errors. Ablation studies show that knowledge-graph-based re-ranking provides the largest single improvement (+4.6 F1), highlighting the value of explicit structural knowledge for handling cross-references. Efficiency experiments demonstrate that combining domain-specific knowledge distillation (from 70B to 8B) with Medusa speculative decoding yields a 2.8x speedup, with regulatory text exhibiting low entropy (H=2.31 bits vs. 3.87 for general text) and enabling a 91.3% draft-token acceptance rate. In a four-month parallel-run deployment on 9,847 regulatory updates at a financial institution, the system achieved an estimated 96.0% recall and 90.7% precision, and delivered a sustained 3.1x improvement in analyst productivity.

Conclusion: ComplianceNLP shows that a domain-specialized, knowledge-graph-augmented NLP system can reliably track regulatory changes, extract obligations, and detect compliance gaps at scale, outperforming general-purpose LLM+RAG approaches while significantly improving analyst efficiency. Structural regulatory knowledge and multi-task obligation modeling are key to handling cross-reference-heavy legal texts, and domain-specific model compression plus speculative decoding can make such systems operationally efficient. Real-world deployment results suggest that the approach is viable for integration into governance, risk, and compliance (GRC) workflows, provided that practitioners carefully manage trust calibration, integration with existing systems, and monitoring for distributional shifts in incoming regulatory content.

Abstract: Financial institutions must track over 60,000 regulatory events annually, overwhelming manual compliance teams; the industry has paid over USD 300 billion in fines and settlements since the 2008 financial crisis. We present ComplianceNLP, an end-to-end system that automatically monitors regulatory changes, extracts structured obligations, and identifies compliance gaps against institutional policies. The system integrates three components: (1) a knowledge-graph-augmented RAG pipeline grounding generations in a regulatory knowledge graph of 12,847 provisions across SEC, MiFID II, and Basel III; (2) multi-task obligation extraction combining NER, deontic classification, and cross-reference resolution over a shared LEGAL-BERT encoder; and (3) compliance gap analysis that maps obligations to internal policies with severity-aware scoring. On our benchmark, ComplianceNLP achieves 87.7 F1 on gap detection, outperforming GPT-4o+RAG by +3.5 F1, with 94.2% grounding accuracy ($r=0.83$ vs. human judgments) and 83.4 F1 under realistic end-to-end error propagation. Ablations show that knowledge-graph re-ranking contributes the largest marginal gain (+4.6 F1), confirming that structural regulatory knowledge is critical for cross-reference-heavy tasks. Domain-specific knowledge distillation (70B $\to$ 8B) combined with Medusa speculative decoding yields $2.8\times$ inference speedup; regulatory text's low entropy ($H=2.31$ bits vs. $3.87$ general text) produces 91.3% draft-token acceptance rates. In four months of parallel-run deployment processing 9,847 updates at a financial institution, the system achieved 96.0% estimated recall and 90.7% precision, with a $3.1\times$ sustained analyst efficiency gain. We report deployment lessons on trust calibration, GRC integration, and distributional shift monitoring for regulated-domain NLP.

### [37] [XITE: Cross-lingual Interpolation for Transfer using Embeddings](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23589)
*Barah Fazili,Preethi Jyothi*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces XITE, an embedding-based cross-lingual data augmentation method that greatly improves multilingual model performance on low-resource languages while preserving performance on high-resource ones.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Cross-lingual transfer in multilingual language models is still difficult, especially for low-resource languages where labeled data is scarce. Existing methods often fail to leverage unlabeled target-language text effectively or suffer from catastrophic forgetting on high-resource languages during adaptation. The paper aims to design a simple, effective augmentation approach that uses unlabeled target-language data to improve transfer without degrading performance on the source language.

Method: The method, XITE, uses unlabeled text from a low-resource target language and an English task-specific labeled corpus. For each unlabeled target sentence, it finds the most similar English sentence in the embedding space (computed by a multilingual model such as XLM-R) and assigns the English label to the target sentence. Then it interpolates the source (English) and target sentence embeddings to generate synthetic training examples for fine-tuning. Additionally, it projects target-language embeddings into a language-rich subspace using linear discriminant analysis (LDA) before interpolation, which further enhances the injected cross-lingual signal. The final fine-tuning uses both original labeled English data and these synthetic interpolated embeddings.

Result: Using XLM-R as the base model, XITE yields large gains in cross-lingual transfer performance for several low-resource target languages (Korean, Arabic, Urdu, Hindi). Reported improvements reach up to 35.91% for sentiment analysis and up to 81.16% for natural language inference compared to baselines without this augmentation. The method also maintains or improves performance on the high-resource source language, indicating that it mitigates catastrophic forgetting during adaptation.

Conclusion: Embedding-based cross-lingual data augmentation via interpolation between source and target sentence embeddings, guided by nearest-neighbor label transfer and enhanced by LDA projection, is an effective and simple strategy for improving cross-lingual transfer in multilingual models. XITE significantly boosts performance on low-resource languages while preserving source-language accuracy, demonstrating a promising direction for leveraging unlabeled target-language data in multilingual NLP.

Abstract: Facilitating cross-lingual transfer in multilingual language models remains a critical challenge. Towards this goal, we propose an embedding-based data augmentation technique called XITE. We start with unlabeled text from a low-resource target language, identify an English counterpart in a task-specific training corpus using embedding-based similarities and adopt its label. Next, we perform a simple interpolation of the source and target embeddings to create synthetic data for task-specific fine-tuning. Projecting the target text into a language-rich subspace using linear discriminant analysis (LDA), prior to interpolation, further boosts performance. Our cross-lingual embedding-based augmentation technique XITE yields significant improvements of up to 35.91% for sentiment analysis and up to 81.16% for natural language inference, using XLM-R, for a diverse set of target languages including Korean, Arabic, Urdu and Hindi. Apart from boosting cross-lingual transfer, adaptation using XITE also safeguards against forgetting and maintains task performance on the high-resource language.

In particular, Dark Triad personality traits are consistently associated with higher gender-stereotypical representations compared to socially desirable HEXACO traits, though these associations vary across models and languages. Our findings demonstrate that gender bias in LLMs is not static but context-dependent. This suggests that persona-conditioned systems used in real-world applications may introduce uneven representational harms, reinforcing gender stereotypes in generated educational, professional, or social content.

### [40] [GraphPlanner: Graph Memory-Augmented Agentic Routing for Multi-Agent LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23626)
*Tao Feng,Haozhen Zhang,Zijie Lei,Peixuan Han,Jiaxuan You*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: GraphPlanner is a reinforcement-learning-based agentic routing framework that uses a heterogeneous graph memory to plan multi-agent LLM workflows, improving accuracy and GPU efficiency while generalizing to new tasks and models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM routing methods mainly operate in simple, single-round settings and do not handle more realistic agentic scenarios that require task planning, multi-round cooperation among heterogeneous agents, and the use of historical interaction memory. There is a gap in routing methods that can dynamically plan complex workflows across multiple LLMs and agent roles while efficiently leveraging past experiences.

Method: The authors introduce GraphPlanner, which frames routing workflow generation as a Markov Decision Process. At each decision step, it chooses both which LLM backbone to use and which agent role (Planner, Executor, Summarizer) to activate. They construct a heterogeneous graph, GARNet, capturing interactions among queries, agent roles, and responses as memory. This graph provides state representations that encode both historical and current workflow information. The system is trained end-to-end with reinforcement learning to jointly optimize task performance and computational efficiency, and it supports both inductive (generalizing to new nodes/tasks) and transductive (exploiting known graph structure) inference.

Result: Across 14 diverse LLM benchmarks, GraphPlanner surpasses strong baselines in both single-round and multi-round routing. It improves task accuracy by up to 9.3% while dramatically lowering GPU memory consumption (from 186.26 GiB to 1.04 GiB) compared to prior approaches. It also shows strong zero-shot generalization to unseen tasks and LLM backbones and demonstrates effective use of historical memories via its graph-based representation in both inductive and transductive regimes.

Conclusion: GraphPlanner successfully extends LLM routing into agentic, multi-agent settings by combining MDP-based workflow planning with heterogeneous graph memory. This design yields better accuracy, much higher GPU efficiency, and strong generalization to new tasks and models while leveraging historical interaction data for more adaptive routing. The work suggests that graph-augmented memory and reinforcement learning are a promising direction for scalable, agentic LLM routing systems.

Abstract: LLM routing has achieved promising results in integrating the strengths of diverse models while balancing efficiency and performance. However, to support more realistic and challenging applications, routing must extend into agentic LLM settings, where task planning, multi-round cooperation among heterogeneous agents, and memory utilization are indispensable. To address this gap, we propose GraphPlanner, a heterogeneous graph memory-augmented agentic router for multi-agent LLMs that generates routing workflows for each query and supports both inductive and transductive inference. GraphPlanner formulates workflow generation as a Markov Decision Process (MDP), where at each step it selects both the LLM backbone and the agent role, including Planner, Executor, and Summarizer. By leveraging a heterogeneous graph, denoted as GARNet, to capture interaction memories among queries, agents, and responses, GraphPlanner integrates historical memory and workflow memory into richer state representations. The entire pipeline is optimized with reinforcement learning, jointly improving task-specific performance and computational efficiency. We evaluate GraphPlanner across 14 diverse LLM tasks and demonstrate that: (1) GraphPlanner outperforms strong single-round and multi-round routers, improving accuracy by up to 9.3% while reducing GPU cost from 186.26 GiB to 1.04 GiB; (2) GraphPlanner generalizes robustly to unseen tasks and LLMs, exhibiting strong zero-shot capabilities; and (3) GraphPlanner effectively leverages historical memories, supporting both inductive and transductive inference for more adaptive routing. Our code for GraphPlanner is released at https://github.com/ulab-uiuc/GraphPlanner.

*Jongyoon Kim,Hojae Han,Seung-won Hwang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce a new, automatic, semantics-based evaluation framework for LLM-generated formal theorems, judging a theorem correct only if all its dependent successor theorems still compile, and show that current top LLMs perform poorly under this stricter metric.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In formal theorem proving with LLMs, current evaluations usually check surface similarity to human-written proofs or require costly human review, which do not reliably reflect true semantic correctness. Inspired by how code generation moved from textual similarity to test-based evaluation, the authors want a scalable, automatic, and semantically meaningful way to assess whether LLM-generated formal theorems are actually correct within a larger formal development.

Method: They propose a framework (T) that evaluates generated formal theorems by integration-style testing: for a candidate theorem in a Lean 4 repository, they check whether all its dependent successor theorems still compile successfully when the generated theorem is substituted. They automatically construct a benchmark from 5 real-world Lean 4 repositories, extracting 2,206 theorem-generation problems and, for each, the set of successor theorems (about 41 on average) that depend on it. They then run various state-of-the-art LLMs under this framework, providing natural language proofs and successor theorems as context, and compute a Testing Accuracy metric based on compilation success of successors.

Result: The benchmark covers 2,206 problems with an average of 41 successor theorems each, all extracted automatically from 5 Lean 4 repos. While models achieve high success rates if judged only by whether the generated theorem itself compiles, their performance drops sharply when evaluated using the new semantic Testing Accuracy metric. The strongest model tested, Claude-Sonnet-4.5, attains only 38.9% Testing Accuracy on the full benchmark, even when given both the natural language proof and the successor theorems as context.

Conclusion: Semantic, integration-style evaluation reveals that current LLMs are far from reliably generating formal theorems that integrate correctly into larger proof developments, despite promising surface-level compilation metrics. The proposed framework and benchmark expose a significant gap in current capabilities and provide a more realistic, automated way to measure progress in LLM-based formal theorem generation.

Abstract: Recent advances in large language models (LLMs) have shown promise in formal theorem proving, yet evaluating semantic correctness remains challenging. Existing evaluations rely on indirect proxies such as lexical overlap with human-annotated proof, or expensive manual inspection. Inspired by the shift from lexical comparison to test-based evaluation in code generation, we propose T , a framework that evaluates the semantic correctness of formal theorems: a generated theorem is considered correct only if all dependent successor theorems compile successfully, analogous to integration testing. We construct a benchmark from 5 real-world Lean 4 repositories, comprising 2,206 problems paired with 41 successor theorems on average, automatically extracted without human effort. Experiments demonstrate that while state-of-the-art models achieve high compilation success, they perform significantly worse under our semantic metric. The best model, Claude-Sonnet-4.5, achieves only 38.9% Testing Accuracy on the full set, given both natural language proof and successor theorems as context, revealing a critical gap in current theorem generation capabilities.

</details>


### [43] [Agri-CPJ: A Training-Free Explainable Framework for Agricultural Pest Diagnosis Using Caption-Prompt-Judge and LLM-as-a-Judge](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23701)
*Wentao Zhang,Qi Zhang,Mingkun Xu,Mu You,Henghua Shen,Zhongzhi He,Keyan Jin,Derek F. Wong,Tao Fang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes Agri-CPJ, a training-free, few-shot framework using large vision-language models and an LLM judge to improve accuracy and interpretability of crop disease diagnosis from field photos via structured, iteratively refined captions and judged candidate answers.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing crop disease vision models, even when strong on benchmarks, often hallucinate plant or disease species and provide opaque reasoning, making them unreliable and hard to trust for practitioners. There is a need for a method that both improves diagnostic accuracy and produces transparent, inspectable reasoning that domain experts can audit.

Method: Agri-CPJ (Caption-Prompt-Judge) is a training-free few-shot framework built on large vision-language models. First, the system generates a structured morphological caption of the crop image, describing visual symptoms. This caption is iteratively refined using multi-dimensional quality gating to ensure completeness and correctness before any diagnostic questions are answered. Then, using this refined caption, two diagnostic candidate responses are produced from complementary viewpoints. Finally, an LLM judge, guided by domain-specific criteria, compares these responses and selects the better one. The approach is evaluated with different model pairs (e.g., GPT-5-Nano with GPT-5-mini-generated captions, and Qwen-VL-Chat) on established benchmarks.

Result: Ablation studies show that the caption refinement stage is the most impactful component: removing it consistently lowers diagnostic accuracy across tested models. On CDDMBench, combining GPT-5-Nano with GPT-5-mini-generated captions improves disease classification accuracy by +22.7 percentage points and QA score by +19.5 points over baselines without captions. When directly applied (without adaptation) to the AgMMU-MCQs benchmark, GPT-5-Nano reaches 77.84% accuracy and Qwen-VL-Chat 64.54%, matching or surpassing most open-source models of similar size despite the change from open-ended answers to multiple-choice format.

Conclusion: Agri-CPJ significantly boosts both accuracy and interpretability of crop disease diagnosis from field images by enforcing a structured, refined morphological caption prior to answering and by using an LLM judge to select among candidate diagnoses. The method is training-free, transfers well across task formats, and produces a readable audit trail (caption plus judge rationale) that allows practitioners to see and contest the specific visual observations underpinning a diagnosis. Code and data are publicly available for further use and validation.

Abstract: Crop disease diagnosis from field photographs faces two recurring problems: models that score well on benchmarks frequently hallucinate species names, and when predictions are correct, the reasoning behind them is typically inaccessible to the practitioner. This paper describes Agri-CPJ (Caption-Prompt-Judge), a training-free few-shot framework in which a large vision-language model first generates a structured morphological caption, iteratively refined through multi-dimensional quality gating, before any diagnostic question is answered. Two candidate responses are then generated from complementary viewpoints, and an LLM judge selects the stronger one based on domain-specific criteria. Caption refinement is the component with the largest individual impact: ablations confirm that skipping it consistently degrades downstream accuracy across both models tested. On CDDMBench, pairing GPT-5-Nano with GPT-5-mini-generated captions yields \textbf{+22.7} pp in disease classification and \textbf{+19.5} points in QA score over no-caption baselines. Evaluated without modification on AgMMU-MCQs, GPT-5-Nano reached 77.84\% and Qwen-VL-Chat reached 64.54\%, placing them at or above most open-source models of comparable scale despite the format shift from open-ended to multiple-choice. The structured caption and judge rationale together constitute a readable audit trail: a practitioner who disagrees with a diagnosis can identify the specific caption observation that was incorrect. Code and data are publicly available https://github.com/CPJ-Agricultural/CPJ-Agricultural-Diagnosis

### [44] [AIPsy-Affect: A Keyword-Free Clinical Stimulus Battery for Mechanistic Interpretability of Emotion in Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23719)
*Michael Keeman*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces AIPsy-Affect, a large, keyword-free emotional stimulus battery to study genuine emotion representations in language models without confounds from explicit emotion words.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most mechanistic interpretability work on emotions in large language models uses prompts that explicitly contain emotion words (e.g., “furious”), making it unclear whether models encode actual affective states or just recognize emotion vocabulary. This confound undermines claims about emotion circuits, features, and causal interventions, so a controlled, keyword-free stimulus set is needed.

Method: The authors constructed a 480-item clinical stimulus battery: 192 short vignettes that implicitly evoke each of Plutchik’s eight primary emotions without using emotion keywords; 192 closely matched neutral vignettes with the same characters, setting, length, and structure but with affective elements removed; and additional splits for moderate intensity and discriminant validity. They then tested whether simple lexical methods or a contextual classifier could detect emotion categories from these vignettes, using a bag-of-words sentiment model, an emotion-category lexicon, and a transformer-based classifier, and compared performance to a keyword-rich control set.

Result: The matched-pair design ensures that any internal model representation that distinguishes an emotional vignette from its neutral counterpart cannot rely on explicit emotion words. Empirically, bag-of-words and lexicon-based methods only register situational content and fail to capture affect categories, while a contextual transformer can reliably detect the presence of affect (high statistical significance, p < 10^-15) but performs poorly at emotion categorization (5.2% top-1 accuracy) compared to strong performance (82.5% top-1) on a keyword-rich control dataset. The new battery scales their previous 96-item set up to 480 items.

Conclusion: AIPsy-Affect provides a rigorously controlled, open-source benchmark for probing genuine affective representations in language models without contamination from explicit emotion words. It enables more valid use of interpretability tools—such as linear probes, activation patching, sparse autoencoders, causal ablations, and steering vectors—for identifying and manipulating emotion-related circuitry, because any discovered distinctions must arise from narrative-evoked affect rather than lexical shortcuts.

Abstract: Mechanistic interpretability research on emotion in large language models -- linear probing, activation patching, sparse autoencoder (SAE) feature analysis, causal ablation, steering vector extraction -- depends on stimuli that contain the words for the emotions they test. When a probe fires on "I am furious", it is unclear whether the model has detected anger or detected the word "furious". The two readings have very different consequences for every downstream claim about emotion circuits, features, and interventions. We release AIPsy-Affect, a 480-item clinical stimulus battery that removes the confound at the stimulus level: 192 keyword-free vignettes evoking each of Plutchik's eight primary emotions through narrative situation alone, 192 matched neutral controls that share characters, setting, length, and surface structure with the affect surgically removed, plus moderate-intensity and discriminant-validity splits. The matched-pair structure supports linear probing, activation patching, SAE feature analysis, causal ablation, and steering vector extraction under a strong methodological guarantee: any internal representation that distinguishes a clinical item from its matched neutral cannot be doing so on the basis of emotion-keyword presence. A three-method NLP defense battery -- bag-of-words sentiment, an emotion-category lexicon, and a contextual transformer classifier -- confirms the property: bag-of-words methods see only situational vocabulary, and a contextual classifier detects affect (p < 10^-15) but cannot identify the category (5.2% top-1 vs. 82.5% on a keyword-rich control). AIPsy-Affect extends our earlier 96-item battery (arXiv:2603.22295) by a factor of four and is released openly under MIT license.

Conclusion: Multimodalizing the QUD framework and providing the MQUD dataset enables VLMs to better capture the deeper inquisitive structure of scientific discourse. While scientific visualizations have been used to evaluate Vision-Language Models (VLMs) capabilities, current benchmarks are limited to questions that focus simply on extracting information from them. Such questions only require lower-level reasoning, do not take into account the context in which a figure appears, and do not reflect the communicative goals the authors wish to achieve. We generate inquisitive questions that reach the depth of questions humans generate when engaging with scientific papers, conditioned on both the figure and the paper's context, and require reasoning across both modalities. To do so, we extend the linguistic theory of Questions Under Discussion (QUD) from being text-only to multimodal, where implicit questions are raised and resolved as discourse progresses. We present MQUD, a dataset of research papers in which such questions are made explicit and annotated by the original authors. We show that fine-tuning a VLM on MQUD shifts the model from generating generic low-level visual questions to content-specific grounding that requires a high-level of multimodal reasoning, yielding higher-quality, more visually grounded multimodal QUD generation.

Abstract: Small language models (SLMs) are promising for real-world deployment due to their efficiency and low operational cost. However, their limited capacity struggles with high-stakes legal reasoning tasks that require coherent statute interpretation and logically consistent deduction. Furthermore, training SLMs for such tasks demands high-quality, concise reasoning trajectories, which are prohibitively expensive to manually collect and difficult to curate via standard rejection sampling, lacking granularity beyond final verdicts. To address these challenges, we propose {LegalDrill}, a diagnosis-driven synthesis framework that extracts and iteratively refines reasoning trajectories from a capable teacher via fine-grained prompting, then a self-reflective verification is employed to adaptively select the most effective data for the SLM student. The resulting data empower SLM training through supervised fine-tuning and direct preference optimization. ### [47] [DRACULA: Hunting for the Actions Users Want Deep Research Agents to Execute](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23815)
*Nishant Balepur,Malachi Hamada,Varsha Kishore,Sergey Feldman,Amanpreet Singh,Pao Siangliulue,Joseph Chee Chang,Rachel Rudinger,Eunsol Choi,Jordan Lee Boyd-Graber,Doug Downey,Aakanksha Naik*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce DRACULA, a dataset of expert user feedback on intermediate actions in scientific deep research agents, and show that modeling and personalizing these action choices is crucial and non-trivial.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing deep research (DR) agents focus on producing final multi-section reports from papers, and feedback protocols typically only rate these final outputs. This makes it difficult to understand or learn which intermediate planning actions (like deciding what sections to add) lead to better reports, limiting the ability to improve long-horizon DR agents.

Method: They build and deploy a DR system used by 19 expert CS researchers over five weeks. The system periodically proposes candidate intermediate actions (e.g., adding or changing sections) in response to user queries. Users select preferred actions and later judge whether the final report actually followed these selections. This process yields a dataset, DRACULA, containing action preference data and execution judgments. They then (1) verify that a DR agent can execute the collected actions and (2) run simulation experiments in which LLMs attempt to predict user action selections, incorporating different forms of user context and history. Finally, they use insights from these simulations to implement an online intervention that dynamically generates new actions conditioned on a user's past behavior.

Result: They collect 8,103 action preference annotations and 5,230 execution judgments, confirming that the DR agent can implement the proposed actions. Simulation shows that LLM judges have difficulty predicting user-preferred actions at first, but accuracy improves substantially when they are given the user’s full selection history, more than when using self-reported profiles or heuristic context. The data also show that even for identical queries, different users choose different actions due to hidden goals, which limits the effectiveness of naive simulation. Using these insights, they deploy an intervention that proposes personalized, history-informed actions, which users choose most frequently in subsequent studies.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that the core challenge for deep research agents is not only executing actions but deciding which actions to take, and that user feedback on intermediate actions is essential for addressing this. DRACULA provides the first large-scale dataset of such feedback and reveals the importance of modeling user-specific, latent goals via interaction history. The authors open-source the dataset, study design, and simulation tasks to facilitate future work on learning and using action-level feedback in long-horizon AI agents.

Abstract: Scientific Deep Research (DR) agents answer user queries by synthesizing research papers into multi-section reports. User feedback can improve their utility, but existing protocols only score the final report, making it hard to study and learn which intermediate actions DR agents should take to improve reports. We collect DRACULA, the first dataset with user feedback on intermediate actions for DR. Over five weeks, nineteen expert CS researchers ask queries to a DR system that proposes actions (e.g., "Add a section on datasets"). Our users select actions they prefer, then judge whether an output report applied their selections successfully, yielding 8,103 action preferences and 5,230 execution judgments. After confirming a DR agent can execute DRACULA's actions, we study the predictability of user-preferred actions via simulation-how well LLMs predict the actions users select-a step toward learning to generate useful actions. We discover: (1) LLM judges initially struggle to predict action selections, but improve most when using a user's full selection history, rather than self-reported or extrapolated user context signals; (2) Users' selections for the same query differ based on unstated goals, bottlenecking simulation and motivating affordances that let users steer reports; and (3) Our simulation results inform an online intervention that generates new actions based on the user's past interactions, which users pick most often in follow-up studies. Overall, while work extensively studies execution, DRACULA reveals a key challenge is deciding which actions to execute in the first place. We open-source DRACULA's study design, user feedback, and simulation tasks to spur future work on action feedback for long-horizon agents.

We empirically show that statistical models (random forests) trained on string similarity features are surprisingly effective for inducing German dialect lexicons. ### [49] [One Size Fits None: Heuristic Collapse in LLM Investment Advice](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23837)
*Jillian Ross,Andrew W. Lo*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that large language models giving investment advice overly rely on a single cue—self-reported risk tolerance—while largely ignoring other important client information, even when enhanced with web search.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are increasingly used as advisors in high-stakes areas like medicine, law, and finance, where regulations and good practice demand that advice be tailored to a person’s full situation. The authors want to know whether current frontier LLMs truly integrate all relevant user information or instead fall back on oversimplified rules, which could make their recommendations systematically biased or non-compliant with professional standards.

Method: The authors focus on investment advice because financial regulation clearly requires individualized recommendations based on a client’s overall profile. They prompt frontier LLMs with client scenarios containing multiple factors (e.g., age, income, assets, goals, time horizon, risk tolerance) and collect the models’ portfolio allocation decisions. They then fit interpretable surrogate models (such as simple predictive models) to the LLMs’ decisions to infer which input variables actually drive the outputs. They also run variants where the LLMs can use web search to see if access to external information changes their decision patterns.

Result: Across models and scenarios, the surrogate analyses show that LLMs’ portfolio recommendations are dominated by a single feature: the user’s stated risk tolerance. Other financially and legally relevant attributes—like time horizon, income, savings, or goals—have much smaller or negligible influence on the final allocations. Allowing the LLMs to use web search somewhat reduces this overreliance but does not eliminate it; the overall pattern of heuristic collapse persists.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that frontier LLMs, when used as investment advisors, tend to compress complex, multi-factor decisions into a simple heuristic driven mainly by risk tolerance. This “heuristic collapse” is not fixed by scaling up models or adding web search. Therefore, evaluating LLM advisors only on surface output quality is insufficient; developers and regulators need tools to audit how sensitive model recommendations are to the full range of user inputs, and systems must be designed to ensure genuine, context-rich reasoning in high-stakes advisory settings.

Abstract: Large language models are increasingly deployed as advisors in high-stakes domains -- answering medical questions, interpreting legal documents, recommending financial products -- where good advice requires integrating a user's full context rather than responding to salient surface features. We investigate whether frontier LLMs actually do this, or whether they instead exhibit heuristic collapse: a systematic reduction of complex, multi-factor decisions to a small number of dominant inputs. We study the phenomenon in investment advice, where legal standards explicitly require individualized reasoning over a client's full circumstances. Applying interpretable surrogate models to LLM outputs, we find systematic heuristic collapse: investment allocation decisions are largely determined by self-reported risk tolerance, while other relevant factors contribute minimally. We further find that web search partially attenuates heuristic collapse but does not resolve it. These findings suggest that heuristic collapse is not resolved by web search augmentation or model scale alone, and that deploying LLMs as advisors requires auditing input sensitivity, not just output quality.

### [50] [Reheat Nachos for Dinner? Evaluating AI Support for Cross-Cultural Communication of Neologisms](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23842)

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Non-native speakers struggle with understanding and appropriately using neologisms and emerging slang in informal conversations with native speakers, but increasingly rely on AI tools for help. It is unclear which kinds of AI support actually improve communicative competence and contextual use, and whether learners’ self-perceptions align with native speakers’ judgments.

Method: The authors conduct a human-subjects experiment with 234 non-native English speakers. Participants learn new English neologisms under one of several support conditions: AI-generated definition, AI-generated rewrite into simpler English, AI-generated explanation of meaning and usage, or a non-AI dictionary baseline. After learning, participants write messages using the target word to an imagined native-speaker friend and judge the contextual appropriateness of the neologism in two provided writing samples. Native speakers then rate the communicative competence of the NNS-produced messages. The authors compare performance across support conditions and analyze gaps between NNS self-perception, NNS judgments, and NS evaluations, as well as NNS vs. NS writing quality.

Result: Among the support conditions, AI Explanation of meaning and usage produces the largest improvement in native speakers’ ratings of NNS communicative competence compared to no support. However, NNS’ judgments of contextual appropriateness of neologism use do not differ meaningfully across AI support types. NNS self-assessed performance tends to be higher than native speakers’ evaluations, indicating overestimation of their communicative competence. The study also finds a substantial quality gap between writing produced by NNS and NS, even with AI support.

Conclusion: AI tools that provide richer explanations of neologism meaning and usage are more effective than simple definitions or rewrites for improving non-native speakers’ communicative competence in informal neologism use, at least as judged by native speakers. Nonetheless, AI support does not translate into better metapragmatic sensitivity (contextual appropriateness judgments), and non-native speakers systematically overestimate their own performance relative to native speakers’ evaluations. The persistent gap between NNS and NS writing underscores current limitations of AI tools and suggests the need for future tool designs that better support pragmatic usage and calibrate learners’ self-assessment.

Abstract: Neologisms and emerging slang are central to daily conversation, yet challenging for non-native speakers (NNS) to interpret and use appropriately in cross-cultural communication with native speakers (NS). NNS increasingly make use of Artificial Intelligence (AI) tools to learn these words. We study the utility of such tools in mediating an informal communication scenario through a human-subjects study (N=234): NNS participants learn English neologisms with AI support, write messages using the learned word to an NS friend, and judge contextual appropriateness of the neologism in two provided writing samples. Using both NS evaluator-rated communicative competence of NNS-produced writing and NNS' contextual appropriateness judgments, we compare three AI-based support conditions: AI Definition, AI Rewrite into simpler English, AI Explanation of meaning and usage, and Non-AI Dictionary for comparison. We show that AI Explanation yields the largest gains over no support in NS-rated competence, while contextual appropriateness judgments show indifference across support. NNS participants' self-reported perceptions tend to overestimate NS ratings, revealing a mismatch between perceived and actual competence. We further observe a significant gap between NNS- and NS-produced writing, highlighting the limitations of current AI tools and informing design for future tools.

### [51] [Translate or Simplify First: An Analysis of Cross-lingual Text Simplification in English and French](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23844)
*Ido Dahan,Omer Toledano,Roey J. Gafter,Sharon Pardo,Oren Tsur,Hila Zahavi,Elior Sulem*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper evaluates different prompting strategies for cross-lingual text simplification between English and French using large language models, comparing direct, compositional, and decompositional prompts across multiple corpora and models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Cross-lingual text simplification can improve accessibility by handling translation and simplification together, but it is unclear which prompting strategies with modern LLMs best balance meaning preservation and simplicity across languages and domains.

Method: The authors define five prompting systems for CLTS: (1) Direct prompting that asks the LLM to translate and simplify in one step; (2) a composition strategy that translates then simplifies within one prompt; (3) a composition strategy that simplifies then translates within one prompt; (4) a decomposition strategy that translates then simplifies using two separate prompts; and (5) a decomposition strategy that simplifies then translates using two separate prompts. They test these on five genre-diverse corpora (Wikipedia and medical texts) using seven current LLMs. Evaluation combines automatic metrics (e.g., BLEU), detailed linguistic feature analysis to quantify simplicity, and human judgments of simplicity and meaning preservation.

Result: Direct prompting yields the highest BLEU scores, suggesting stronger meaning preservation, while Translate-then-Simplify strategies produce outputs with the greatest simplification according to linguistic feature-based measures. Performance trends are consistent across multiple LLMs, domains, and evaluation setups.

Conclusion: Different prompting strategies trade off between fidelity and simplicity in cross-lingual text simplification: direct prompts better preserve meaning, whereas translate-then-simplify prompts better increase simplicity. Prompt design is therefore a key factor in CLTS performance, and practitioners should choose strategies depending on whether meaning preservation or simplification is the primary goal.

Abstract: Cross-Lingual Text Simplification (CLTS) aims to make content more accessible across languages by simultaneously addressing both linguistic complexity and translation. This study investigates the effectiveness of different prompting strategies for CLTS between English and French using large language models (LLMs). We examine five distinct prompting systems: a direct prompt instructing the LLM to perform both translation and simplification simultaneously, two Composition approaches that either translate-then-simplify or simplify-then-translate within a single prompt, and two decomposition approaches that perform the same operations in separate, consecutive prompts. These systems are evaluated across a diverse set of five corpora of different genres (Wikipedia and medical texts) using seven state-of-the-art LLMs. Output quality is assessed through a multi-faceted evaluation framework comprising automatic metrics, comprehensive linguistic feature analysis, and human evaluation of simplicity and meaning preservation. Our findings reveal that while direct prompting consistently achieves the highest BLEU scores, indicating meaning fidelity, Translate-then-Simplify approaches demonstrate the highest simplicity, as measured by the linguistic features.

This setup lets one operator supervise multiple concurrent sessions and be interrupted only when the system is uncertain. The system operates on a schema-driven view of the BPM interface and includes monitoring and safe fallbacks for production. In production, it automated 45% of sessions and reduced average handling time by 39% without degrading support quality level.

### [53] [Knowledge Vector of Logical Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23877)
*Zixuan Wang,Yuanyuan Lei*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how different types of logical reasoning (deductive, inductive, abductive) are represented inside LLMs, finds they correspond to largely independent vectors in a linear space, and proposes a refinement method that makes these representations more complementary, improving reasoning performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Logical reasoning is central to LLM capability, but deductive, inductive, and abductive reasoning may be encoded differently and interact suboptimally. Cognitive science suggests these reasoning types interact in humans, so understanding and improving their representation and interaction in LLMs could enhance reasoning abilities and interpretability.

Method: 1) Analyze LLM internal representations to identify reasoning-specific knowledge vectors for deductive, inductive, and abductive reasoning in a linear representation space.
2) Measure independence/correlation among these vectors.
3) Propose a complementary subspace-constrained refinement framework: a complementary loss that lets each reasoning vector draw auxiliary knowledge from others and a subspace constraint loss that preserves each vector's unique characteristics.
4) Perform steering experiments by moving representations along these reasoning vectors to evaluate performance.
5) Conduct mechanism-interpretability analysis to examine shared vs. specific features of the reasoning vectors.

Result: They find that each reasoning type can be modeled as a distinct reasoning-specific vector and that these vectors are largely independent. After applying their refinement framework, steering along the refined vectors leads to consistent gains in logical reasoning performance, indicating that incorporating complementary knowledge across reasoning types is beneficial.

Conclusion: Logical reasoning types in LLMs are encoded as distinct, mostly independent vectors in a linear space, but these can be refined to share complementary knowledge without losing their unique properties. The proposed complementary subspace-constrained refinement framework improves reasoning performance and provides mechanistic interpretability insights into how different reasoning types are represented and can interact in LLMs.

Abstract: Logical reasoning serve as a central capability in LLMs and includes three main forms: deductive, inductive, and abductive reasoning. In this work, we study the knowledge representations of these reasoning types in LLMs and analyze the correlations among them. Our analysis shows that each form of logical reasoning can be captured as a reasoning-specific knowledge vector in a linear representation space, yet these vectors are largely independent of each other. Motivated by cognitive science theory that these subforms of logical reasoning interact closely in the human brain, as well as our observation that the reasoning process for one type can benefit from the reasoning chain produced by another, we further propose to refine the knowledge representations of each reasoning type in LLMs to encourage complementarity between them. To this end, we design a complementary subspace-constrained refinement framework, which introduces a complementary loss that enables each reasoning vector to leverage auxiliary knowledge from the others, and a subspace constraint loss that prevents erasure of their unique characteristics. Through steering experiments along reasoning vectors, we find that refined vectors incorporating complementary knowledge yield consistent performance gains. We also conduct a mechanism-interpretability analysis of each reasoning vector, revealing insights into the shared and specific features of different reasoning in LLMs.

### [56] [Quantum Knowledge Graph: Modeling Context-Dependent Triplet Validity](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23972)
*Yao Wang,Zixu Geng,Jun Yan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Quantum Knowledge Graphs (QKGs), where the validity of each KG triplet depends on context, and shows that using QKGs for validation improves large language model medical question answering, especially when aligned with patient-specific context.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard knowledge graphs treat relations as universally valid, which is unrealistic in domains like medicine, where the applicability of a fact depends on specific patient contexts (e.g., demographics, comorbidities). The authors aim to make KG-based LLM reasoning more clinically accurate and patient-specific by explicitly modeling when a relation should count as valid evidence.

Method: They define triplet validity as a function of context, calling this structure a Quantum Knowledge Graph (QKG). They instantiate a QKG using a diabetes-focused subgraph of PrimeKG, adding 68,651 context-sensitive relations annotated with patient-group constraints. They then build a Reasoner–Validator pipeline for medical QA on a KG-grounded subset of MedReason (2,788 questions). Different LLMs (Haiku-4.5 and Qwen-3.6-Plus) are used as Reasoner and Validator, and several settings are compared: no validator, standard KG validation, and QKG validation with context matching.

Result: With Haiku-4.5 as both Reasoner and Validator, KG-backed validation improves answer accuracy over no validation by 0.61 percentage points, while QKG with context matching yields the best performance: +0.79 pp over KG without context matching and +1.40 pp over no-validator (all statistically significant by paired McNemar tests, p<0.05). Using a stronger validator, Qwen-3.6-Plus, the raw gain of QKG over the no-validator baseline increases to +5.96 pp. The advantage of context matching is not statistically significant on the raw set (p=0.73), but becomes borderline significant (p=0.05) after filtering for potential knowledge leakage and suspicious questions, suggesting that evaluation artifacts, rather than QKG limitations, cap performance.

Conclusion: Context-dependent modeling of KG triplets (QKG) improves LLM-based clinical reasoning beyond what is possible with standard, context-agnostic KGs. The key value of a medical KG is not only its factual coverage but its ability to represent when and for which patient groups those facts are applicable. The authors make their QKG datasets and code publicly available to facilitate reproducibility and further research.

Abstract: Knowledge graphs (KGs) are increasingly used to support large lan guage model (LLM) reasoning, but standard triplet-based KGs treat each relation as globally valid. In many settings, whether a relation should count as evidence depends on the context. We therefore formulate triplet validity as a triplet-specific function of context and refer to this formulation as a Quantum Knowledge Graph (QKG). We instantiate QKG in medicine using a diabetes-centered PrimeKG subgraph, whose 68,651 context-sensitive relations are further annotated with patient-group-specific constraints. We evaluate it in a reasoner--validator pipeline for medical question answering on a KG-grounded subset of MedReason containing 2,788 questions. With Haiku-4.5 as both the Reasoner and the Validator, KG-backed validation significantly improves over a no-validator baseline ($+0.61$ pp), and QKG with context matching yields the largest gain, outperforming both KG validation without context matching ($+0.79$ pp) and the no-validator baseline ($+1.40$ pp; paired McNemar, all $p<0.05$). Under a stronger validator (Qwen-3.6-Plus), the raw QKG gain over the no-validator baseline grows from $+1.40$ pp to $+5.96$ pp; the context-matching gap is non-significant ($p=0.73$) on the raw set but becomes borderline significant ($p=0.05$) after adjustment for knowledge leakage and suspicious questions, consistent with a benchmark-gold ceiling rather than a QKG limitation. Taken together, the results support the view that the value of a KG in LLM-based clinical reasoning lies not merely in storing medically related facts, but in representing whether those facts are applicable to the specific patient context. For reproducibility and further research, we release the curated QKG datasets and source code.\footnote{https://github.com/HKAI-Sci/QKG}

Although some recent works consider the effect of irrelevant user interactions in a hybrid-modeling way, they still suffer from the mutual interference between structural noise and semantic noise, leading to limited performance for robust detection. To alleviate this issue, this paper proposes a novel Propagation Structure-Semantic Transfer Learning framework (PSS-TL) for robust fake news detection under a teacher-student architecture. Our central idea is to distill high-cost agentic reasoning into a trainable in-house model. These findings suggest that reinforcement learning can turn product mapping from a high-latency agentic pipeline into a scalable, inspectable, and production-ready in-house system.

### [59] [Stabilizing Efficient Reasoning with Step-Level Advantage Selection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24003)
*Han Wang,Xiaodong Yu,Jialian Wu,Jiang Liu,Ximeng Sun,Mohit Bansal,Zicheng Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper studies how short-context post-training affects reasoning length and stability in LLMs and introduces Step-level Advantage Selection (SAS), a method that makes training more stable and compresses reasoning while preserving or improving accuracy.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs achieve strong reasoning performance but often require long, computationally expensive reasoning traces at inference time. Existing efficiency methods use length-based rewards or pruning and are usually post-trained with shorter context windows than those used in base model training, but the isolated effect of short-context post-training is not well understood. There is a need to both understand this effect and to design methods that reduce reasoning length without sacrificing accuracy or stability.

Method: The authors first perform an empirical study where they apply standard GRPO post-training under a shorter context window, without any explicit length-aware objective, to observe its effects on reasoning compression, training stability, and accuracy. They then propose Step-level Advantage Selection (SAS), which works at the granularity of reasoning steps. SAS sets the advantage to zero for: (1) low-confidence steps in correct rollouts, and (2) high-confidence steps in rollouts rejected by a verifier, since such failures often come from truncation or verifier issues rather than genuinely bad reasoning. This selective masking of step-level advantages aims to stabilize optimization and better align credit assignment with true reasoning quality.

Result: Short-context GRPO alone already compresses reasoning traces significantly but leads to unstable training and reduced accuracy. With SAS, LLMs trained on diverse reasoning tasks (mathematical and general) achieve an average Pass@1 accuracy improvement of 0.86 percentage points over the best length-aware baseline, while also reducing reasoning length by 16.3% on average, demonstrating a superior accuracy–efficiency trade-off.

Conclusion: Short-context post-training is itself a strong driver of reasoning compression, but naively applying it causes instability and accuracy loss. Step-level Advantage Selection provides a principled way to control which steps influence the learning signal, mitigating these problems. SAS yields shorter reasoning traces with improved or preserved accuracy across benchmarks, offering a better balance between computational efficiency and reasoning performance than prior length-aware methods.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) achieve strong reasoning performance by allocating substantial computation at inference time, often generating long and verbose reasoning traces. While recent work on efficient reasoning reduces this overhead through length-based rewards or pruning, many approaches are post-trained under a much shorter context window than base-model training, a factor whose effect has not been systematically isolated. We first show that short-context post-training alone, using standard GRPO without any length-aware objective, already induces substantial reasoning compression-but at the cost of increasingly unstable training dynamics and accuracy degradation. To address this, we propose Step-level Advantage Selection (SAS), which operates at the reasoning-step level and assigns a zero advantage to low-confidence steps in correct rollouts and to high-confidence steps in verifier-failed rollouts, where failures often arise from truncation or verifier issues rather than incorrect reasoning. Across diverse mathematical and general reasoning benchmarks, SAS improves average Pass@1 accuracy by 0.86 points over the strongest length-aware baseline while reducing average reasoning length by 16.3%, yielding a better accuracy-efficiency trade-off.
