### [60] [From Skill Text to Skill Structure: The Scheduling-Structural-Logical Representation for Agent Skills](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24026)
*Qiliang Liang,Hansi Wang,Zhong Liang,Yang Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes SSL, a structured representation for LLM agent skills, and shows it improves tasks like skill search and risk assessment over text-only baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agent skills are stored in text-heavy formats (e.g., SKILL.md, natural-language records), where interfaces, control flow, and side effects are entangled. This makes it hard for machines to manage, search, and assess skills because they must reason over implicit information buried in text. The authors are motivated to create an explicit, machine-usable representation that better organizes this skill knowledge.

Method: Inspired by classic knowledge representation frameworks (Memory Organization Packets, Script Theory, Conceptual Dependency), the authors design the Scheduling-Structural-Logical (SSL) representation that separates three layers of information: (1) scheduling signals at the skill level, (2) execution structure at the scene level, and (3) logic-level actions and resource usage. They then build an LLM-based normalizer to convert existing skill artifacts into SSL and evaluate SSL on two tasks: Skill Discovery and Risk Assessment, comparing against text-only baselines.

Result: Using SSL yields improved performance on both evaluated tasks. For Skill Discovery, the Mean Reciprocal Rank (MRR) increases from 0.573 (text-only) to 0.707 with SSL. For Risk Assessment, macro F1 improves from 0.744 to 0.787. These results indicate that the structured SSL representation makes the skills more searchable and easier to assess for risk than when represented only as text.

Conclusion: Explicit, structured, and source-grounded representations of skills, as instantiated in SSL, help LLM agent systems better search, review, and assess reusable skills than text-only representations. SSL should be viewed as an early, practical step toward more inspectable, reusable, and operational skill representations, not as a complete standard or full solution for skill management and use.

Abstract: LLM agents increasingly rely on reusable skills, capability packages that combine instructions, control flow, constraints, and tool calls. In most current agent systems, however, skills are still represented by text-heavy artifacts, including SKILL.md-style documents and structured records whose machine-usable evidence remains embedded largely in natural-language descriptions. This poses a challenge for skill-centered agent systems: managing skill collections and using skills to support agent both require reasoning over invocation interfaces, execution structure, and concrete side effects that are often entangled in a single textual surface. An explicit representation of skill knowledge may therefore help make these artifacts easier for machines to acquire and leverage. Drawing on Memory Organization Packets, Script Theory, and Conceptual Dependency from Schank and Abelson's classical work on linguistic knowledge representation, we introduce what is, to our knowledge, the first structured representation for agent skill artifacts that disentangles skill-level scheduling signals, scene-level execution structure, and logic-level action and resource-use evidence: the Scheduling-Structural-Logical (SSL) representation. We instantiate SSL with an LLM-based normalizer and evaluate it on a corpus of skills in two tasks, Skill Discovery and Risk Assessment, and superiorly outperform the text-only baselines: in Skill Discovery, SSL improves MRR from 0.573 to 0.707; in Risk Assessment, it improves macro F1 from 0.744 to 0.787. These findings reveal that explicit, source-grounded structure makes agent skills easier to search and review. They also suggest that SSL is best understood as a practical step toward more inspectable, reusable, and operationally actionable skill representations for agent systems, rather than as a finished standard or an end-to-end mechanism for managing and using skills.

### [61] [Improving Robustness of Tabular Retrieval via Representational Stability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24040)
*Kushal Raj Bhandari,Adarsh Singh,Jianxi Gao,Soham Dan,Vivek Gupta*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Study of how different table serializations (CSV, HTML, etc.) affect transformer-based table retrieval, and methods to make retrieval robust to these format differences.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Transformer-based table retrieval flattens tables into text, but semantically identical tables can be serialized in different textual formats (csv, tsv, html, markdown, ddl). These different serializations can lead to different embeddings and retrieval performance, which is undesirable because retrieval should depend on table semantics, not arbitrary formatting choices.

Method: 1) Empirically test multiple table serializations (csv, tsv, html, markdown, ddl) across several retrieval models (MPNet, BGE-M3, ReasonIR, SPLADE) to quantify variability in embeddings and retrieval quality. 2) Interpret embeddings from each serialization as noisy views of a common semantic signal and compute a centroid (average) embedding across serializations as a canonical representation. 3) Show that centroid averaging suppresses format-specific variations when format-induced shifts differ across tables. 4) Design a lightweight residual bottleneck adapter layer on top of a frozen encoder that learns to map single-serialization embeddings toward the centroid targets, while preserving overall variance and enforcing covariance regularization. 5) Evaluate how this adapter impacts robustness for dense and sparse retrievers.

Result: 1) Demonstrate that semantically equivalent table serializations lead to substantially different embeddings and retrieval rankings for multiple retrieval models and benchmarks. 2) Show that centroid embeddings, formed by averaging across serializations, outperform individual serializations in aggregate pairwise comparisons for several retrievers (MPNet, BGE-M3, ReasonIR, SPLADE). 3) Show that the proposed residual bottleneck adapter improves robustness to serialization changes for several dense retrievers, though its benefits vary by model and are weaker for sparse lexical retrievers such as SPLADE.

Conclusion: Serialization format is a major, previously underappreciated source of variance in transformer-based table retrieval. Treating different serializations as noisy views and using centroid-based target representations can stabilize performance and better capture table semantics independent of format. A simple post hoc geometric correction via a residual bottleneck adapter can move single-serialization embeddings toward these centroid representations, improving robustness especially for dense retrievers. This points toward serialization-invariant table retrieval as an important direction for building more stable and reliable systems.

Abstract: Transformer-based table retrieval systems flatten structured tables into token sequences, making retrieval sensitive to the choice of serialization even when table semantics remain unchanged. We show that semantically equivalent serializations, such as $\texttt{csv}$, $\texttt{tsv}$, $\texttt{html}$, $\texttt{markdown}$, and $\texttt{ddl}$, can produce substantially different embeddings and retrieval results across multiple benchmarks and retriever families. To address this instability, we treat serialization embedding as noisy views of a shared semantic signal and use its centroid as a canonical target representation. We show that centroid averaging suppresses format-specific variation and can recover the semantic content common to different serializations when format-induced shifts differ across tables. Empirically, centroid representations outrank individual formats in aggregate pairwise comparisons across $\texttt{MPNet}$, $\texttt{BGE-M3}$, $\texttt{ReasonIR}$, and $\texttt{SPLADE}$. We further introduce a lightweight residual bottleneck adapter on top of a frozen encoder that maps single-serialization embeddings towards centroid targets while preserving variance and enforcing covariance regularization. The adapter improves robustness for several dense retrievers, though gains are model-dependent and weaker for sparse lexical retrieval. These results identify serialization sensitivity as a major source of retrieval variance and show the promise of post hoc geometric correction for serialization-invariant table retrieval. Our code, datasets, and models are available at $\href{https://github.com/KBhandari11/Centroid-Aligned-Table-Retrieval}{https://github.com/KBhandari11/Centroid-Aligned-Table-Retrieval}$.

</details>


### [62] [Distilling Self-Consistency into Verbal Confidence: A Pre-Registered Negative Result and Post-Hoc Rescue on Gemma 3 4B](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24070)
*Jon-Paul Cacioli*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper investigates why small instruct-tuned LLMs give overconfident, uninformative verbal confidence scores and tests a simple training method to align verbal confidence with internal uncertainty. Initial design fails, but an adjusted approach shows promising binary confidence readout and yields design lessons for future confidence training.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Small instruct-tuned LLMs tend to answer with very high apparent confidence almost all the time, regardless of whether they are correct, leading to poor Type-2 AUROC and invalid confidence–accuracy relationships. This mismatch between internal uncertainty signals (e.g., self-consistency across samples) and expressed verbal confidence limits safe and reliable deployment of LLMs. The authors aim to see if a lightweight supervised fine-tuning procedure can make verbal confidence better reflect the model’s internal information without extensive architectural changes or costly methods.

Method: They use a confidence-conditioned supervised fine-tuning (CSFT) scheme on Gemma 3 4B-it. First, they derive confidence targets from self-consistency over 10 sampled answers on calibration items. In a pre-registered Phase 0, they apply a ‘modal filter’ that restricts training to items where the model’s modal answer is correct. This inadvertently shrinks label entropy in the confidence targets. After this negative result, they run an exploratory variant removing the modal filter and train on all 2,000 calibration items, preserving more entropy in targets. They then evaluate: (1) Type-2 AUROC (AUROC2) for verbal correctness discrimination on held-out TriviaQA; (2) the quality of the compressed single-pass signal versus raw self-consistency and logit entropy; (3) a shuffled-target control to test for overfitting or spurious gains; and (4) effects on MMLU accuracy to see whether the learned targets impact task performance in a target-dependent way.

Result: With the modal filter (Phase 0), AUROC2 actually worsens from 0.554 to 0.509, attributed to label-entropy collapse in training targets. Removing the filter and training on all 2,000 calibration items yields a strong binary verbal correctness discriminator with AUROC2 = 0.774 on held-out TriviaQA. This single-pass confidence readout effectively compresses a 10-sample self-consistency signal (which has AUROC2 = 0.999) and outperforms logit entropy (AUROC2 = 0.701). A shuffled-target control fails to improve performance (AUROC2 = 0.501), showing the benefit depends on aligned targets. On MMLU, the model trained with real targets improves accuracy from 54.2% to 77.4%, whereas the shuffled-target model only reaches 56.1%, further supporting a target-dependent effect and arguing against a generic fine-tuning benefit.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that naive confidence training with overly filtered data can degrade metacognitive performance by collapsing label entropy. Effective confidence calibration needs sufficient entropy in the targets, and using correct (non-shuffled) self-consistency-derived targets not only sharpens binary correctness discrimination but also regularises the model’s output format and improves downstream accuracy. The results are exploratory, evaluated at a single small model scale and in binary rather than continuous calibration, but they highlight principled design lessons for future confidence training schemes in LLMs.

Abstract: Small instruct-tuned LLMs produce degenerate verbal confidence under minimal elicitation: ceiling rates above 95%, near-chance Type-2 AUROC, and Invalid validity profiles. We test whether confidence-conditioned supervised fine-tuning (CSFT) with self-consistency-derived targets can close the gap between internal information and verbal readout. A pre-registered Phase 0 protocol on Gemma 3 4B-it with a modal filter restricting training to items with correct modal answers produced a negative result: AUROC2 dropped from 0.554 to 0.509 due to label-entropy collapse in the training targets. An exploratory rescue removed the filter, training on all 2,000 calibration items. This produced a binary verbal correctness discriminator with AUROC2 = 0.774 on held-out TriviaQA, compressing a 10-sample self-consistency signal (AUROC2 = 0.999) into a single-pass readout exceeding logit entropy (0.701). The shuffled-target control showed no improvement (0.501). On MMLU, accuracy improved from 54.2% to 77.4% with the shuffled model at baseline (56.1%), supporting a target-dependent interpretation. The result is exploratory, binary rather than continuously calibrated, and observed at a single scale. It identifies two design lessons: confidence training requires label entropy, and correct targets regularise output format.

</details>


### [63] [PeeriScope: A Multi-Faceted Framework for Evaluating Peer Review Quality](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24071)
*Sajad Ebrahimi,Soroush Sadeghian,Ali Ghorbanpour,Negar Arabzadeh,Sara Salamat,Seyed Mohammad Hosseini,Hai Son Le,Mahdi Bashari,Ebrahim Bagheri*

  <summary>Details</summary>

They expose this functionality through both a web interface and a documented API, enabling integration with editorial systems and further research use.

Result: PeeriScope is implemented as a working system with a live demo and an accessible API. The system successfully integrates multiple evaluation signals into a unified, interpretable framework.

Conclusion: PeeriScope operationalizes multi-dimensional peer review quality assessment in an open, extensible, and practically deployable platform. By providing both user-facing and programmatic access, it supports immediate applications in editorial practice and creates a foundation for future research on methods to measure and improve scientific peer review quality.

Abstract: The increasing scale and variability of peer review in scholarly venues has created an urgent need for systematic, interpretable, and extensible tools to assess review quality. We present PeeriScope, a modular platform that integrates structured features, rubric-guided large language model assessments, and supervised prediction to evaluate peer review quality along multiple dimensions. Designed for openness and integration, PeeriScope provides both a public interface and a documented API, supporting practical deployment and research extensibility. The demonstration illustrates its use for reviewer self-assessment, editorial triage, and large-scale auditing, and it enables the continued development of quality evaluation methods within scientific peer review. PeeriScope is available both as a live demo at https://app.reviewer.ly/app/peeriscope and via API services at https://github.com/Reviewerly-Inc/Peeriscope.

### [64] [How Sensitive Are Safety Benchmarks to Judge Configuration Choices?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24074)
*Xinran Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper shows that how you prompt an LLM judge greatly changes measured safety, making current safety benchmarks less reliable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Safety benchmarks like HarmBench depend heavily on LLMs acting as judges of harmfulness, but the specific prompt used to instruct these judges is often treated as an unimportant technical detail. The authors suspect that judge configuration, especially prompt wording, may substantially affect measured safety and thus undermine the validity and stability of benchmark results, model rankings, and safety claims.

Method: The authors run a 2 x 2 x 3 factorial experiment to construct 12 different judge prompts that vary along two key axes—evaluation structure and instruction framing—as well as surface wording. They fix the judge model to Claude Sonnet 4.6 and use these 12 prompts to label responses from six target models on 400 HarmBench behaviors, yielding 28,812 judgments. They then analyze how harmful-response rates, model rankings, and category-level results change across prompts. Additionally, they perform a supplementary experiment using three different judge models to quantify added variance from judge-model choice.

Result: Changing only the judge prompt, while holding the judge model fixed, shifts estimated harmful-response rates by as much as 24.2 percentage points. Even minor surface rewordings within the same condition can alter harmfulness estimates by up to 20.1 percentage points. Model safety rankings are only moderately stable, with an average Kendall tau correlation of 0.89 across prompts, and sensitivity varies widely by behavior category—from 39.6 percentage points for copyright to 0 for harassment. Using multiple judge models introduces further variability on top of the prompt-induced variance.

Conclusion: LLM safety benchmarks that rely on LLM judges are significantly affected by the wording of the judge prompt. This prompt-induced variance is large enough to meaningfully alter measured harmfulness rates, model rankings, and category-level conclusions, and is further compounded by the choice of judge model. Therefore, judge configuration is not a minor implementation detail but a major methodological factor that must be explicitly considered, reported, and controlled when designing, interpreting, and comparing safety benchmarks.

Abstract: Safety benchmarks such as HarmBench rely on LLM judges to classify model responses as harmful or safe, yet the judge configuration, namely the combination of judge model and judge prompt, is typically treated as a fixed implementation detail. We show this assumption is problematic. Using a 2 x 2 x 3 factorial design, we construct 12 judge prompt variants along two axes, evaluation structure and instruction framing, and apply them using a single judge model, Claude Sonnet 4-6, producing 28,812 judgments over six target models and 400 HarmBench behaviors. We find that prompt wording alone, holding the judge model fixed, shifts measured harmful-response rates by up to 24.2 percentage points, with even within-condition surface rewording causing swings of up to 20.1 percentage points. Model safety rankings are moderately unstable, with mean Kendall tau = 0.89, and category-level sensitivity ranges from 39.6 percentage points for copyright to 0 percentage points for harassment. A supplementary multi-judge experiment using three judge models shows that judge-model choice adds further variance. Our results demonstrate that judge prompt wording is a substantial, previously under-examined source of measurement variance in safety benchmarking.

*Jisoo Yang,Jongwon Ryu,Minuk Ma,Trung X. Pham,Junyeong Kim*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

These methods miss deeper discourse structures and implicit conceptual links that actually maintain persona consistency in LLM-generated conversations. The authors want a more cognitively grounded, structurally rich way to uncover how personas are encoded in LLM behavior.

Method: They model LLM dialogue through the concept of bridging inference: implicit conceptual relations that connect utterances using shared world knowledge and discourse coherence. These relations are represented as structured knowledge graphs that encode how meaning is organized across turns. Using this graph representation, they analyze and extract persona traits at the discourse level rather than by token frequencies or styles. They apply this framework to multiple reasoning backbones and various LLMs (from small models to 80B parameters) and compare against frequency- and style-based baselines.

Result: Across different LLMs and reasoning setups, bridging-inference-based graphs provide significantly higher semantic coherence and more stable persona identification compared with traditional frequency- or style-based methods. The experiments show that their discourse-structural representation is better aligned with consistent persona traits.

Conclusion: Persona traits in LLMs are systematically reflected in the structural organization of discourse, not just in isolated words or stylistic markers. By leveraging bridging inference and knowledge graphs grounded in Cognitive Discourse Theory, their framework offers a systematic way to probe, extract, and visualize latent LLM personas, connecting ideas from computational linguistics, cognitive semantics, and persona reasoning. The released code supports further exploration and application of this discourse-level persona analysis.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) reveal inherent and distinctive personas through dialogue. However, most existing persona discovery approaches rely on surface-level lexical or stylistic cues, treating dialogue as a flat sequence of tokens and failing to capture the deeper discourse-level structures that sustain persona consistency. To address this limitation, we propose a novel analytical framework that interprets LLM dialogue through bridging inference -- implicit conceptual relations that connect utterances via shared world knowledge and discourse coherence. By modeling these relations as structured knowledge graphs, our approach captures latent semantic links that govern how LLMs organize meaning across turns, enabling persona discovery at the level of discourse coherence rather than surface realizations. Experimental results across multiple reasoning backbones and target LLMs, ranging from small-scale models to 80B-parameter systems, demonstrate that bridging-inference graphs yield significantly stronger semantic coherence and more stable persona identification than frequency or style-based baselines. These results show that persona traits are consistently encoded in the structural organization of discourse rather than isolated lexical patterns. This work presents a systematic framework for probing, extracting, and visualizing latent LLM personas through the lens of Cognitive Discourse Theory, bridging computational linguistics, cognitive semantics, and persona reasoning in large language models. Codes are available at https://github.com/JiSoo-Yang/Persona_Bridging.git

Abstract: Bridging molecular structures and natural language is essential for controllable design. Autoregressive models struggle with long-range dependencies, while standard diffusion processes apply uniform corruption across positions, which can distort structurally informative tokens. We present BiMol-Diff, a unified diffusion framework for the paired tasks of text-conditioned molecule generation and molecule captioning. Our key component is a token-aware noise schedule that assigns position-dependent corruption based on token recovery difficulty, preserving harder-to-recover substructures during the forward process. On ChEBI-20 and M3-20M, BiMol-Diff improves molecule reconstruction with a 15.4% relative gain in Exact Match and achieves strong captioning results, attaining best BLEU and BERTScore among compared baselines. These results indicate token-aware noising improves fidelity in molecular structure-language modelling.

### [67] [Factual and Edit-Sensitive Graph-to-Sequence Generation via Graph-Aware Adaptive Noising](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24104)
*Aditya Hemant Shahane,Anuj Kumar Sirohi,Tanmoy Chakraborty,Prathosh A P,Sandeep Kumar*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes DLM4G, a non-autoregressive diffusion language model for graph-to-sequence generation that adaptively noises entity/relation tokens, achieving better factual grounding and edit sensitivity than autoregressive and diffusion baselines, and generalizing to molecule captioning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing fine-tuned autoregressive models for graph-to-sequence generation often produce text that is not well grounded in the input graph and react poorly to small edits in the graph (low edit sensitivity). There is a need for models that both better preserve graph structure in generated text and can update outputs locally when the input graph is modified, while remaining competitive with much larger pretrained language models and LLMs.

Method: Introduce DLM4G, a non-autoregressive diffusion-based framework for text generation conditioned on an input graph. The method aligns graph components (entities and relations) with corresponding sequence tokens, then applies an adaptive noising strategy where the amount of noise per token is modulated using its denoising error. This allows the model to control noise more precisely on entity and relation tokens, enhancing structure preservation and enabling localized refinement under graph edits. The model is trained and evaluated on several G2S datasets, with both surface-form and embedding-based metrics, and extended to molecule captioning as a non-textual graph task.

Result: On three G2S datasets, DLM4G consistently outperforms strong diffusion-based G2S baselines trained on the same data splits, across surface-form and embedding-based metrics. It also surpasses fine-tuned autoregressive baselines up to 12x larger (e.g., T5-Large), and is competitive with zero-shot LLM transfer baselines up to 127x larger. Relative to the strongest fine-tuned PLM baseline, DLM4G improves factual grounding (FGT@0.5) by +5.16% and edit sensitivity (ESR) by +7.9%. Compared to the best diffusion baseline, it gains +3.75% in FGT@0.5 and +23.6% in ESR. The method also performs well on molecule captioning, demonstrating its broader applicability beyond textual graphs.

Conclusion: DLM4G shows that a non-autoregressive diffusion framework with adaptive, error-guided noising on aligned graph-token structures can substantially improve factual grounding and edit sensitivity in graph-to-sequence generation. It can match or outperform much larger autoregressive PLMs and diffusion baselines, and generalizes to non-textual graphs such as molecular structures, suggesting it is a general and scalable approach for scientific and structured G2S tasks.

Abstract: Fine-tuned autoregressive models for graph-to-sequence generation (G2S) often struggle with factual grounding and edit sensitivity. To tackle these issues, we propose a non-autoregressive diffusion framework that generates text by iterative refinement conditioned on an input graph, named as Diffusion Language Model for Graphs (DLM4G). By aligning graph components (entities/relations) with their corresponding sequence tokens, DLM4G employs an adaptive noising strategy. The proposed strategy uses per-token denoising error as a signal to adaptively modulate noise on entity and relation tokens, improving preservation of graph structure and enabling localized updates under graph edits. Evaluated on three datasets, DLM4G consistently outperforms competitive G2S diffusion baselines trained on identical splits across both surface-form and embedding-based metrics. DLM4G further exceeds fine-tuned autoregressive baselines up to 12x larger (e.g., T5-Large) and is competitive with zero-shot LLM transfer baselines up to 127x larger. Relative to the strongest fine-tuned PLM baseline, DLM4G improves factual grounding (FGT@0.5) by +5.16% and edit sensitivity (ESR) by +7.9%; compared to the best diffusion baseline, it yields gains of +3.75% in FGT@0.5 and +23.6% in ESR. We additionally demonstrate applicability beyond textual graphs through experiments on molecule captioning, indicating the method's generality for scientific G2S generation.

The IRIS framework proves especially beneficial for low-resource Indian languages and bilingual scenarios, while still offering benefits for high-resource languages. The newly released CL-Math dataset provides crucial infrastructure for further research on multilingual, step-annotated reasoning.

Abstract: Curriculum learning helps language models tackle complex reasoning by gradually increasing task difficulty. However, it often fails to generate consistent step-by-step reasoning, especially in multilingual and low-resource settings where cross-lingual transfer from English to Indian languages remains limited. We propose IRIS: Interleaved Reinforcement with Incremental Staged Curriculum, a two-axis framework that combines Supervised Fine-Tuning on progressively harder problems (vertical axis) with Reverse Curriculum Reinforcement Learning to reduce reliance on step-by-step guidance (horizontal axis). We design a composite reward combining correctness, step-wise alignment, continuity, and numeric incentives, optimized via Group Relative Policy Optimization (GRPO). We release CL-Math, a dataset of 29k problems with step-level annotations in English, Hindi, and Marathi. Across standard benchmarks and curated multilingual test sets, IRIS consistently improves performance, with strong results on math reasoning tasks and substantial gains in low-resource and bilingual settings, alongside modest improvements in high-resource languages.

### [70] [AdapTime: Enabling Adaptive Temporal Reasoning in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24175)

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: The authors propose AdapTime, an adaptive temporal reasoning method that operates on top of existing LLMs. It defines three temporal reasoning actions—reformulate, rewrite, and review—and uses an LLM-based planner to decide dynamically which actions to apply and in what order based on the input question and context. This creates a context-sensitive reasoning trajectory instead of a fixed pipeline, and it is designed to integrate directly with state-of-the-art LLMs without external tools.

It improves temporal QA performance without depending on external tools, demonstrating a more generalizable and efficient approach to temporal reasoning in LLMs.

Abstract: Large language models have demonstrated strong reasoning capabilities in general knowledge question answering. However, their ability to handle temporal information remains limited. To address this limitation, existing approaches often involve external tools or manual verification and are tailored to specific scenarios, leading to poor generalizability. Moreover, these methods apply a fixed pipeline to all questions, overlooking the fact that different types of temporal questions require distinct reasoning strategies, which leads to unnecessary processing for simple cases and inadequate reasoning for complex ones. To this end, we propose AdapTime, an adaptive temporal reasoning method that dynamically executes reasoning steps based on the input context. Specifically, it involves three temporal reasoning actions: reformulate, rewrite and review, with an LLM planner guiding the reasoning process. AdapTime integrates seamlessly with state-of-the-art LLMs and significantly enhances their temporal reasoning capabilities without relying on external support. Extensive experiments demonstrate the effectiveness of our approach.

### [71] [MemeScouts@LT-EDI 2026: Asking the Right Questions -- Prompted Weak Supervision for Meme Hate Speech Detection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24179)
*Ivo Bueno,Lea Hirlimann,Enkelejda Kasneci*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a prompted weak supervision framework that breaks meme hate-speech detection into multiple targeted VLM questions, outperforming direct end-to-end VLM classification in a multilingual shared task.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Hate speech in memes is hard to detect because it mixes images and text, often uses sarcasm, implicit cues, and culture-specific context, and must be handled across multiple languages. Existing end-to-end VLM prompting forces one model call to jointly infer target, stance, implicitness, and irony, which is brittle and unreliable, especially in multilingual settings. There is a need for a more structured, robust way to exploit VLMs for multimodal hate speech detection without depending on a single monolithic prediction.

Method: The authors introduce Prompted Weak Supervision (PWS) for homophobia and transphobia meme detection in the LT-EDI 2026 shared task. Instead of asking a VLM for a single final label, they design a set of targeted, question-based labeling functions, each with constrained answer options (e.g., about hate target, stance, presence of slurs, implicitness, irony). They use a quantized Qwen3-VLM to answer these questions over the meme (image + text), treating the answers as features or weak labels. They then iteratively refine the labeling functions based on observed errors and prune redundant features to improve generalization, feeding the resulting representations into a downstream classifier for final hate-speech prediction.

Result: Compared to direct VLM-based classification (end-to-end prompting), the PWS approach yields better performance overall, with especially strong improvements in Chinese and Hindi. In the LT-EDI 2026 shared task on homophobia and transphobia meme detection, their system ranked 1st for English, 2nd for Chinese, and 3rd for Hindi. Feature pruning and iterative LF refinement further reduced redundancy and improved robustness.

Conclusion: Decomposing multimodal hate speech detection into a set of structured, question-based VLM labeling functions is more effective than relying on a single end-to-end prompt. Prompted weak supervision leverages VLMs as modular feature/label generators, which enhances performance, especially in multilingual settings, and provides a practical path for scalable, robust multimodal hate speech detection across languages.

Abstract: Detecting hate speech in memes is challenging due to their multimodal nature and subtle, culturally grounded cues such as sarcasm and context. While recent vision-language models (VLMs) enable joint reasoning over text and images, end-to-end prompting can be brittle, as a single prediction must resolve target, stance, implicitness, and irony. These challenges are amplified in multilingual settings. We propose a prompted weak supervision (PWS) approach that decomposes meme understanding into targeted, question-based labeling functions with constrained answer options for homophobia and transphobia detection in the LT-EDI 2026 shared task. Using a quantized Qwen3-VLM to extract features by answering targeted questions, our method outperforms direct VLM classification, with substantial gains for Chinese and Hindi, ranking 1st in English, 2nd in Chinese, and 3rd in Hindi. Iterative refinement via error-driven LF expansion and feature pruning reduces redundancy and improves generalization. Our results highlight the effectiveness of prompted weak supervision for multilingual multimodal hate speech detection.

Existing approaches often rely on internal model knowledge or static knowledge bases, resulting in knowledge insufficiency and limited adaptability, which hinder their capacity to perform diagnostic reasoning. Moreover, these methods focus solely on the accuracy of final predictions, overlooking alignment with standard clinical reasoning trajectories. To this end, we propose MultiDx, a two-stage diagnostic reasoning framework that performs differential diagnosis by analyzing evidence collected from multiple knowledge sources. Specifically, it first generates suspected diagnoses and reasoning paths by leveraging knowledge from web search, SOAP-formatted case, and clinical case database. Then it integrates multi-perspective evidence through matching, voting, and differential diagnosis to generate the final prediction.~Extensive experiments on two public benchmarks demonstrate the effectiveness of our approach.

They then use this framework to derive policy and technical recommendations.

Result: They find that the most concerning risks do not stem from photorealism alone, but from the combination of several capabilities: realism plus accurate and readable text, persistent depiction of identities, rapid iteration, and integration into high-speed or high-reach distribution contexts. These converging capabilities enable more convincing fake documents, medical imagery, news photos, screenshots, and other assets that can be quickly tailored and spread. The analysis shows significant risk pathways in finance, healthcare, journalism, law, emergency response, identity verification, and public discourse more broadly.

Conclusion: The authors conclude that mitigating synthetic visual risk requires a layered control strategy rather than a single technical fix. They advocate for: (a) model-level restrictions and safety controls; (b) cryptographic provenance for authentic media; (c) visible labeling of synthetic content; (d) friction mechanisms on distribution platforms; (e) sector-specific verification standards in high-stakes domains; and (f) robust incident response processes. These capabilities create large benefits for design, education, accessibility, and communication, yet they also weaken one of society's most common trust shortcuts: the belief that a plausible picture is a reliable record. This paper provides a source-grounded technical and policy analysis of synthetic visual risk. We first summarize the public capabilities of recent image models, then analyze public incidents involving fake crisis images, celebrity and public-figure imagery, medical scans, forged-looking documents, synthetic screenshots, phishing assets, and market-moving rumors. We introduce a capability-weighted risk framework that links model affordances to real-world harm in finance, medicine, news, law, emergency response, identity verification, and civic discourse. Our findings show that risk is driven less by photorealism alone than by the convergence of realism, legible text, identity persistence, fast iteration, and distribution context. We argue for layered control: model-side restrictions, cryptographic provenance, visible labeling, platform friction, sector-grade verification, and incident response. The paper closes with practical recommendations for model providers, platforms, newsrooms, financial institutions, healthcare systems, legal organizations, regulators, and ordinary users.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing process reward models work well for static reasoning (e.g., math) but perform poorly for dynamic data analysis agents. They miss silent logical errors that don’t raise execution exceptions and wrongly penalize necessary exploratory behavior, limiting effective supervision of data-analysis LLM agents.

Method: The authors design DataPRM, an environment-aware generative PRM that can query intermediate execution states to detect silent errors and apply a reflection-aware ternary reward (correct, correctable grounding error, irrecoverable mistake). Result: DataPRM improves policy LLMs’ performance by 7.21% on ScienceAgentBench and 11.28% on DABStep under Best-of-N inference. Despite having only 4B parameters, it surpasses strong baselines and shows robust generalization to diverse test-time scaling strategies. When used in reinforcement learning, DataPRM-based process rewards significantly outperform outcome-only rewards, achieving 78.73% on DABench and 64.84% on TableBench.

Conclusion: Process reward models must be environment- and reflection-aware to supervise dynamic data analysis agents effectively. DataPRM, with its active verification and ternary reward design plus scalable training pipeline, provides more reliable step-level supervision, leading to substantial and generalizable gains over traditional PRMs and outcome-based rewards on data-analysis benchmarks.

Abstract: Process Reward Models (PRMs) have achieved remarkable success in augmenting the reasoning capabilities of Large Language Models (LLMs) within static domains such as mathematics. However, their potential in dynamic data analysis tasks remains underexplored. In this work, we first present a empirical study revealing that general-domain PRMs struggle to supervise data analysis agents. Specifically, they fail to detect silent errors, logical flaws that yield incorrect results without triggering interpreter exceptions, and erroneously penalize exploratory actions, mistaking necessary trial-and-error exploration for grounding failures. To bridge this gap, we introduce DataPRM, a novel environment-aware generative process reward model that (1) can serve as an active verifier, autonomously interacting with the environment to probe intermediate execution states and uncover silent errors, and (2) employs a reflection-aware ternary reward strategy that distinguishes between correctable grounding errors and irrecoverable mistakes. We design a scalable pipeline to construct over 8K high-quality training instances for DataPRM via diversity-driven trajectory generation and knowledge-augmented step-level annotation. Experimental results demonstrate that DataPRM improves downstream policy LLMs by 7.21% on ScienceAgentBench and 11.28% on DABStep using Best-of-N inference. Notably, with only 4B parameters, DataPRM outperforms strong baselines, and exhibits robust generalizability across diverse Test-Time Scaling strategies. Furthermore, integrating DataPRM into Reinforcement Learning yields substantial gains over outcome-reward baselines, achieving 78.73% on DABench and 64.84% on TableBench, validating the effectiveness of process reward supervision. Code is available at https://github.com/zjunlp/DataMind.

### [75] [Differentiable Faithfulness Alignment for Cross-Model Circuit Transfer](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24302)
*Shun Shao,Binxu Wang,Shay B. Cohen,Anna Korhonen,Yonatan Belinkov*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes Differentiable Faithfulness Alignment (DFA), a method to transfer mechanistic circuit information from smaller to larger language models via a learned differentiable alignment, improving scalability of mechanistic interpretability.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Mechanistic interpretability can localize circuits for specific behaviors in language models but current methods are costly, model-specific, and hard to scale to large architectures. The authors want a more scalable way to obtain circuit-level understanding of large models by reusing information from smaller, already-analyzed models.

Method: Introduce Differentiable Faithfulness Alignment (DFA), which learns a differentiable mapping from node importance scores in a smaller source model to corresponding nodes in a larger target model. The method projects source node importance into the target and trains this projection using a soft faithfulness objective, so that the mapped importance scores in the target correlate with the target model’s own behavior, thereby bypassing full circuit discovery in the target.

Result: DFA is evaluated on Llama-3 and Qwen-2.5 models across six tasks, including factual retrieval, multiple-choice reasoning, and arithmetic. The best performance is observed when transferring from Llama-3 1B to 3B: aligned circuits often match or closely approach direct node attribution, and even zero-shot transfer performs well. Performance degrades with increasing size and architectural differences between source and target, with substantially weaker recovery on Qwen-2.5.

Conclusion: DFA generally outperforms naive baselines and can, in some scenarios, recover target-model circuits with faithfulness similar to or better than direct attribution. This shows that smaller models can serve as useful mechanistic priors for larger ones, while also demonstrating that significant architectural or scaling gaps limit the effectiveness of cross-model node-level circuit transfer.

Abstract: Mechanistic interpretability has made it possible to localize circuits underlying specific behaviors in language models, but existing methods are expensive, model-specific, and difficult to scale to larger architectures. We introduce \textbf{Differentiable Faithfulness Alignment (DFA)}, a framework that transfers circuit information from a smaller source model to a larger target model through a learned differentiable alignment. DFA projects source-model node importance scores into the target model and trains this mapping with a soft faithfulness objective, avoiding full circuit discovery on the target model. We evaluate DFA on Llama-3 and Qwen-2.5 across six tasks spanning factual retrieval, multiple-choice reasoning, and arithmetic. The strongest results occur on Llama-3 $1$B$\rightarrow3$B, where aligned circuits are often competitive with direct node attribution and zero-shot transfer remains effective. Recovery weakens for larger source--target gaps and is substantially lower on Qwen-2.5, suggesting that transfer becomes harder as architectural and scaling differences increase. Overall, DFA consistently outperforms simple baselines and, in some settings, recovers target-model circuits with faithfulness comparable to or stronger than direct attribution. These results suggest that smaller models can provide useful mechanistic priors for larger ones, while highlighting both the promise and the limits of node-level cross-model circuit alignment.\footnote{Code is available at https://github.com/jasonshaoshun/dfa-circuits.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

This limits exploration and leads to incomplete understanding of complex environments, which is problematic for tasks that require broad, diverse experience. The authors want to overcome these exploration and coverage limitations while preserving efficiency.

Method: They propose a new paradigm where an agent interacts with multiple environments simultaneously and shares cross-trajectory experience. On top of this, they develop DPEPO, an RL algorithm with two stages: (1) supervised fine-tuning to teach basic parallel reasoning and action generation; (2) a reinforcement learning phase with a hierarchical reward design. The rewards include a trajectory-level success reward plus two step-level rewards: a Diverse Action Reward and a Diverse State Transition Reward that penalize redundant behavior across parallel trajectories and encourage wide, varied exploration of states and actions.

Result: On the ALFWorld and ScienceWorld benchmarks, DPEPO achieves state-of-the-art success rates compared with prior LLM agents, while keeping efficiency roughly on par with strong sequential baselines. This shows that diverse parallel exploration can improve task performance without significant extra computational cost per step.

Conclusion: Allowing LLM agents to interact with multiple environments in parallel, combined with a hierarchical diversity-oriented reward structure, leads to better exploration and higher success rates on complex interactive tasks. DPEPO demonstrates that parallel, diversity-aware reinforcement learning can surpass traditional sequential "reason-then-act" agents while maintaining similar efficiency, indicating a promising direction for future LLM-based agent design.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents that follow the sequential "reason-then-act" paradigm have achieved superior performance in many complex tasks.However, these methods suffer from limited exploration and incomplete environmental understanding, as they interact with only a single environment per step. In this paper, we first introduce a novel paradigm that enables an agent to interact with multiple environments simultaneously and share cross-trajectory experiences. Building upon this paradigm, we further propose DPEPO, a reinforcement learning (RL) algorithm that encourages the agent to perform diverse parallel exploration. There are two stages in DPEPO: initial supervised fine-tuning (SFT) imparts basic parallel reasoning and action generation, followed by reinforcement learning stage with a hierarchical reward scheme. We design a parallel trajectory-level success reward and two step-level rewards: Diverse Action Reward and Diverse State Transition Reward, which actively penalize behavioral redundancy and promote broad exploration. Extensive experiments on ALFWorld and ScienceWorld show that DPEPO achieves state-of-the-art (SOTA) success rates, while maintaining comparable efficiency to strong sequential baselines. (Code is available at https://github.com/LePanda026/Code-for-DPEPO)

Method: The authors propose a framework (\model) that attaches an explicit natural-language strategy description to each candidate program and uses it as a first-class state in evolution. It has three main components: (1) Strategy Articulation, which structures mutation as diagnose-direct-implement guided by the strategy text; (2) Stratified Experience Retrieval, which clusters archived candidates by strategy and selects inspirations based on behavioral complementarity; and (3) Strategic Landscape Navigation, which periodically summarizes which strategy families are effective, saturated, or underexplored to steer future mutations.

Result: On benchmarks for mathematical algorithm discovery, systems optimization, and agent scaffolding, the proposed method improves performance over baseline LLM-driven evolutionary backbones in most settings, with particularly notable gains (21% relative improvement) on open-ended systems optimization tasks.

Conclusion: Making strategy descriptions persistent and population-level provides a practical way to improve robustness and efficiency in LLM-guided evolutionary search. This approach enables compound AI systems that can explicitly represent, organize, and accumulate algorithmic strategies over time, leading toward more systematic algorithm discovery.

Abstract: LLM-guided evolutionary search has emerged as a promising paradigm for automated algorithm discovery, yet most systems track search progress primarily through executable programs and scalar fitness. Even when natural-language reflection is used, it is often used locally in mutation prompts or stored without an explicit population-level organization of strategic directions. As a result, evolutionary search can struggle to distinguish syntactically different implementations of the same idea, preserve lower-fitness but strategically promising directions, or detect when an entire family of strategies has saturated.
  We introduce \model, a modular strategy-space layer that elevates natural-language strategy descriptions from transient prompt context to first-class population-level evolutionary state in LLM-driven program search. \model augments each candidate program with an explicit natural language strategy description and uses this representation in three ways: Strategy Articulation turns mutation into a diagnose-direct-implement process; Stratified Experience Retrieval organizes the archive into strategy clusters and selects inspirations by behavioral complementarity; and Strategic Landscape Navigation periodically summarizes effective, saturated, and underexplored strategy families to guide future mutations. Across mathematical algorithm discovery, systems optimization, and agent-scaffold benchmarks, \model improves the underlying evolutionary backbones in most settings, with particularly large gains (21% relative improvement) on open-ended system optimization tasks. These results suggest that persistent strategy representations provide a practical mechanism for improving the robustness and efficiency of LLM-guided evolutionary search, suggesting a path toward compound AI systems that accumulate algorithmic knowledge over time.

### [81] [MIPIC: Matryoshka Representation Learning via Self-Distilled Intra-Relational and Progressive Information Chaining](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24374)
*Phung Gia Huy,Hai An Vu,Minh-Phuc Truong,Thang Duc Tran,Linh Ngo Van,Thanh Hong Nguyen,Trung Le*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MIPIC, a training framework that creates Matryoshka (nested, budget-flexible) text embeddings that perform well across a wide range of dimensions and model depths.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard representation learning produces one fixed-size embedding, which is inefficient when applications need to trade off quality vs. computational budget. Matryoshka Representation Learning (MRL) allows nested embeddings that can be truncated for cheaper inference, but it is hard to ensure that information is well-organized across dimensions and layers so that all budget levels perform well. The paper aims to systematically learn such coherent, multi-capacity representations.

Method: They propose MIPIC, a unified training framework with two main components. (1) Self-Distilled Intra-Relational Alignment (SIA): aligns token-level relational structure (geometry and attention relations) between full embeddings and their truncated (lower-dimensional) counterparts using a top-k CKA-based self-distillation objective. This enforces structural consistency across embedding dimensions. (2) Progressive Information Chaining (PIC): a depth-wise, scaffolded alignment mechanism that progressively transfers task-specific semantics from deeper (more mature) layers to earlier layers, encouraging shallower layers to encode richer semantic information. Together, these mechanisms train Matryoshka representations that are both structurally and semantically coherent across depths and sizes.

Result: Across a variety of benchmarks—semantic textual similarity (STS), natural language inference (NLI), and text classification—and across multiple backbone models from small (TinyBERT) to large (BGEM3, Qwen3), MIPIC-trained models produce Matryoshka embeddings that remain strong at all capacity levels. The gains are particularly pronounced in extreme low-dimensional settings, where truncated embeddings from MIPIC significantly outperform prior approaches.

Conclusion: MIPIC provides an effective, general training recipe for Matryoshka Representation Learning, yielding nested embeddings that are structurally consistent and semantically strong across dimensions and network depths. This makes it easier to deploy a single model that can adapt to different computational budgets without large performance drops, especially in very compact embedding regimes.

Abstract: Representation learning is fundamental to NLP, but building embeddings that work well at different computational budgets is challenging. Matryoshka Representation Learning (MRL) offers a flexible inference paradigm through nested embeddings; however, learning such structures requires explicit coordination of how information is arranged across embedding dimensionality and model depth. In this work, we propose MIPIC (Matryoshka Representation Learning via Self-Distilled Intra-Relational Alignment and Progressive Information Chaining), a unified training framework designed to produce structurally coherent and semantically compact Matryoshka representations. MIPIC promotes cross-dimensional structural consistency through Self-Distilled Intra-Relational Alignment (SIA), which aligns token-level geometric and attention-driven relations between full and truncated representations using top-k CKA self-distillation. Complementarily, it enables depth-wise semantic consolidation via Progressive Information Chaining (PIC), a scaffolded alignment strategy that incrementally transfers mature task semantics from deeper layers into earlier layers. Extensive experiments on STS, NLI, and classification benchmarks (spanning models from TinyBERT to BGEM3, Qwen3) demonstrate that MIPIC yields Matryoshka representations that are highly competitive across all capacities, with significant performance advantages observed under extreme low-dimensional.

</details>


### [82] [Learning Evidence of Depression Symptoms via Prompt Induction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24376)
*Eliseo Bao,Anxo Perez,David Otero,Javier Parapar*

Main category: cs.CL

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Automatically detecting clinical symptom evidence in this user-generated text could support mental health monitoring and triage at scale. However, the task of labeling fine-grained depression symptoms (based on the 21 BDI-II items) is challenging: the labels are numerous, data is highly imbalanced, and common LLM techniques (zero-shot, in-context learning, fine-tuning) struggle to apply consistent, clinically meaningful criteria for each symptom. A more robust, interpretable way to guide LLMs in recognizing specific symptoms is needed.

Method: The authors use BDI-Sen, a sentence-level dataset annotated for relevance to 21 depression symptoms from the BDI-II questionnaire. They first evaluate common LLM approaches—zero-shot prompting, in-context learning, and fine-tuning—on this multi-label, highly imbalanced classification task and find substantial inconsistencies, especially for rare symptoms. They then propose Symptom Induction (SI), a two-stage framework: (1) compress labeled examples into concise, interpretable guidelines for each symptom, effectively specifying what counts as evidence; and (2) condition LLM-based classification on these induced guidelines when determining whether a sentence is evidence for a given symptom. They test SI across four LLM families and eight models, and also perform cross-domain evaluation on an external dataset with bipolar and eating disorder texts that share overlapping symptomatology with depression.

Result: Across the four LLM families and eight tested models, Symptom Induction yields the highest overall weighted F1 score on the BDI-Sen dataset, outperforming zero-shot, in-context, and fine-tuning baselines. The gains are particularly pronounced for infrequent symptoms, where traditional approaches suffer most from data imbalance and inconsistent criteria. In cross-domain experiments on an external dataset containing bipolar and eating disorder content, the guidelines induced from depression data still provide strong performance, indicating that the induced symptom definitions transfer to related mental health conditions with overlapping symptoms.

Conclusion: Symptom Induction offers an effective, interpretable way to guide LLMs in fine-grained clinical symptom classification in user-generated text, overcoming limitations of standard prompting and fine-tuning strategies on highly imbalanced, multi-symptom tasks. By compressing training examples into symptom-specific guidelines and using them to condition classification, SI improves performance—especially on rare symptoms—and the induced guidelines generalize to other disorders that share symptomatology. This suggests SI can help scale symptom-level mental health monitoring across large, noisy text corpora while maintaining clearer, clinically grounded decision criteria.

Abstract: Depression places substantial pressure on mental health services, and many people describe their experiences outside clinical settings in high-volume user-generated text (e.g., online forums and social media). Automatically identifying clinical symptom evidence in such text can therefore complement limited clinical capacity and scale to large populations. We address this need through sentence-level classification of 21 depression symptoms from the BDI-II questionnaire, using BDI-Sen, a dataset annotated for symptom relevance. This task is fine-grained and highly imbalanced, and we find that common LLM approaches (zero-shot, in-context learning, and fine-tuning) struggle to apply consistent relevance criteria for most symptoms. We propose Symptom Induction (SI), a novel approach which compresses labeled examples into short, interpretable guidelines that specify what counts as evidence for each symptom and uses these guidelines to condition classification. Across four LLM families and eight models, SI achieves the best overall weighted F1 on BDI-Sen, with especially large gains for infrequent symptoms. Cross-domain evaluation on an external dataset further shows that induced guidelines generalize across other diseases shared symptomatology (bipolar and eating disorders).

### [83] [Structural Pruning of Large Vision Language Models: A Comprehensive Study on Pruning Dynamics, Recovery, and Data Efficiency](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24380)
*Yiran Huang,Lukas Thede,Massimiliano Mancini,Wenjia Xu,Zeynep Akata*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper explores compressing existing Large Vision Language Models via structured pruning plus light recovery training, enabling deployment on resource-limited edge devices while retaining most performance with far less compute and data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LVLMs are powerful but too computationally and memory intensive for edge devices. Existing parameter reduction methods mostly train LVLMs from small LMs, which is inflexible and still expensive. There is a need for a practical way to shrink already-trained LVLMs using limited compute and data, while maintaining high performance.

Method: The authors compress existing LVLMs by applying structured pruning to the language model backbone, then performing lightweight recovery training. They systematically study two pruning strategies: layerwise pruning (removing entire layers) and widthwise pruning (reducing layer widths, e.g., neurons/heads). For recovery, they compare supervised finetuning and knowledge distillation (on logits and hidden states), including combinations of the two. They also explore how effective recovery is when only a small portion of the original training data is available. Experiments span three LVLM families from 3B–7B parameters.

Result: Widthwise pruning consistently outperforms layerwise pruning in low-resource settings, preserving better performance when compute or finetuning data are limited. For modest compression ratios, finetuning only the multimodal projector is enough for effective recovery. Combining supervised finetuning with hidden-state distillation gives the best recovery across pruning levels. Remarkably, fine-tuning on just 5% of the original data can recover more than 95% of the unpruned model’s performance.

Conclusion: Structured pruning plus light recovery training is a practical, effective way to compress existing LVLMs for edge deployment without retraining from scratch or requiring full datasets. Widthwise pruning is preferable in constrained regimes; small, projector-only finetuning can suffice for mild compression; and blended supervised finetuning with hidden-state distillation yields strong recovery, even with only 5% of the original data. These findings provide concrete guidelines for practitioners to downsize LVLMs under compute and data constraints.

Abstract: While Large Vision Language Models (LVLMs) demonstrate impressive capabilities, their substantial computational and memory requirements pose deployment challenges on resource-constrained edge devices. Current parameter reduction techniques primarily involve training LVLMs from small language models, but these methods offer limited flexibility and remain computationally intensive. We study a complementary route: compressing existing LVLMs by applying structured pruning to the language model backbone, followed by lightweight recovery training. Specifically, we investigate two structural pruning paradigms: layerwise and widthwise pruning, and pair them with supervised finetuning and knowledge distillation on logits and hidden states. Additionally, we assess the feasibility of conducting recovery training with only a small fraction of the available data. Our results show that widthwise pruning generally maintains better performance in low-resource scenarios, where computational resources are limited or there is insufficient finetuning data. As for the recovery training, finetuning only the multimodal projector is sufficient at small compression levels. Furthermore, a combination of supervised finetuning and hidden-state distillation yields optimal recovery across various pruning levels. Notably, effective recovery can be achieved using just 5% of the original data, while retaining over 95% of the original performance. Through empirical study on three representative LVLM families ranging from 3B to 7B parameters, this study offers actionable insights for practitioners to compress LVLMs without extensive computation resources or sufficient data. The code base is available at https://github.com/YiranHuangIrene/VLMCompression.git.

</details>


### [84] [A Multi-Dimensional Audit of Politically Aligned Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24429)
*Lisa Korver,Mohamed Mostagir,Sherief Reda*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper proposes a framework to quantitatively audit politically aligned LLMs along four dimensions (effectiveness, fairness, truthfulness, persuasiveness), revealing systematic trade-offs and deficiencies across popular models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs are increasingly deployed, some are deliberately aligned with specific political ideologies (e.g., for campaigns), raising risks of bias, misinformation, degraded reasoning, and harmful rhetoric. There is a lack of systematic, quantitative methods to audit such politically aligned models for safety and reliability. The authors are motivated to provide a principled, multidimensional evaluation framework to assess whether these models can argue politically while remaining legitimate and harmless.

Method: The authors design an automated, quantitative auditing framework grounded in Habermas' Theory of Communicative Action, operationalizing four dimensions: effectiveness (how well a model plays a political role), fairness (bias, toxicity, and equitable treatment of different ideologies), truthfulness (factual accuracy, hallucinations), and persuasiveness (ability to convince audiences). They apply this framework to nine popular LLMs that are politically aligned either via fine-tuning or via prompt-based role-playing, and measure performance on these metrics, comparing sizes and alignment methods.

Result: The evaluation shows consistent trade-offs across models. Larger LLMs better follow political role instructions and produce more factually correct answers, but they also show reduced fairness, including more biased, angry, or toxic language toward opposing ideologies. Fine-tuned models align more effectively and exhibit lower measured bias than prompt-based role-playing models, but their general reasoning performance drops and their hallucination rates increase. Every examined model is deficient in at least one of the four dimensions.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that current politically aligned LLMs cannot yet deliver simultaneously high effectiveness, fairness, truthfulness, and persuasiveness. Alignment choices (model size, fine-tuning vs. role-play) introduce systematic trade-offs between bias, factuality, and reasoning. Therefore, more balanced and robust alignment strategies are needed, and the proposed Habermas-inspired framework provides a practical tool to evaluate and guide the responsible political alignment of LLMs to ensure that their generated arguments are legitimate and harmless.

Abstract: As the application of Large Language Models (LLMs) spreads across various industries, there are increasing concerns about the potential for their misuse, especially in sensitive areas such as political discourse. Deliberately aligning LLMs with specific political ideologies, through prompt engineering or fine-tuning techniques, can be advantageous in use cases such as political campaigns, but requires careful consideration due to heightened risks of performance degradation, misinformation, or increased biased behavior. In this work, we propose a multi-dimensional framework inspired by Habermas' Theory of Communicative Action to audit politically aligned language models across four dimensions: effectiveness, fairness, truthfulness, and persuasiveness using automated, quantitative metrics. Applying this to nine popular LLMs aligned via fine-tuning or role-playing revealed consistent trade-offs: while larger models tend to be more effective at role-playing political ideologies and truthful in their responses, they were also less fair, exhibiting higher levels of bias in the form of angry and toxic language towards people of different ideologies. Fine-tuned models exhibited lower bias and more effective alignment than the corresponding role-playing models, but also saw a decline in performance reasoning tasks and an increase in hallucinations. Overall, all of the models tested exhibited some deficiency in at least one of the four metrics, highlighting the need for more balanced and robust alignment strategies. Ultimately, this work aims to ensure politically-aligned LLMs generate legitimate, harmless arguments, offering a framework to evaluate the responsible political alignment of these models.

### [85] [Kwai Summary Attention Technical Report](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24432)
*Chenglong Chu,Guorui Zhou,Guowang Zhang,Han Li,Hao Peng,Hongtao Cheng,Jian Liang,Jiangxia Cao,Kun Gai,Lingzhi Zhou,Lu Ren,Qi Zhang,Ruiming Tang,Ruitao Wang,Xinchen Luo,Yi Su,Zhiyuan Liang,Ziqi Wang,Boyang Ding,Chengru Song,Dunju Zang,Hui Wang,Jiao Ou,Jiaxin Deng,Jijun Shi,Jinghao Zhang,Junmin Chen,Lejian Ren,Minxuan Lv,Qianqian Wang,Qigen Hu,Shiyao Wang,Siyang Mao,Tao Wang,Xingmei Wang,Zhixin Ling,Ziming Li,Zixing Zhang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose Kwai Summary Attention (KSA), an attention mechanism that compresses long histories into summary tokens to keep KV cache linear but cheaper while preserving long-range dependencies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context LLMs are important for tasks like semantic reasoning, code agents, and recommendation, but standard softmax attention is quadratic in sequence length and existing solutions either only mildly reduce KV cache or sacrifice long-context modeling. There is a need for a better trade-off that keeps linear KV scaling while compressing information semantically.

Method: They introduce an intermediate approach where the KV cache remains linearly related to sequence length but is semantically compressed by a ratio k using learnable summary tokens. KSA compresses historical context into these summary tokens, which are then used in attention instead of all past tokens, aiming at O(n/k) cost reduction while retaining interpretability and long-distance information.

Result: KSA reduces sequence modeling cost by using summary tokens in place of full historical context, leading to more efficient long-context handling while maintaining long-range dependency information. (Details like benchmarks and exact gains are not in the abstract but implied improvements in training/inference efficiency and context retention.)

Conclusion: Semantic-level compression via learnable summary tokens offers a promising middle ground between minimal KV cache and strong long-context modeling. KSA demonstrates that O(n/k) summary-based attention can reduce memory and computation while keeping long-distance dependencies complete, referential, and interpretable.

Abstract: Long-context ability, has become one of the most important iteration direction of next-generation Large Language Models, particularly in semantic understanding/reasoning, code agentic intelligence and recommendation system. However, the standard softmax attention exhibits quadratic time complexity with respect to sequence length. As the sequence length increases, this incurs substantial overhead in long-context settings, leading the training and inference costs of extremely long sequences deteriorate rapidly. Existing solutions mitigate this issue through two technique routings: i) Reducing the KV cache per layer, such as from the head-level compression GQA, and the embedding dimension-level compression MLA, but the KV cache remains linearly dependent on the sequence length at a 1:1 ratio. ii) Interleaving with KV Cache friendly architecture, such as local attention SWA, linear kernel GDN, but often involve trade-offs among KV Cache and long-context modeling effectiveness. Besides the two technique routings, we argue that there exists an intermediate path not well explored: {Maintaining a linear relationship between the KV cache and sequence length, but performing semantic-level compression through a specific ratio $k$}. This $O(n/k)$ path does not pursue a ``minimum KV cache'', but rather trades acceptable memory costs for complete, referential, and interpretable retention of long distant dependency. Motivated by this, we propose Kwai Summary Attention (KSA), a novel attention mechanism that reduces sequence modeling cost by compressing historical contexts into learnable summary tokens.

</details>

*Connor Baumler,Calvin Bao,Huy Nghiem,Xinchen Yang,Marine Carpuat,Hal Daumé*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Study of whether people can make LLM-generated drafts match their personal writing style by post-editing, and how that style compares to pure human vs pure LLM text.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are widely used for drafting, but when personal style matters (e.g., statements, essays, emails), people may mistrust or underuse them. Post-editing is a natural compromise, yet it is unknown how effectively users can bend LLM text toward their authentic style and how this looks under quantitative style metrics and subjective perception.

Method: Pre-registered online experiment with 81 participants who completed writing tasks where personal style was important to them. Each participant produced unassisted writing and also post-edited LLM-generated drafts. The authors then applied embedding-based style similarity metrics to compare (1) post-edited texts, (2) unassisted human texts, and (3) fully LLM-generated texts. They also collected participants’ subjective judgments of stylistic authenticity.

Result: Post-editing makes the text more stylistically similar to participants’ unassisted writing and less similar to fully LLM-generated output. Yet, even after editing, the text is still closer in style to LLM-generated text than to the participants’ pure human writing and shows lower stylistic diversity. Participants often perceive post-edited text as strongly representative of their own style, despite quantitative measures indicating residual LLM-style traces.

Conclusion: Post-editing LLM drafts helps move the text toward a user’s personal style but does not fully erase LLM stylistic signatures and tends to compress stylistic diversity. There is also a notable disconnect between subjective perceptions of authenticity and objective style metrics, implying that users may overestimate how much they have imprinted their own style onto LLM-assisted writing.

Abstract: Despite the growing use of large language models (LLMs) for writing tasks, users may hesitate to rely on LLMs when personal style is important. Post-editing LLM-generated drafts or translations is a common collaborative writing strategy, but it remains unclear whether users can effectively reshape LLM-generated text to reflect their personal style. We conduct a pre-registered online study ($n=81$) in which participants post-edit LLM-generated drafts for writing tasks where personal style matters to them. Using embedding-based style similarity metrics, we find that post-editing increases stylistic similarity to participants' unassisted writing and reduces similarity to fully LLM-generated output. However, post-edited text still remains stylistically closer in style to LLM text than to participants' unassisted control text, and it exhibits reduced stylistic diversity compared to unassisted human text. We find a gap between perceived stylistic authenticity and model-measured stylistic similarity, with post-edited text often perceived as representative of participants' personal style despite remaining detectable LLM stylistic traces.

In this paper, we propose a new zero-shot prompting methodology for ARA and present the first comprehensive evaluation of using large language models (LLMs) as an unsupervised ARA method by testing 10 diverse open-source LLMs (e.g., different sizes and developers) on 14 diverse datasets (e.g., different text lengths and languages). Our findings show that our proposed prompting methodology outperforms prior methods on 13 of the 14 datasets. Furthermore, we propose LAURAE, which combines LLM and readability formula scores to improve robustness by capturing both contextual and shallow (e.g., sentence length) features of readability.
