### [88] [SEARCH-R: Structured Entity-Aware Retrieval with Chain-of-Reasoning Navigator for Multi-hop Question Answering](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24515)
*Yuqing Fu,Yimin Deng,Wanyu Wang,Yuhao Wang,Yejing Wang,Hongshi Liu,Yiqi Wang,Xiao Han,Maolin Wang,Guoshuai Zhao,Yi Chang,Xiangyu Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: SEARCH-R is a framework for multi-hop question answering that jointly learns to decompose questions into reasoning steps and to retrieve documents based on their true informational contribution rather than just similarity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multi-hop question answering requires models to reason through multiple intermediate steps and to gather several supporting pieces of evidence. Existing LLM-based methods typically generate reasoning paths via prompting, but this generation is weakly controlled and can drift off-topic. At the same time, standard sparse/dense retrieval mainly depends on lexical or semantic similarity, often returning redundant or unhelpful passages that do not actually advance the reasoning. The paper is motivated by the need for: (1) a more controllable, trainable navigator of multi-step reasoning; and (2) a retrieval mechanism that ranks documents by how much they help the reasoning process, not just by surface similarity.

Method: The authors propose SEARCH-R, a Structured Entity-Aware Retrieval with Chain-of-Reasoning Navigator framework. First, they train an end-to-end reasoning path navigator by fine-tuning the Llama3.1-8B model to decompose a multi-hop question into a sequence of sub-questions or reasoning steps; this acts as a powerful sub-question decomposer. Second, they introduce a dependency tree-based retrieval mechanism that quantitatively assesses each document’s informational contribution. This structured, entity-aware retrieval uses dependency structures to better capture which passages add new, useful entities or relations relevant to the evolving reasoning chain, rather than just high similarity scores. The system integrates the navigator and the retrieval into a unified pipeline for MHQA.

Result: On three challenging multi-hop QA benchmarks, SEARCH-R outperforms existing baselines. The experiments show that the trained reasoning path navigator generates more accurate and useful sub-questions than prompt-only baselines, while the dependency tree-based retrieval reduces retrieval of homogeneous or non-informative evidence. Overall, the framework yields better answer accuracy and more reliable reasoning chains compared with standard sparse/dense retrieval plus prompt-based CoT methods.

Conclusion: SEARCH-R demonstrates that explicitly training a reasoning path navigator and coupling it with structured, entity-aware retrieval leads to more effective multi-hop question answering. By evaluating documents according to their incremental informational contribution, rather than simple similarity, the system retrieves more diverse and relevant evidence. The integration of fine-tuned Llama3.1-8B for sub-question decomposition and dependency tree-based retrieval results in consistent performance gains on multiple MHQA datasets, suggesting a promising direction for controllable reasoning and utility-based retrieval in complex QA tasks.

Abstract: Multi-hop Question Answering (MHQA) aims to answer questions that require multi-step reasoning. It presents two key challenges: generating correct reasoning paths in response to the complex user queries, and accurately retrieving essential knowledge in the face of potential limitations in large language models (LLMs). Existing approaches primarily rely on prompt-based methods to generate reasoning paths, which are further combined with traditional sparse or dense retrieval to produce the final answer. However, the generation of reasoning paths commonly lacks effective control over the generative process, thus leading the reasoning astray. Meanwhile, the retrieval methods over-rely on knowledge matching or similarity scores rather than evaluating the practical utility of the information, resulting in retrieving homogeneous or non-useful information. Therefore, we propose a Structured Entity-Aware Retrieval with Chain-of-Reasoning Navigator framework named SEARCH-R. Specifically, SEARCH-R trains an end-to-end reasoning path navigator, which is able to provide a powerful sub-question decomposer by fine-tuning the Llama3.1-8B model. Moreover, a novel dependency tree-based retrieval is designed to evaluate the informational contribution of the document quantitatively. Extensive experiments on three challenging multi-hop datasets validate the effectiveness of the proposed framework. The code and dataset are available at: https://github.com/Applied-Machine-Learning-Lab/ACL2026_SEARCH-R.

</details>

(5) Train two smaller foundation models with margin-based preference alignment using this dataset, eliminating the need for explicit empathy scoring at inference.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) excel academically but struggle with social intelligence tasks, such as creating good compromises. In this paper, we present methods for generating empathically neutral compromises between two opposing viewpoints. We first compared four different prompt engineering methods using Claude 3 Opus and a dataset of 2,400 contrasting views on shared places. A subset of the gen erated compromises was evaluated for acceptability in a 50-participant study. We found that the best method for generating compromises between two views used external empathic similarity between a compromise and each viewpoint as iterative feedback, outperforming stan dard Chain of Thought (CoT) reasoning. The results indicate that the use of empathic neutrality improves the acceptability of compromises. The dataset of generated compromises was then used to train two smaller foundation models via margin-based alignment of human preferences, improving efficiency and removing the need for empathy estimation during inference.

### [91] [MEG-RAG: Quantifying Multi-modal Evidence Grounding for Evidence Selection in RAG](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24564)
*Xihang Wang,Zihan Wang,Chengkai Huang,Quan Z. Sheng,Lina Yao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces MEG and MEG-RAG, a semantic-aware evidence grounding metric and framework that improve how multimodal RAG systems select and align retrieved evidence with the true semantic core of answers, leading to better accuracy and multimodal consistency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Multimodal RAG systems suffer from hallucinations and failure to properly leverage retrieved multimodal data because they cannot tell whether a piece of evidence actually supports the semantic core of an answer or is only superficially related. Existing metrics mainly use heuristic, position-based confidence, which does not reflect how information-dense or semantically central a multimodal entity is. The authors aim to provide a more principled way to measure and use the true contribution of retrieved evidence.

Method: The authors propose Multi-modal Evidence Grounding (MEG), a semantic-aware metric that measures the contribution of retrieved evidence to an answer. MEG uses Semantic Certainty Anchoring by identifying high-IDF, information-bearing tokens in the answer, treating them as semantic anchors that represent the core content. It then evaluates how well retrieved multimodal evidence supports these anchors. On top of this metric, they design MEG-RAG, a training framework for a multimodal reranker. The reranker is trained to align retrieved evidence with the semantic anchors of the ground-truth answer, prioritizing high-value content based on semantic grounding rather than token-level probability or positional heuristics.

Result: On the M^2RAG benchmark, MEG-RAG surpasses strong baseline methods in performance metrics related to accuracy and multimodal consistency. The framework also shows solid generalization when using different teacher models, indicating that the improvements are not narrowly tied to a single backbone model.

Conclusion: Semantic-aware evidence grounding via MEG provides a more accurate way to quantify the usefulness of retrieved multimodal evidence, and training a reranker with this signal (MEG-RAG) leads to more accurate and better-grounded multimodal RAG outputs. The approach is robust across different teacher models, suggesting it is a generally applicable enhancement for MRAG systems.

Abstract: Multimodal Retrieval-Augmented Generation (MRAG) addresses key limitations of Multimodal Large Language Models (MLLMs), such as hallucination and outdated knowledge. However, current MRAG systems struggle to distinguish whether retrieved multimodal data truly supports the semantic core of an answer or merely provides superficial relevance. Existing metrics often rely on heuristic position-based confidence, which fails to capture the informational density of multimodal entities. To address this, we propose Multi-modal Evidence Grounding (MEG), a semantic-aware metric that quantifies the contribution of retrieved evidence. Unlike standard confidence measures, MEG utilizes Semantic Certainty Anchoring, focusing on high-IDF information-bearing tokens that better capture the semantic core of the answer. Building on MEG, we introduce MEG-RAG, a framework that trains a multimodal reranker to align retrieved evidence with the semantic anchors of the ground truth. By prioritizing high-value content based on semantic grounding rather than token probability distributions, MEG-RAG improves the accuracy and multimodal consistency of generated outputs. Extensive experiments on the M$^2$RAG benchmark show that MEG-RAG consistently outperforms strong baselines and demonstrates robust generalization across different teacher models.

</details>


### [92] [Skill Retrieval Augmentation for Agentic AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24594)
*Weihang Su,Jianming Long,Qingyao Ai,Yichen Tang,Changyue Wang,Yiteng Tu,Yiqun Liu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Skill Retrieval Augmentation (SRA), where LLM-based agents dynamically retrieve and use skills from large external corpora, and provides SRA-Bench to evaluate retrieval, incorporation, and execution, revealing that the main bottleneck is deciding which skills to load and when.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM agents rely on listing all available tools/skills in the context window, which does not scale as the number of skills grows: it wastes context, hurts retrieval accuracy, and limits agents’ effective capabilities. There is a need for a principled, scalable way to augment LLM agents with large skill libraries and to systematically evaluate this process.

Method: They formally define Skill Retrieval Augmentation (SRA) as a pipeline where an agent, given a task, first retrieves potentially useful skills from a large skill corpus, then incorporates selected skills into its context, and finally executes the task using them. To study this, they build a large skill corpus and construct SRA-Bench, a benchmark decomposing evaluation into three stages: skill retrieval quality, skill incorporation behavior, and downstream task performance. The corpus contains 636 carefully crafted gold skills embedded in 26,262 total skills (with many distractors) and 5,400 challenging test instances. They then run extensive experiments with current LLM agents under different augmentation strategies to assess performance and behavior across these stages.

Result: Results indicate that retrieval-based augmentation significantly boosts task success when relevant skills are correctly retrieved and incorporated. However, analyses show that current agents have poor sensitivity in deciding when and what to load: they tend to incorporate a similar number of skills regardless of whether a correct (gold) skill is retrieved or whether the task truly needs external skills. This uncovers limitations not just in retrieval but also in the incorporation and decision-making components of SRA.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that SRA is a promising and effective way to extend LLM agents’ capabilities via large external skill repositories, but also highlights a key bottleneck in agents’ ability to judiciously select and apply retrieved skills. By releasing SRA-Bench and the associated corpus, the paper frames SRA as a distinct research area and provides a foundation for future work on scalable, skill-augmented agent systems that can better decide which skills to load and when external augmentation is necessary.

Abstract: As large language models (LLMs) evolve into agentic problem solvers, they increasingly rely on external, reusable skills to handle tasks beyond their native parametric capabilities. In existing agent systems, the dominant strategy for incorporating skills is to explicitly enumerate available skills within the context window. However, this strategy fails to scale: as skill corpora expand, context budgets are consumed rapidly, and the agent becomes markedly less accurate in identifying the right skill. To this end, this paper formulates Skill Retrieval Augmentation (SRA), a new paradigm in which agents dynamically retrieve, incorporate, and apply relevant skills from large external skill corpora on demand. To make this problem measurable, we construct a large-scale skill corpus and introduce SRA-Bench, the first benchmark for decomposed evaluation of the full SRA pipeline, covering skill retrieval, skill incorporation, and end-task execution. SRA-Bench contains 5,400 capability-intensive test instances and 636 manually constructed gold skills, which are mixed with web-collected distractor skills to form a large-scale corpus of 26,262 skills. Extensive experiments show that retrieval-based skill augmentation can substantially improve agent performance, validating the promise of the paradigm. At the same time, we uncover a fundamental gap in skill incorporation: current LLM agents tend to load skills at similar rates, regardless of whether a gold skill is retrieved or whether the task actually requires external capabilities. This shows that the bottleneck in skill augmentation lies not only in retrieval but also in the base model's ability to determine which skill to load and when external loading is actually needed. These findings position SRA as a distinct research problem and establish a foundation for the scalable augmentation of capabilities in future agent systems.

### [93] [Looking for the Bottleneck in Fine-grained Temporal Relation Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24620)
*Hugo Sousa,Ricardo Campos,Alípio Jorge*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose a new method for temporal relation classification that models interval relations by first predicting relations between interval endpoints and then decoding them, achieving state-of-the-art results on TempEval-3.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Temporal relation classification between events and times is difficult, and prior work has simplified the problem by using fewer relation types and focusing mainly on event-event relations. The authors want to return to the original, richer task: determining the full set of possible interval relations between temporal entities, which better captures temporal structure but is more complex.

Method: They introduce an approach called Interval from Point. Instead of directly classifying the interval relation between two temporal entities, they first classify the relations between their endpoints (start and end points). From these predicted point-level relations, they algorithmically decode the corresponding interval-level relation using the full set of interval relations (e.g., as in interval algebra).

Result: On the TempEval-3 benchmark dataset, their Interval from Point approach attains a temporal awareness score of 70.1%, which surpasses previous methods and sets a new state-of-the-art on this dataset.

Conclusion: Modeling interval relations indirectly via endpoint (point) relations is an effective strategy for the more complex, full temporal relation classification task. This shows that it is feasible to handle the complete set of interval relations without resorting to overly simplified problem formulations, and it improves state-of-the-art performance on a standard benchmark.

Abstract: Temporal relation classification is the task of determining the temporal relation between pairs of temporal entities in a text.
  Despite recent advancements in natural language processing, temporal relation classification remains a considerable challenge.
  Early attempts framed this task using a comprehensive set of temporal relations between events and temporal expressions.
  However, due to the task complexity, datasets have been progressively simplified, leading recent approaches to focus on the relations between event pairs and to use only a subset of relations.
  In this work, we revisit the broader goal of classifying interval relations between temporal entities by considering the full set of relations that can hold between two time intervals.
  The proposed approach, Interval from Point, involves first classifying the point relations between the endpoints of the temporal entities and then decoding these point relations into an interval relation.
  Evaluation on the TempEval-3 dataset shows that this approach can yield effective results, achieving a temporal awareness score of $70.1$ percent, a new state-of-the-art on this benchmark.

Crucially, Korean models outperform significantly larger global models in local contexts, demonstrating that parameter scaling alone cannot resolve cultural dependencies. K-MetBench serves as a roadmap for developing reliable, culturally aware expert AI agents. The dataset is available at https://huggingface.co/datasets/soyeonbot/K-MetBench .

### [95] [DepthKV: Layer-Dependent KV Cache Pruning for Long-Context LLM Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24647)
*Zahra Dehghanighobadi,Asja Fischer*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces DepthKV, a layer-aware KV cache pruning strategy for LLMs that improves long-context inference efficiency under a fixed KV budget.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-context reasoning in LLMs requires storing large key-value (KV) caches during autoregressive inference, whose memory grows linearly with sequence length and becomes a bottleneck. Existing KV pruning methods use a uniform pruning ratio across layers, assuming equal importance of all layers, but this may not be optimal because different layers may respond differently to pruning. There is a need for a more nuanced, layer-aware approach that better exploits limited KV memory.

Method: The authors first empirically analyze how different transformer layers respond to KV cache pruning, characterizing each layer’s sensitivity to pruning. Based on this, they design DepthKV, a framework that enforces a fixed global KV cache budget but allocates that budget non-uniformly across layers according to their measured sensitivity. Instead of discarding low-attention tokens at the same rate in every layer, DepthKV applies layer-dependent pruning ratios while still using attention scores as the pruning criterion within each layer.

Result: Experiments across multiple LLMs and long-context tasks show that DepthKV yields better performance than existing uniform pruning strategies when compared at the same overall KV pruning ratio (i.e., same global KV budget). This indicates that redistributing the same memory budget in a depth-aware manner leads to more accurate long-context reasoning under constrained memory.

Conclusion: Layer sensitivity to KV cache pruning is heterogeneous and should be taken into account when designing memory-efficient inference schemes for LLMs. By allocating a fixed global KV cache budget across layers based on their pruning sensitivity, DepthKV improves the trade-off between memory savings and model performance compared with uniform pruning, leading to more effective use of limited KV memory for long-context reasoning.

Abstract: Long-context reasoning is a critical capability of large language models (LLMs), enabling applications such as long-document understanding, summarization, and code generation. However, efficient autoregressive inference relies on the key-value (KV) cache, whose memory footprint grows linearly with sequence length, leading to a major memory bottleneck. To mitigate this overhead, KV cache pruning methods discard cached tokens with low attention scores during inference. Most existing methods apply a uniform pruning ratio across layers, implicitly assuming that all layers contribute equally to overall model performance. We show that this assumption is suboptimal, as layers differ significantly in their sensitivity to pruning. We propose DepthKV, a layer-dependent pruning framework that allocates a fixed global KV budget across layers based on their sensitivity, rather than using a uniform allocation. Across multiple models and tasks, DepthKV consistently outperforms uniform pruning at the same global pruning ratio, demonstrating more effective utilization of the KV cache budget through layer-dependent allocation.

### [96] [Benchmarking Source-Sensitive Reasoning in Turkish: Humans and LLMs under Evidential Trust Manipulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24665)
*Sercan Karakaş,Yusuf Şimşek*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper tests whether trust in information sources influences Turkish evidential verb suffix choice and whether LLMs mirror this behavior. Humans show a robust trust effect; LLMs mostly do not.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand if and how perceived reliability of information sources affects grammatical evidential choices in Turkish, and to determine whether LLMs capture this nuanced, source-sensitive evidential reasoning.

Method: Human experiment: controlled cloze production task in Turkish manipulating source reliability (High-Trust vs. Low-Trust) while keeping source externality constant, examining distribution of past tense suffixes -DI vs. -mIs. LLM experiment: evaluation of 10 large language models across three prompting paradigms (open gap-fill, explicit past-tense gap-fill, forced-choice A/B) using same contrast, analyzing suffix choices, stability of effects, and prompt/model dependence.

Result: Human participants consistently use -DI more in High-Trust and -mIs more in Low-Trust contexts, with effects robust to sensitivity checks. LLMs show heterogeneous, weak, or unstable trust-related patterns, often reversed or masked by strong default suffix preferences and compliance/formatting issues.

Conclusion: Turkish evidential morphology is sensitive to perceived source trustworthiness, supporting trust-/commitment-based theories of evidentiality. Current LLMs fail to robustly model this source-sensitive evidential reasoning, highlighting a gap between human and model handling of evidential trust distinctions.

Abstract: This paper investigates whether source trustworthiness shapes Turkish evidential morphology and whether large language models (LLMs) track this sensitivity. We study the past-domain contrast between -DI and -mIs in controlled cloze contexts where the information source is overtly external, while only its perceived reliability is manipulated (High-Trust vs. Low-Trust). In a human production experiment, native speakers of Turkish show a robust trust effect: High-Trust contexts yield relatively more -DI, whereas Low-Trust contexts yield relatively more -mIs, with the pattern remaining stable across sensitivity analyses. We then evaluate 10 LLMs in three prompting paradigms (open gap-fill, explicit past-tense gap-fill, and forced-choice A/B selection). LLM behavior is highly model- and prompt-dependent: some models show weak or local trust-consistent shifts, but effects are generally unstable, often reversed, and frequently overshadowed by output-compliance problems and strong base-rate suffix preferences. The results provide new evidence for a trust-/commitment-based account of Turkish evidentiality and reveal a clear human-LLM gap in source-sensitive evidential reasoning.

Method: They introduce Contextual Linear Activation Steering (CLAS), which extends linear activation steering by dynamically adapting the steering strength based on the input context. They evaluate CLAS on eleven steering benchmarks across four LLM families and compare it to standard linear steering, ReFT, and LoRA in low-data settings.

Result: Across the evaluated benchmarks and model families, CLAS consistently outperforms standard linear activation steering and achieves performance that matches or surpasses more complex fine-tuning methods like ReFT and LoRA when only limited labeled data is available.

Conclusion: CLAS provides a scalable, interpretable, and accurate approach for specializing and steering large language models, overcoming the limitations of fixed-strength linear activation steering and offering a competitive alternative to heavier fine-tuning methods in low-data regimes.

Abstract: Linear activation steering is a powerful approach for eliciting the capabilities of large language models and specializing their behavior using limited labeled data. While effective, existing methods often apply a fixed steering strength to all tokens, resulting in inconsistent steering quality across diverse input prompts. In this work, we introduce Contextual Linear Activation Steering (CLAS), a method that dynamically adapts linear activation steering to context-dependent steering strengths. Across eleven steering benchmarks and four model families, it consistently outperforms standard linear activation steering and matches or exceeds the performance of ReFT and LoRA in settings with limited labeled data. We therefore propose CLAS as a scalable, interpretable, and accurate method for specializing and steering large language models.

### [99] [The Chameleon's Limit: Investigating Persona Collapse and Homogenization in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24698)
*Yunze Xiao,Vivienne J. Zhang,Chenghao Yang,Ningshan Ma,Weihao Xuan,Jen-tse Huang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper identifies and quantifies a new failure mode in LLM-based multi-agent populations, where agents with different assigned personas behave homogeneously, and provides metrics and tools to evaluate and analyze this population-level behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Many LLM applications—such as multi-agent simulations and social modeling—need agents to exhibit diverse, persona-consistent behaviors, but in practice agents often converge to similar behaviors despite different profiles, undermining realism and utility. There was no systematic way to detect or quantify this failure mode at the population level.

Method: The authors define and formalize the notion of Persona Collapse and introduce a three-part evaluation framework with metrics for Coverage (how much of the intended persona space is realized), Uniformity (how evenly agents occupy that space), and Complexity (how rich and varied their behavioral patterns are). They run controlled simulations with ten different LLMs on tasks spanning personality simulation using BFI-44, moral reasoning scenarios, and self-introduction prompts, and perform both aggregate metric analysis and item-level diagnostics of model outputs.

Result: Across models and tasks, they find consistent evidence of persona collapse: populations of agents, though given distinct profiles, cluster into narrow behavioral modes. Collapse is multidimensional: a model may look diverse on one behavioral axis but be degenerate on another, and performance varies by domain—for example, collapsing more on personality while being relatively diverse on moral reasoning. Fine-grained persona differences are largely ignored; instead, the emergent variation reflects broad demographic stereotypes. Strikingly, models that best match each individual persona specification often produce the overall most stereotyped and least diverse populations.

Conclusion: The work shows that current LLMs systematically fail to maintain genuine population-level diversity, even when they appear persona-faithful at the individual level, and that standard per-agent evaluations miss this problem. The authors argue for population-level evaluation using their Coverage–Uniformity–Complexity framework, provide open-source tools and data to enable such analysis, and highlight the need for methods that avoid stereotyped, collapsed populations in LLM-based simulations.

Abstract: Applications based on large language models (LLMs), such as multi-agent simulations, require population diversity among agents. We identify a pervasive failure mode we term \emph{Persona Collapse}: agents each assigned a distinct profile nonetheless converge into a narrow behavioral mode, producing a homogeneous simulated population. To quantify persona collapse, we propose a framework that measures how much of the persona space a population occupies (Coverage), how evenly agents spread across it (Uniformity), and how rich the resulting behavioral patterns are (Complexity). Evaluating ten LLMs on personality simulation (BFI-44), moral reasoning, and self-introduction, we observe persona collapse along two axes: (1) Dimensions: a model can appear diverse on one axis yet structurally degenerate on another, and (2) Domains: the same model may collapse the most in personality yet be the most diverse in moral reasoning. Furthermore, item-level diagnostics reveal that behavioral variation tracks coarse demographic stereotypes rather than the fine-grained individual differences specified in each persona. Counter-intuitively, \textbf{the models achieving the highest per-persona fidelity consistently produce the most stereotyped populations}. We release our toolkit and data to support population-level evaluation of LLMs.

### [100] [Green Shielding: A User-Centric Approach Towards Trustworthy AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24700)
*Aaron J. Li,Nicolas Sanchez,Hao Huang,Ruijiang Dong,Jaskaran Bains,Katrin Jaradeh,Zhen Xiang,Bo Li,Feng Liu,Aaron Kornblith,Bin Yu*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: The paper introduces Green Shielding, a user-centric framework to study how benign variations in user prompts affect LLM behavior, instantiated in a medical diagnosis benchmark showing that simple prompt changes create systematic, clinically meaningful tradeoffs in model outputs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing red-teaming and safety evaluations focus on adversarial prompts or extreme cases, but in real deployments LLM outputs can vary a lot even with routine, non-adversarial changes in how users phrase questions. This sensitivity is especially problematic in high-stakes settings like medical diagnosis, and there is little evidence-based guidance for users on how to phrase prompts to achieve safe and useful behavior. The authors aim to systematically characterize these benign prompt-induced shifts and turn that understanding into practical deployment guidance.

Method: They propose the Green Shielding agenda, which focuses on understanding model sensitivity to realistic, benign input variations using the CUE criteria: (C) authentic Context from real user queries; (U) Utility-focused reference standards and evaluation metrics that reflect what matters in practice; and (E) realistic Elicitation perturbations capturing how people naturally vary their prompts. Concretely, they build HealthCareMagic-Diagnosis (HCM-Dx), a benchmark of patient-authored medical queries with structured reference diagnosis sets and clinically grounded metrics for differential diagnosis quality. Using the PCS (Prediction, Comparison, Stability) framework, they design and apply different perturbation regimes to prompts (e.g., neutralization removing user-level factors while preserving clinical content) and analyze how these variations shift model outputs across several frontier LLMs.

Result: They find that routine, non-adversarial prompt variations reliably shift LLM behavior along clinically meaningful axes, and that these shifts form Pareto-like tradeoffs between different aspects of diagnostic quality. For example, neutralized prompts lead models to produce more plausible, concise, and clinician-like differential diagnosis lists but with reduced coverage of the most likely and safety-critical conditions. Across multiple LLMs, these tradeoff patterns are consistent, showing that prompt-level choices systematically influence key task-relevant properties rather than causing random noise.

Conclusion: Interaction and prompt-design choices in everyday use, even without adversarial intent, can systematically and predictably alter LLM behavior in ways that trade off between desirable properties such as plausibility, conciseness, and coverage of critical conditions. The Green Shielding framework and the HCM-Dx benchmark provide a way to study and quantify these effects and to derive evidence-backed, user-facing guidance for safer LLM deployment in high-stakes domains like healthcare. The approach is general and can be extended to other decision-support and agentic AI applications.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed, yet their outputs can be highly sensitive to routine, non-adversarial variation in how users phrase queries, a gap not well addressed by existing red-teaming efforts. We propose Green Shielding, a user-centric agenda for building evidence-backed deployment guidance by characterizing how benign input variation shifts model behavior. We operationalize this agenda through the CUE criteria: benchmarks with authentic Context, reference standards and metrics that capture true Utility, and perturbations that reflect realistic variations in the Elicitation of model behavior. Guided by the PCS framework and developed with practicing physicians, we instantiate Green Shielding in medical diagnosis through HealthCareMagic-Diagnosis (HCM-Dx), a benchmark of patient-authored queries, together with structured reference diagnosis sets and clinically grounded metrics for evaluating differential diagnosis lists. We also study perturbation regimes that capture routine input variation and show that prompt-level factors shift model behavior along clinically meaningful dimensions. Across multiple frontier LLMs, these shifts trace out Pareto-like tradeoffs. In particular, neutralization, which removes common user-level factors while preserving clinical content, increases plausibility and yields more concise, clinician-like differentials, but reduces coverage of highly likely and safety-critical conditions. Together, these results show that interaction choices can systematically shift task-relevant properties of model outputs and support user-facing guidance for safer deployment in high-stakes domains. Although instantiated here in medical diagnosis, the agenda extends naturally to other decision-support settings and agentic AI systems.

</details>


### [101] [Sentiment and Emotion Classification of Indonesian E-Commerce Reviews via Multi-Task BiLSTM and AutoML Benchmarking](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24720)
*Hermawan Manurung,Ibrahim Al-Kahfi,Ahmad Rizqi,Martin Clinton Tosima Manullang*

TL;DR: They propose and benchmark two sentiment and emotion classification pipelines for Indonesian marketplace reviews, comparing classic TF-IDF + AutoML with deep BiLSTM/TextCNN models, with heavy preprocessing for slang and noisy text, and deploy them as public Gradio apps.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing lexicon-based sentiment tools struggle with Indonesian marketplace language, which mixes slang, regional words, numbers, and emojis, so a more robust, data-driven approach is needed to handle both sentiment (positive/negative) and fine-grained emotions across diverse product categories.

Method: They use a two-track approach on the PRDECT-ID dataset (5,400 Indonesian e-commerce reviews labeled with binary sentiment and five emotions): (1) a traditional ML pipeline using TF-IDF text vectorization and PyCaret AutoML over multiple classifiers; (2) a deep learning pipeline with a shared-encoder BiLSTM in PyTorch with two task-specific heads for sentiment and emotion, plus TextCNN and several BiLSTM size/feature variants. Result: They obtain benchmark performance for four neural configurations (BiLSTM Baseline/Improved/Large and TextCNN) alongside traditional TF-IDF + AutoML models on the PRDECT-ID dataset; although exact numbers are not stated in the abstract, the implication is that the improved BiLSTM variants outperform baseline and traditional models for both sentiment and emotion prediction on noisy Indonesian reviews.

Conclusion: A carefully preprocessed, multitask BiLSTM architecture, complemented by traditional TF-IDF + AutoML baselines, can effectively handle noisy Indonesian marketplace language for simultaneous sentiment and emotion classification, and the resulting models are practical enough to deploy as public web services, providing a reusable benchmark and open-source implementation for future work.

Abstract: Indonesian marketplace reviews mix standard vocabulary with slang, regional loanwords, numeric shorthands, and emoji, making lexicon-based sentiment tools unreliable in practice. This paper describes a two-track classification pipeline applied to the PRDECT-ID dataset, which contains 5,400 product reviews from 29 Indonesian e-commerce categories, each labeled for binary sentiment (Positive/Negative) and five-class emotion (Happy, Sad, Fear, Love, Anger). The first track applies TF-IDF vectorization with a PyCaret AutoML sweep across standard classifiers. The second track is a PyTorch Bidirectional Long Short-Term Memory (BiLSTM) network with a shared encoder and two task-specific output heads. A preprocessing module applies 14 sequential cleaning steps, including a 140-entry slang dictionary assembled from marketplace corpora. Four configurations are benchmarked: BiLSTM Baseline, BiLSTM Improved, BiLSTM Large, and TextCNN. Training uses class-weighted cross-entropy loss, ReduceLROnPlateau scheduling, and early stopping. Both tracks are deployed as Gradio applications on Hugging Face Spaces. Source code is publicly available at https://github.com/ikii-sd/pba2026-crazyrichteam.

</details>

### [102] [PExA: Parallel Exploration Agent for Complex Text-to-SQL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22934)
*Tanmay Parekh,Ella Hofmann-Coyle,Shuyi Wang,Sachith Sri Ram Kothur,Srivas Prasad,Yunmo Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a new way to generate text-to-SQL with LLM agents by decomposing complex queries into parallel, simpler SQL test cases to improve both accuracy and latency, achieving state-of-the-art results on Spider 2.0.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM-based text-to-SQL systems face a trade-off between accuracy and latency: more complex reasoning or multi-step prompting improves performance but slows response time, while faster single-shot approaches lose accuracy. The authors want a method that maintains strong performance without incurring large latency costs, inspired by how software engineers use test coverage to validate behavior efficiently.

Method: The authors reinterpret text-to-SQL generation as a software testing and coverage problem. For a natural language query, they design a suite of simpler, atomic SQL "test cases" that each capture parts of the query’s semantics. These test case SQLs are executed in parallel to quickly probe the database and gather evidence. The system iteratively refines test coverage until it deems that enough semantic information has been collected. Only then does it generate the final SQL, grounding it in the already executed test case SQLs and their results, thereby reducing search space and improving reliability while allowing parallel execution to control latency.

Result: On the Spider 2.0 benchmark—currently a state-of-the-art, challenging dataset for text-to-SQL—the proposed framework achieves 70.2% execution accuracy, which surpasses previous best-reported systems. This demonstrates that their coverage-based, test-case-driven generation approach can reach or exceed SOTA performance while preserving favorable latency characteristics.

Conclusion: Viewing text-to-SQL generation as a coverage problem and using parallel, atomic test case SQLs to accumulate semantic evidence enables a more effective balance between accuracy and latency. The method both improves execution accuracy on a strong benchmark and offers a structured, test-driven paradigm that can be extended to other agentic LLM settings where complex reasoning must be reliably and efficiently grounded in executable operations.

Abstract: LLM-based agents for text-to-SQL often struggle with latency-performance trade-off, where performance improvements come at the cost of latency or vice versa. We reformulate text-to-SQL generation within the lens of software test coverage where the original query is prepared with a suite of test cases with simpler, atomic SQLs that are executed in parallel and together ensure semantic coverage of the original query. After iterating on test case coverage, the final SQL is generated only when enough information is gathered, leveraging the explored test case SQLs to ground the final generation. We validated our framework on a state-of-the-art benchmark for text-to-SQL, Spider 2.0, achieving a new state-of-the-art with 70.2% execution accuracy.

</details>


### [103] [The Power of Power Law: Asymmetry Enables Compositional Reasoning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22951)
*Zixuan Wang,Xingyu Dang,Jason D. Lee,Kaifeng Lyu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper shows that training language models on naturally occurring power-law data distributions is better for learning long-tail compositional skills than training on artificially uniform data.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Most language data is heavily skewed: common patterns are very frequent while many skills appear rarely. A standard belief is that rebalancing toward a uniform distribution should help models learn rare, long-tail skills. The paper is motivated by the need to test this belief and understand what data distributions are actually most effective for learning compositional reasoning skills, especially in the long tail.

Method: Empirically, the authors compare model training on power-law vs uniform data across various compositional reasoning tasks such as state tracking and multi-step arithmetic. Theoretically, they introduce a simplified, minimalist skill-composition task and analyze its learning dynamics under different sampling distributions. They study how power-law sampling shapes the loss landscape and data complexity required for learning both common and rare skill compositions.

Result: Empirical results show that power-law training consistently outperforms uniform training on compositional reasoning tasks, particularly for learning long-tail skills. Theoretical analysis proves that, in the minimalist setup, learning under a power-law distribution requires significantly fewer training examples than under a uniform distribution. The analysis attributes this to an asymmetry in the loss landscape induced by power-law sampling that guides learning more efficiently.

Conclusion: Contrary to common intuition, reweighting data toward uniformity can hurt learning of long-tail compositional skills. Natural power-law distributions can be inherently beneficial because they allow models to first master high-frequency compositions cheaply, which then serve as scaffolding for efficiently learning rare compositions. The paper concludes that an effective training distribution for models may need to preserve, or even exploit, power-law characteristics rather than flatten them.

Abstract: Natural language data follows a power-law distribution, with most knowledge and skills appearing at very low frequency. While a common intuition suggests that reweighting or curating data towards a uniform distribution may help models better learn these long-tail skills, we find a counterintuitive result: across a wide range of compositional reasoning tasks, such as state tracking and multi-step arithmetic, training under power-law distributions consistently outperforms training under uniform distributions. To understand this advantage, we introduce a minimalist skill-composition task and show that learning under a power-law distribution provably requires significantly less training data. Our theoretical analysis reveals that power law sampling induces a beneficial asymmetry that improves the pathological loss landscape, which enables models to first acquire high-frequency skill compositions with low data complexity, which in turn serves as a stepping stone to efficiently learn rare long-tailed skills. Our results offer an alternative perspective on what constitutes an effective data distribution for training models.

</details>


### [104] [On the Existence of an Inverse Solution for Preference-Based Reductions in Argumentation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22958)
*Alessio Zaninotto,Bruno Yun,Nir Oren,Srdjan Vesic*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies when a given labelling of an argumentation graph can be explained by some preference relation in preference-based argumentation frameworks, and shows this is usually efficiently decidable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Preference-based argumentation frameworks refine abstract argumentation by incorporating preferences that determine which attacks succeed as defeats. In practice, we often have an argument graph and an intended or observed labelling of arguments (accepted, rejected, undecided) under some semantics, and we want to know whether there exist preferences over arguments that justify this labelling. This inverse perspective is important for preference elicitation and for generating explanations of argument evaluation outcomes.

Method: The authors formalize an inverse problem: given an argumentation graph, a labelling, and a choice of semantics, determine whether there exists a preference relation such that, when applied via a given preference-based reduction, the resulting abstract argumentation framework has the given labelling under the chosen semantics. They investigate this problem under complete semantics for the four most widely used preference-based reductions, and analyze its computational complexity, giving algorithms and polynomial-time decision procedures in many cases.

Result: They show that, for the considered reductions under complete semantics, deciding whether some preference relation can produce a desired labelling is in most cases solvable in polynomial time. This identifies conditions under which the inverse problem is tractable.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that, for common preference-based reductions in argumentation, the inverse problem of reconstructing a compatible preference relation from a target labelling is generally computationally feasible under complete semantics. This supports practical uses of PAFs in preference elicitation and explainability, since it is often efficient to check whether observed or desired labellings can be explained by some underlying preferences.

Abstract: Preference-based argumentation frameworks (PAFs) extend Dung's approach to abstract argumentation (AAFs) by encoding preferences over arguments. Such preferences control the transformation of attacks into defeats, and different approaches to doing so result in different reductions from a PAF to an AAF. In this paper we consider a PAF inverse problem which takes an argumentation graph, a labelling and a semantics as an input, and outputs a ``yes" or ``no" as to whether there is a preference relation between the arguments which can yield the desired labelling. This inverse problem has applications in areas including preference elicitation and explainability. We consider this problem in the context of the four most widely-used preference based reductions under the complete semantics. We show that in most cases, the problem can be answered in polynomial time.

</details>


### [105] [Towards Causally Interpretable Wi-Fi CSI-Based Human Activity Recognition with Discrete Latent Compression and LTL Rule Extraction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.22979)
*Luca Cotti,Luca Lavazza,Marco Cominelli,Liying Han,Gaofeng Dong,Francesco Gringoli,Mani B. Srivastava,Trevor Bihl,Erik P. Blasch,Daniel O. Brigham,Kara Combs,Lance M. Kaplan,Federico Cerutti*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes CHARL-TRE, a method that converts high-dimensional Wi-Fi CSI signals into discrete latent codes, discovers temporal causal rules over them, and uses these rules as a fully symbolic classifier for human activity recognition, achieving competitive accuracy with interpretable and controllable models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing CSI-based HAR methods use deep neural networks that operate on raw signals and achieve high accuracy but are black-box, continuous, and hard to interpret or control. Symbolic and rule-based approaches, while interpretable and causally meaningful, cannot directly process high-dimensional raw CSI streams. There is a need for a method that (1) works directly on CSI, (2) yields causal and temporal structure, and (3) offers symbolic representations that are manipulable and support structured multi-antenna fusion.

Method: The authors design a two-stage, fully decoupled pipeline. First, they train a categorical variational autoencoder with Gumbel-Softmax latent variables and a capacity-controlled objective on windows of CSI magnitudes, learning a compressed discrete latent representation. After training, the encoder is frozen and used as a deterministic mapping from CSI windows to one-hot latent trajectories. Second, they run causal discovery on these discrete trajectories to estimate activity-class-conditional temporal dependency graphs. These lagged dependencies are then automatically converted into Linear Temporal Logic rules. Classification is performed purely by evaluating and aggregating these LTL rules, with no additional discriminative head or end-to-end fine-tuning. Because the rules operate on discrete latent variables, rule sets from different antennas can be combined symbolically for multi-antenna fusion without retraining the encoder.

Result: On Wi-Fi CSI-based HAR benchmarks, the proposed CHARL-TRE framework delivers performance that is competitive with existing deep neural models. At the same time, it produces explicit temporal and causal structures in the form of LTL rules and dependency graphs, making the resulting classifier more interpretable and symbolically controllable than standard black-box deep models.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that deterministic symbolic classification, grounded in unsupervised discrete latent representations learned from raw CSI, is a practical and competitive alternative to end-to-end neural networks for wireless HAR. By separating representation learning from symbolic reasoning and leveraging causal discovery plus LTL rule extraction, the approach provides interpretable, temporally structured, and potentially composable multi-antenna models without sacrificing much predictive performance.

Abstract: We address Human Activity Recognition (HAR) utilizing Wi-Fi Channel State Information (CSI) under the joint requirements of causal interpretability, symbolic controllability, and direct operation on high-dimensional raw signals. Deep neural models achieve strong predictive performance on CSI-based HAR (CHAR), yet rely on continuous latent representations that are opaque and difficult to modify; purely symbolic approaches, in contrast, cannot process raw CSI streams. We propose a fully automatic and strictly decoupled pipeline in which CSI magnitude windows are compressed by a categorical variational autoencoder with Gumbel-Softmax latent variables under a capacity-controlled objective, yielding a compact discrete representation. The encoder is then frozen and used as a deterministic mapping to one-hot latent trajectories. Causal discovery is performed on these trajectories to estimate class-conditional temporal dependency graphs. Statistically supported lagged dependencies are translated into Linear Temporal Logic (LTL) rules, producing a fully symbolic and deterministic classifier based solely on rule evaluation and aggregation, without any learned discriminative head. Because rules are defined over discrete latent variables, antenna-specific rule sets can in principle be combined at the symbolic level, enabling structured multi-antenna fusion without retraining the encoder. Results from CHAR Latent Temporal Rule Extraction (CHARL-TRE) indicate competitive performance while preserving explicit temporal and causal structure, showing that deterministic symbolic classification grounded in unsupervised discrete latent representations constitutes a viable alternative to end-to-end black-box models for wireless HAR.

</details>


### [106] [FormalScience: Scalable Human-in-the-Loop Autoformalisation of Science with Agentic Code Generation in Lean](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23002)
*Jordan Meadows,Lan Zhang,Andre Freitas*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces FormalScience, a human-in-the-loop agentic pipeline to turn informal scientific reasoning (focusing on physics) into syntactically correct and semantically aligned formal proofs in Lean4, and releases the FormalPhysics dataset plus tools and UI for broader scientific autoformalisation research.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large language models still struggle to translate informal mathematical and scientific reasoning into formally verifiable code, especially in physics where domain-specific notation and concepts add complexity. Existing formal math benchmarks lack realistic physics problems, and there is limited understanding of how and why semantic meaning is lost (semantic drift) when autoformalising such content. The authors want a practical workflow that domain experts can actually use, plus a benchmark and analysis framework to study and improve autoformalisation in scientific domains.

Method: They design FormalScience, a domain-agnostic, human-in-the-loop agentic pipeline that coordinates LLMs with a domain expert who need not know Lean in depth. The pipeline guides the expert and model through stages to produce Lean4 code that is both syntactically valid and semantically faithful to the original LaTeX solutions. Using this pipeline for physics, they build FormalPhysics, a dataset of 200 university-level problems and solutions (mainly quantum mechanics and electromagnetism) together with their Lean4 formal proofs. They then benchmark multiple open-source and proprietary LLMs on a statement autoformalisation task under several settings: zero-shot prompting, self-refinement with error feedback, and a new multi-stage agentic approach. Finally, they analyse cases of semantic drift, introducing notions like notational collapse and abstraction elevation to categorise types of misalignment between informal and formal statements.

Result: FormalPhysics achieves perfect formal validity (all statements check in Lean4) and has higher statement complexity than existing formal mathematics benchmarks. The FormalScience pipeline enables a single domain expert to efficiently produce formally verified versions of physics reasoning at low cost. Empirical evaluation shows that current LLMs and agentic setups still have substantial difficulty with faithful autoformalisation of physics statements, with characteristic patterns of semantic drift identified and categorised. They also implement and release an interactive UI and codebase to run FormalScience and support further experiments in scientific theorem proving and autoformalisation.

Conclusion: The work demonstrates that a structured, human-in-the-loop agentic pipeline can reliably produce high-quality formal physics proofs from informal LaTeX, yielding a new benchmark (FormalPhysics) that is more complex yet fully formally valid. It also shows that current LLMs, even with sophisticated prompting and multi-stage agents, are limited in preserving semantics when autoformalising physics, and it provides a first systematic vocabulary and framework for understanding these failures. The released tools and dataset aim to catalyse further research on autoformalisation and machine-assisted theorem proving across scientific domains, not just pure mathematics.

Abstract: Formalising informal mathematical reasoning into formally verifiable code is a significant challenge for large language models. In scientific fields such as physics, domain-specific machinery (\textit{e.g.} Dirac notation, vector calculus) imposes additional formalisation challenges that modern LLMs and agentic approaches have yet to tackle. To aid autoformalisation in scientific domains, we present FormalScience; a domain-agnostic human-in-the-loop agentic pipeline that enables a single domain expert (without deep formal language experience) to produce \textit{syntactically correct} and \textit{semantically aligned} formal proofs of informal reasoning for low economic cost. Applying FormalScience to physics, we construct FormalPhysics, a dataset of 200 university-level (LaTeX) physics problems and solutions (primarily quantum mechanics and electromagnetism), along with their Lean4 formal representations. Compared to existing formal math benchmarks, FormalPhysics achieves perfect formal validity and exhibits greater statement complexity. We evaluate open-source models and proprietary systems on a statement autoformalisation task on our dataset via zero-shot prompting, self-refinement with error feedback, and a novel multi-stage agentic approach, and explore autoformalisation limitations in modern LLM-based approaches. We provide the first systematic characterisation of semantic drift in physics autoformalisation in terms of concepts such as notational collapse and abstraction elevation which reveals what formal language verifies when full semantic preservation is unattainable. We release the codebase together with an interactive UI-based FormalScience system which facilitates autoformalisation and theorem proving in scientific domains beyond physics.https://github.com/jmeadows17/formal-science

### [107] [A Systematic Approach for Large Language Models Debugging](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23027)
*Basel Shbita,Anna Lisa Gentile,Bing Zhang,Sungeun An,Shailja Thakur,Shubhi Asthana,Yi Zhou,Saptha Surendran,Farhan Ahmed,Rohan Kulkarni,Yuya Jeremy Ong,Chad DeLuca,Hima Patel*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a structured, model-agnostic methodology for debugging large language models, unifying evaluation, interpretability, and error analysis to systematically detect, diagnose, and fix model issues.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs are widely used but difficult to debug because their behavior is opaque, probabilistic, and task- and context-dependent, and many practical settings lack standardized benchmarks or clear evaluation criteria. Practitioners need a systematic way to understand and improve LLM behavior across diverse workflows.

Method: Treat LLMs as observable systems and define a structured, model-agnostic debugging pipeline that spans issue detection, error categorization, interpretability-driven investigation, and targeted refinement of prompts, model parameters, and data. The approach emphasizes iterative cycles and unifies existing evaluation and error-analysis tools under a single methodology that can operate even without formal benchmarks.

Result: The proposed framework organizes the debugging process for LLMs, showing how practitioners can systematically surface weaknesses, trace them to specific failure modes, and make targeted adjustments to prompts, parameters, and datasets. It demonstrates that this unified approach works across varied tasks and in settings lacking standard benchmarks, leading to more efficient troubleshooting and improvement of LLM systems.

Conclusion: A structured debugging methodology for LLMs can significantly improve the transparency, reproducibility, and scalability of building and maintaining LLM-based systems. By integrating evaluation, interpretability, and error analysis into a single, iterative process, practitioners can more reliably diagnose and fix model issues even in complex, under-specified application contexts.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) have become central to modern AI workflows, powering applications from open-ended text generation to complex agent-based reasoning. However, debugging these models remains a persistent challenge due to their opaque and probabilistic nature and the difficulty of diagnosing errors across diverse tasks and settings. This paper introduces a systematic approach for LLM debugging that treats models as observable systems, providing structured, model-agnostic methods from issue detection to model refinement. By unifying evaluation, interpretability, and error-analysis practices, our approach enables practitioners to iteratively diagnose model weaknesses, refine prompts and model parameters, and adapt data for fine-tuning or assessment, while remaining effective in contexts where standardized benchmarks and evaluation criteria are lacking. We argue that such a structured methodology not only accelerates troubleshooting but also fosters reproducibility, transparency, and scalability in the deployment of LLM-based systems.

</details>

Existing HITL mechanisms are tightly coupled to specific applications, making them hard to reuse, standardize, and scale across multi-agent systems. The paper aims to address these limitations by rethinking HITL as a first-class, decoupled system component.

Result: The resulting architecture supports integration of HITL at the protocol level, aligned with emerging agent communication standards. It allows HITL to be implemented externally to individual applications while still influencing agent behavior, enabling reuse and consistent governance across agentic workflows. Prior work has established the importance of human oversight for ensuring transparency, accountability, and trustworthiness in such systems. However, existing implementations of Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) mechanisms are typically embedded within application logic, limiting reuse, consistency, and scalability across multi-agent environments.
  This paper presents a decoupled HITL system architecture that treats human oversight as an independent system component within the agent operating environment. The proposed design separates human interaction management from application workflows through explicit interfaces and a structured execution model. In addition, a design framework is introduced to formalize HITL integration along four dimensions: intervention conditions, role resolution, interaction semantics, and communication channel. This framework enables selective and context-aware human involvement while maintaining system-level consistency.
  The approach supports alignment with emerging agent communication protocols, allowing HITL to be implemented as a protocol-level concern. By externalizing HITL and structuring its integration, the system provides a foundation for scalable governance and progressive autonomy in agentic workflows.

### [109] [Don't Make the LLM Read the Graph: Make the Graph Think](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23057)
*Yuqi Sun,Tianqin Meng,George Liu,Yashraj Panwar,Lakshya Chaudhry,Munasib Ilham,Aman Chadha*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies whether making LLMs use explicit belief graphs improves their cooperative reasoning in the game Hanabi, and finds that graphs help mainly when tightly integrated into action selection and inter-agent conventions, with diminishing returns for deeper graphs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs show promising but inconsistent performance in multi-agent cooperative tasks that require Theory of Mind, such as Hanabi. It is unclear when explicit representations of other agents’ beliefs (belief graphs) actually help, how they should be integrated with LLM decision-making, and how these effects scale with model strength and number of players. The paper aims to systematically test these issues across multiple LLM families and configurations.

Method: The authors run over 3,000 controlled Hanabi game trials using four different LLM families. They introduce explicit belief graphs encoding nested beliefs (including 2nd-order Theory of Mind) and compare two main integration schemes: (1) including belief graphs as additional prompt context, and (2) using belief graphs to gate and rank action choices via shortlists that constrain the LLM’s options. They measure performance on Theory of Mind reasoning subtasks and full-game scores, analyze a newly observed failure mode called Planner Defiance, compare single-agent vs multi-agent convention-based interventions, and run an exploratory scaling study varying belief graph depth and player count.

Result: 1) As simple prompt additions, belief graphs add little for strong models but help weaker ones on 2nd-order ToM; when used to gate action selection via ranked shortlists, belief graphs become critical even for strong models, dramatically improving 2nd-order ToM performance. 2) They discover Planner Defiance, where LLMs often override correct planner recommendations, with large variation by model family: Gemini models rarely defy planners, while Llama 70B frequently does and selectively ignores advisory content. 3) In full Hanabi games, introducing inter-agent conventions and combining multiple belief-graph components yields much larger gains than any single-agent tweak alone. 4) Exploratory scaling suggests shallow belief graphs are most efficient: deeper ToM graphs show diminishing or even negative returns, especially in larger, 5-player settings.

Conclusion: Explicit belief graphs can significantly enhance LLM performance in cooperative multi-agent reasoning, but only when tightly coupled to the decision pipeline (e.g., action gating) rather than used as passive context. Model-family-specific behaviors like Planner Defiance constrain how planners and advisors should interact with LLMs. Coordinated inter-agent conventions and judiciously shallow belief graphs appear more effective and scalable than ever-deeper Theory of Mind modeling, informing future design of multi-agent LLM systems.

Abstract: We investigate whether explicit belief graphs improve LLM performance in cooperative multi-agent reasoning. Through 3,000+ controlled trials across four LLM families in the cooperative card game Hanabi, we establish four findings. First, integration architecture determines whether belief graphs provide value: as prompt context, graphs are decorative for strong models and beneficial only for weak models on 2nd-order Theory of Mind (80% vs 10%, p<0.0001, OR=36.0); when graphs gate action selection through ranked shortlists, they become structurally essential even for strong models (100% vs 20% on 2nd-order ToM, p<0.001). Second, we identify "Planner Defiance," a model-family-specific failure where LLMs override correct planner recommendations at partial competence (90% override, replicated N=20); Gemini models show near-zero defiance while Llama 70B shows 90%, and models distinguish factual context (deferred to) from advisory recommendations (overridden). Third, full-game evidence confirms inter-agent conventions (+128% over baseline, p=0.003) outperform all single-agent interventions, and individual belief-graph components must be combined to produce gains. Fourth, preliminary scaling analysis (N=10/cell, exploratory) suggests graph depth has diminishing returns: shallow graphs provide the best cost-benefit ratio, while deeper ToM graphs appear harmful at larger player counts (-1.5 pts at 5-player, p=0.029).

</details>


### [110] [Analytica: Soft Propositional Reasoning for Robust and Scalable LLM-Driven Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23072)
*Junyan Cheng,Kyle Richardson,Peter Chin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Analytica is an LLM-based agent architecture that performs structured, soft propositional reasoning to improve accuracy and stability in complex analytical tasks like forecasting, significantly reducing bias, variance, cost, and time compared to standard LLM agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LLM agents, used for complex real-world analysis such as financial forecasting and scientific discovery, often produce unstable, hard-to-verify reasoning with no clear compositional structure. Their outputs are sensitive to randomness (high variance) and can systematically deviate from the truth (bias), making them unreliable for high-stakes analytical tasks. The authors aim to design an approach that makes LLM reasoning more structured, statistically grounded, verifiable, and robust, while remaining scalable and cost-effective.

Method: The paper introduces Soft Propositional Reasoning (SPR), which models complex analyses as estimating soft (probabilistic) truth values of outcome propositions and subpropositions. Analytica implements SPR via a parallel, divide-and-conquer architecture: (1) decompose the main problem into a tree of subpropositions; (2) use tool-equipped LLM “grounder” agents—including a new Jupyter Notebook agent—to validate, compute, and score factual claims at the leaves; (3) recursively aggregate these leaf scores using robust linear models that minimize bias and variance, averaging out stochastic noise. The system supports modular integration of different LLM grounders and enables interactive what-if analysis on the structured proposition graph.

Result: On economic, financial, and political forecasting benchmarks, Analytica yields an average 15.84% accuracy gain over various base LLMs. With a Deep Research grounder, it reaches 71.06% accuracy and the lowest reported variance of 6.02%. The Jupyter Notebook grounder attains a close 70.11% accuracy while cutting cost by 90.35% and runtime by 52.85%. The architecture shows near-linear scaling with analysis depth, strong robustness to noise, and successful adaptation to open-weight LLMs and scientific tasks.

Conclusion: Analytica demonstrates that framing LLM-based analysis as soft propositional reasoning, combined with structured decomposition and statistical aggregation, yields more accurate, stable, and interpretable reasoning than conventional agent setups. The architecture effectively reduces both bias and variance, scales efficiently, is cost- and time-effective (especially with the Jupyter Notebook grounder), and generalizes across domains and model types, making it a promising foundation for reliable, complex real-world analysis.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) agents are increasingly tasked with complex real-world analysis (e.g., in financial forecasting, scientific discovery), yet their reasoning suffers from stochastic instability and lacks a verifiable, compositional structure. To address this, we introduce Analytica, a novel agent architecture built on the principle of Soft Propositional Reasoning (SPR). SPR reframes complex analysis as a structured process of estimating the soft truth values of different outcome propositions, allowing us to formally model and minimize the estimation error in terms of its bias and variance. Analytica operationalizes this through a parallel, divide-and-conquer framework that systematically reduces both sources of error. To reduce bias, problems are first decomposed into a tree of subpropositions, and tool-equipped LLM grounder agents are employed, including a novel Jupyter Notebook agent for data-driven analysis, that help to validate and score facts. To reduce variance, Analytica recursively synthesizes these grounded leaves using robust linear models that average out stochastic noise with superior efficiency, scalability, and enable interactive "what-if" scenario analysis. Our theoretical and empirical results on economic, financial, and political forecasting tasks show that Analytica improves 15.84% accuracy on average over diverse base models, achieving 71.06% accuracy with the lowest variance of 6.02% when working with a Deep Research grounder. Our Jupyter Notebook grounder shows strong cost-effectiveness that achieves a close 70.11% accuracy with 90.35% less cost and 52.85% less time. Analytica also exhibits highly noise-resilient and stable performance growth as the analysis depth increases, with a near-linear time complexity, as well as good adaptivity to open-weight LLMs and scientific domains.

</details>


### [111] [Towards Automated Ontology Generation from Unstructured Text: A Multi-Agent LLM Approach](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23090)
*Abid Talukder,Maruf Ahmed Mridul,Oshani Seneviratne*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper studies how to better generate formal ontologies from natural language using large language models, comparing a single-agent baseline to a structured multi-agent architecture, and finds that planning-first, artifact-driven multi-agent designs significantly improve structural quality and somewhat improve query performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Automatically turning unstructured natural language (here, insurance contracts) into high‑quality formal ontologies is still difficult. Although LLMs look promising, it is unclear which model/architecture choices matter, why existing methods fail, and how to design systems that are both scalable and auditable. The authors aim to systematically analyze failure modes of current single‑agent LLM approaches and test whether a decomposed multi‑agent setup can address these issues.

Method: 1) Build a single‑agent LLM baseline for ontology generation from domain‑specific insurance contracts and analyze its failure modes (including Ontology Design Pattern violations, redundancy, and weak iterative repair). 2) Propose a multi‑agent architecture with four specialized roles—Domain Expert, Manager, Coder, and Quality Assurer—each producing intermediate artifacts to structure the process. 3) Evaluate both architectures using: (a) architectural/structural quality via a panel of heterogeneous LLM judges; and (b) functional usability via competency question–driven SPARQL tests plus a complementary RAG‑based assessment of queryability.

Result: The multi‑agent architecture substantially improves structural aspects of the generated ontologies, such as alignment with Ontology Design Patterns, reduced redundancy, and better overall organization. It also produces modest but consistent gains in functional usability, as measured by SPARQL competency question performance and RAG‑based evaluation. Analysis indicates that the main source of improvement is the early, explicit planning steps enforced by the multi‑agent, artifact‑driven workflow.

Conclusion: A planning‑first, artifact‑driven multi‑agent LLM architecture is more effective than a single‑agent setup for automatic ontology engineering from natural language, especially in terms of structural quality and auditability, and yields moderate gains in queryability. Decomposing the task into specialized roles and enforcing upfront planning emerges as a promising direction for scalable, reliable ontology generation systems.

Abstract: Automatically generating formal ontologies from unstructured natural language remains a central challenge in knowledge engineering. While large language models (LLMs) show promise, it remains unclear which architectural design choices drive generation quality and why current approaches fail. We present a controlled experimental study using domain-specific insurance contracts to investigate these questions. We then introduce a multi-agent architecture that decomposes ontology construction into four artifact-driven roles: Domain Expert, Manager, Coder, and Quality Assurer. We evaluate performance across architectural quality (via a panel of heterogeneous LLM judges) and functional usability (via competency question driven SPARQL evaluation with complementary retrieval augmented generation based assessment). Results show that the multi-agent approach significantly improves structural quality and modestly enhances queryability, with gains driven primarily by front-loaded planning. These findings highlight planning-first, artifact-driven generation as a promising and more auditable path toward scalable automated ontology engineering.
