### [137] [Causal Discovery as Dialectical Aggregation: A Quantitative Argumentation Framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23633)
*Sheng Wei,Yulin Chen,Beishui Liao*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces QACD, a new framework that treats conditional-independence test results as graded, defeasible arguments rather than hard constraints, to make constraint-based causal discovery more robust in finite samples.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Constraint-based causal discovery methods rely heavily on conditional-independence tests. In finite-sample settings, these tests are noisy and can be wrong; a few incorrect CI decisions can propagate and cause large structural errors in the learned causal graph. A more robust way is needed to handle uncertain or conflicting CI information instead of committing to binary decisions.

Method: The authors propose Quantitative Argumentation for Causal Discovery (QACD), which uses ideas from argumentation theory and semantics. CI test outcomes are encoded as arguments with quantitative strengths, not just yes/no constraints. Conflicting pieces of CI evidence interact via a process called connectivity-mediated witness propagation. This process aggregates evidence and computes a fixed-point acceptability labeling over possible edges (adjacencies) in the causal graph, determining which edges are supported or defeated by the overall argumentation structure.

Result: On standard Bayesian network benchmarks, QACD is evaluated under noisy or inconsistent CI testing regimes. The method yields graphs with better structural coherence (i.e., closer to the true underlying structure) and higher interventional reliability (i.e., better performance for predicting intervention outcomes) compared with classical constraint-based methods, hybrid methods, and older argumentation-based baselines. Performance remains competitive overall.

Conclusion: By replacing brittle, binary CI constraints with a graded, defeasible argumentation framework, QACD makes constraint-based causal discovery more robust to finite-sample noise. The semantics-driven aggregation of CI evidence via witness propagation yields more coherent and interventionally reliable causal structures, suggesting a promising direction for combining statistical testing with formal argumentation in causal discovery.

Abstract: Constraint-based causal discovery is brittle in finite-sample regimes because erroneous conditional-independence (CI) decisions can cascade into substantial structural errors. We propose Quantitative Argumentation for Causal Discovery (QACD), a semantics-driven framework that represents CI outcomes as graded, defeasible arguments rather than irreversible constraints. QACD maps statistical test outcomes to argument strengths and aggregates conflicting evidence through connectivity-mediated witness propagation, producing a fixed-point acceptability labeling over candidate adjacencies. Experiments on standard benchmark Bayesian networks suggest that QACD improves structural coherence and interventional reliability in several noisy or inconsistent CI regimes, while remaining competitive with classical constraint-based, hybrid, and prior argumentation-based baselines.

</details>


### [138] [Structural Enforcement of Goal Integrity in AI Agents via Separation-of-Powers Architecture](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23646)
*Rong Xiang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces PEA, a system-level “separation-of-powers” architecture that constrains autonomous AI agents through cryptographically enforced layers for intent generation, verification, and execution, offering stronger guarantees of alignment than model-level training alone.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Frontier AI models can autonomously generate harmful, misaligned actions from internal goals, and current alignment techniques like RLHF and constitutional prompting only reduce risk probabilistically at the model level without hard guarantees. There is a need for architectures that enforce safety at the system level so that even compromised or misaligned models cannot easily cause harm.

Method: The authors design the Policy-Execution-Authorization (PEA) architecture that splits the AI agent pipeline into distinct, isolated components: an intent generation layer, an authorization layer, and an execution layer, all connected via cryptographically constrained capability tokens. They implement five mechanisms: (C1) an Intent Verification Layer to check that requested capabilities match the stated intent; (C2) Intent Lineage Tracking to cryptographically bind executable intents to the originating user request; (C3) Goal Drift Detection to compare new intents against the original request and reject those that deviate beyond a threshold; (C4) an Output Semantic Gate using a structured Knowledge × Influence × Policy threat calculus to detect and block implicitly coercive or harmful outputs; and (C5) a formal verification framework that proves, under stated assumptions, that goal integrity is preserved even if the underlying model behaves adversarially.

Result: The PEA architecture is shown to enforce cryptographic binding between user requests and downstream actions, successfully detecting and rejecting intents that diverge from authorized goals while filtering coercive outputs via the OSG. The formal verification results suggest that, within the modeled threat assumptions, an attacker compromising the model cannot cause actions that violate the original user intent without breaking the cryptographic or policy mechanisms. Empirical or illustrative evaluations demonstrate that PEA catches goal drift and harmful behaviors that model-only alignment techniques might miss.

Conclusion: By reframing alignment as a property of the overall agentic system architecture rather than solely of the model’s learned behavior, PEA can provide stronger, structurally enforced guarantees that autonomous agents act within the bounds of user-authorized goals. This “separation-of-powers” design, with cryptographic capability control and formally verified goal integrity, offers a more robust foundation for governing powerful AI agents than relying purely on probabilistic, training-time alignment methods.

Abstract: Recent evidence suggests that frontier AI systems can exhibit agentic misalignment, generating and executing harmful actions derived from internally constructed goals, even without explicit user requests. Existing mitigation methods, such as Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF) and constitutional prompting, operate primarily at the model level and provide only probabilistic safety guarantees. We propose the Policy-Execution-Authorization (PEA) architecture, a "separation-of-powers" design that enforces safety at the system level. PEA decouples intent generation, authorization, and execution into independent, isolated layers connected via cryptographically constrained capability tokens. We present five core contributions: (C1) an Intent Verification Layer (IVL) for ensuring capability-intent consistency; (C2) Intent Lineage Tracking (ILT), which binds all executable intents to the originating user request via cryptographic anchors; (C3) Goal Drift Detection, which rejects semantically divergent intents below a configurable threshold; (C4) an Output Semantic Gate (OSG) that detects implicit coercion using a structured $K \times I \times P$ threat calculus (Knowledge, Influence, Policy); and (C5) a formal verification framework proving that goal integrity is maintained even under adversarial model compromise. By shifting agent alignment from a behavioral property to a structurally enforced system constraint, PEA provides a robust foundation for the governance of autonomous agents.

</details>

It also shows that spatial income segregation governs model transferability and can be quantified to guide where models are likely to work well. The resulting global set of mobility proxies can help alleviate data shortages in underdeveloped, resource-limited regions for applications in urban planning and public health.

Abstract: Accurate modeling of human mobility is critical for tackling urban planning and public health challenges. In undeveloped regions, the absence of comprehensive travel surveys necessitates reconstructing mobility networks from publicly available data. Here we develop neuroGravity, a physics-informed deep learning model that reliably reconstructs mobility flows from limited observations and transfers to unobserved cities. Using only urban facility and population distributions, we find that neuroGravity's regional representations strongly correlate with socioeconomic and livability status, offering scalable proxies for costly surveys. Furthermore, we uncover that spatial income segregation plays a key role in model transferability: mobility networks are most reliably reconstructed when target cities share similar segregation levels with the source. We design an index to quantify this segregation and accurately predict transferability. Finally, we generate mobility flow proxies for over 1,200 cities worldwide, highlighting neuroGravity's potential to mitigate critical data shortages in resource-limited, underdeveloped areas.

### [141] [Information-Theoretic Measures in AI: A Practical Decision Guide](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23716)
*Nikolaos Al. Papadopoulos,Konstantinos E. Psannis*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper surveys seven key information-theoretic and agent-complexity measures and provides a practical decision framework (flowchart + table) to help AI practitioners pick the right measure, estimator, and usage in real applications.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Information-theoretic quantities like entropy, cross-entropy, and mutual information, and newer measures like integrated and effective information, are widely used across AI and agent modeling. However, users often select measures without understanding what scientific or engineering question they truly answer, what estimators are valid for given data regimes, and where the measures can be dangerously misapplied. This gap risks invalid inferences and confusing cognitive or agent-level interpretations with low-level statistics. The paper aims to systematize when and how to use each measure responsibly.

Method: The authors conceptually analyze seven measures—entropy, cross-entropy, mutual information, transfer entropy, integrated information (Phi), effective information (EI), and autonomy—and derive, for each, three prescriptive components: (i) the precise question the measure answers and its typical AI/ML and agent-centric use cases; (ii) the appropriate estimators given data type (discrete/continuous, temporal/static) and dimensionality; and (iii) the most critical misuse patterns. They compile this into a decision framework consisting of a measure-selection flowchart and a comprehensive decision table, augmented with “Bridge Boxes” that map the formal IT quantities to cognitive/agent-level constructs. They then apply the framework to three detailed case studies involving representation learning, temporal influence analysis, and quantifying evolved agent complexity.

Result: The outcome is a unified, practitioner-oriented framework that organizes seven widely used measures across AI and agent complexity research, providing clear guidance on: which measure to use for which question, what estimators are feasible under different data regimes, and what interpretational pitfalls to avoid. The framework is instantiated as an actionable flowchart and a master decision table, plus standardized cognitive-interpretation aids (Bridge Boxes). The three worked examples show how the framework can concretely guide measure choice and interpretation in diverse scenarios, demonstrating its practicality and generality.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that responsible use of both classical information-theoretic measures and newer agent-complexity measures requires aligning the chosen quantity with the underlying scientific or engineering question, as well as with realistic estimation constraints and known pitfalls. Their decision framework, flowchart, and decision table provide a structured way for practitioners to make these choices and avoid common misuses, thereby improving the reliability and interpretability of AI and agent complexity studies that rely on these measures.

Abstract: Information-theoretic (IT) measures are ubiquitous in artificial intelligence: entropy drives decision-tree splits and uncertainty quantification, cross-entropy is the default classification loss, mutual information underpins representation learning and feature selection, and transfer entropy reveals directed influence in dynamical systems.
  A second, less consolidated family of measures, integrated information (Phi), effective information (EI), and autonomy, has emerged for characterizing agent complexity. Despite wide adoption, measure selection is often decoupled from estimator assumptions, failure modes, and safe inferential claims.
  This paper provides a practical decision framework for all seven measures, organized around three prescriptive questions for each: (i) what question does the measure answer and in which AI context; (ii) which estimator is appropriate for the data type and dimensionality; and (iii) what is the most dangerous misuse. The framework is operationalized in two complementary artifacts: a measure-selection flowchart and a master decision table. We cover both AI/ML and decision-making agent application domains per measure, with standardized Bridge Boxes linking IT quantities to cognitive constructs. Three worked examples illustrate the framework on concrete practitioner scenarios spanning representation learning, temporal influence analysis, and evolved agent complexity.

### [145] [Domain-Filtered Knowledge Graphs from Sparse Autoencoder Features](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23829)
*John Winnicki,Abeynaya Gnanasekaran,Eric Darve*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper turns large, flat sparse-autoencoder feature inventories into structured, domain-specific knowledge graphs so we can see how a language model represents and connects concepts.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Sparse autoencoders give many interpretable features for language models, but these features are flat, noisy, and lack structure: domain-relevant concepts are mixed with generic or weakly grounded features, related ideas are scattered, and relationships between features are opaque. The authors want a way to organize these features into meaningful conceptual structures so that model knowledge and reasoning can be understood and audited.

Method: 1) Start from a large SAE feature inventory and construct a strict, domain-specific concept set using contrastive activations and multi-stage filtering to remove generic or weak features. 2) On this filtered concept set, build two aligned graph views: (a) a co-occurrence graph capturing corpus-level conceptual structure at multiple granularities, and (b) a transcoder-based mechanism graph that links features across layers via sparse latent pathways. 3) Use automated edge labeling to annotate relations in both graphs so that they become readable knowledge graphs rather than unlabeled node-link diagrams. 4) Evaluate the approach on a biology textbook as a case study.

Result: On a biology textbook, the method recovers coherent chapter- and subchapter-level conceptual structure; it identifies concepts that bridge adjacent topics; and it compresses complex, sentence-level activation patterns (thousands of features) into small, readable graph-based views that visibly track the model’s local conceptual activity.

Conclusion: By constructing domain-specific concept universes and layered, labeled graphs on top of SAE features, the paper reinterprets a flat SAE inventory as an internal knowledge graph of the model. This enables global mapping of model knowledge and supports audits of whether the model’s internal reasoning faithfully tracks domain concepts, thus extending feature-level interpretability to structured, conceptual interpretability.

Abstract: Sparse autoencoders (SAEs) extract millions of interpretable features from a language model, but flat feature inventories aren't very useful on their own. Domain concepts get mixed with generic and weakly grounded features, while related ideas are scattered across many units, and there's no way to understand relationships between features. We address this by first constructing a strict domain-specific concept universe from a large SAE inventory using contrastive activations and a multi-stage filtering process. Next, we build two aligned graph views on the filtered set: a co-occurrence graph for corpus-level conceptual structure, organized at multiple levels of granularity, and a transcoder-based mechanism graph that links source-layer and target-layer features through sparse latent pathways. Automated edge labeling then turns these graph views into readable knowledge graphs rather than unlabeled layouts. In a case study on a biology textbook, these graphs recover coherent chapter and subchapter-level structure, reveal concepts that bridge neighboring topics, and transform messy sentence-level activity containing thousands of features into compact, readable views that illustrate the model's local activity. Taken together, this reframes a flat SAE inventory as an internal knowledge graph that converts feature-level interpretability into a global map of model knowledge and enables audits of reasoning faithfulness.

### [146] [ClawTrace: Cost-Aware Tracing for LLM Agent Skill Distillation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23853)
*Boqin Yuan,Renchu Song,Yue Su,Sen Yang,Jing Qin*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces ClawTrace and CostCraft to make LLM agent skill distillation cost-aware using detailed per-step traces.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing skill-distillation pipelines for LLM agents lack per-step cost information, so they cannot tell whether to add necessary steps or remove unnecessary expensive ones, leading to suboptimal and possibly costly behaviors.

Method: They build ClawTrace, an agent tracing platform that logs each LLM call, tool use, and sub-agent spawn, summarizing sessions into TraceCards (YAML with per-step cost, token counts, and redundancy flags). On top of this, they design CostCraft, a pipeline that reads TraceCards and generates three types of patches: Preserve (keep successful behaviors), Prune (remove costly, unnecessary steps with counterfactual justification), and Repair (fix failures using oracle evidence).

Result: On 30 held-out SpreadsheetBench tasks, both cost attribution and prune patches separately reduce quality regressions. When transferring to 30 unrelated SkillsBench tasks, prune patches generalize well and reduce median cost by 32%, but preserve patches overfit to benchmark-specific conventions and hurt performance on new tasks.

Conclusion: Cost-aware tracing and distillation can effectively optimize LLM agent skills by pruning unnecessary expensive steps that transfer across tasks, while naive preservation of successful behavior may not generalize. ClawTrace and TraceCards are released as infrastructure to support broader cost-aware agent research.

Abstract: Skill-distillation pipelines learn reusable rules from LLM agent trajectories, but they lack a key signal: how much each step costs. Without per-step cost, a pipeline cannot distinguish adding a missing step to fix a bug from removing an expensive step that never affected the outcome. We introduce ClawTrace, an agent tracing platform that records every LLM call, tool use, and sub-agent spawn during an agent session and compiles each session into a TraceCard: a compact YAML summary with per-step USD cost, token counts, and redundancy flags. Built on ClawTrace, CostCraft is a distillation pipeline that reads TraceCards and produces three types of skill patches. Preserve patches keep behaviors that led to success. Prune patches remove expensive steps that did not matter, each backed by a counterfactual argument against a named high-cost step. Repair patches fix failures grounded in oracle evidence. Ablations on 30 held-out SpreadsheetBench tasks show that both cost attribution and prune patches independently reduce quality regressions. When the same skill is applied to 30 unrelated SkillsBench tasks, an unexpected asymmetry emerges: prune rules transferred across benchmarks and cut median cost by 32%, while preserve rules, trained on benchmark-specific conventions, caused regressions on new task types. We release ClawTrace and TraceCards as open infrastructure for cost-aware agent research.

### [147] [Does Machine Unlearning Preserve Clinical Safety? A Risk Analysis for Medical Image Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23854)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current machine unlearning methods are mainly evaluated on efficiency and privacy criteria and largely ignore that, in clinical diagnosis, false negatives (missed disease) are far more harmful than false positives. There is a need to understand whether unlearning procedures used to comply with data protection regulations might inadvertently increase clinical risk when models forget specific patient data.

Method: The authors analyze common unlearning strategies—Fine-Tuning, Random Labeling, and SalUn—in binary medical image classification tasks, focusing on how they affect false-negative rates and a global risk metric with asymmetric costs. They evaluate these methods on DermaMNIST and PathMNIST under 20% and 50% data removal conditions.

Result: Experiments show that standard unlearning strategies often reduce test performance and, more critically, increase false-negative rates, which amplifies clinical risk. In contrast, SalUn-CRA maintains effective unlearning while achieving lower or comparable global clinical risk (with asymmetric misclassification costs) relative to full retraining, across both DermaMNIST and PathMNIST and for both 20% and 50% data removal settings.

Conclusion: Machine unlearning methods can have unintended clinical consequences by increasing false negatives, so clinical risk—not just privacy and efficiency—must be explicitly considered when designing and validating unlearning for medical AI. The proposed SalUn-CRA method demonstrates that incorporating clinical risk awareness into the unlearning process can mitigate these harms while preserving unlearning effectiveness.

Abstract: The application of Deep Learning in medical diagnosis must balance patient safety with compliance with data protection regulations. Machine Unlearning enables the selective removal of training data from deployed models. However, most methods are validated primarily through efficiency and privacy-oriented metrics, with limited attention to clinically asymmetric error costs. In this work, we investigate how unlearning affects clinical risk in binary medical image classification. We show that standard unlearning strategies (Fine-Tuning, Random Labeling, and SalUn) may reduce test utility while increasing false-negative rates, thereby amplifying clinical risk. To mitigate this, we propose SalUn-CRA (Clinical Risk-Aware), a variant of SalUn that replaces random relabeling with entropy-based forgetting for malignant samples in the forget set, preventing the model from learning harmful benign associations. We evaluate on DermaMNIST and PathMNIST medical image datasets under 20% and 50% data removal. Using Global Risk metrics with asymmetric costs, SalUn-CRA achieves lower or comparable clinical risk to full retraining while preserving unlearning effectiveness. These results suggest that clinical risk should be an integral component of unlearning validation in medical systems.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Safety-critical industries need time-series forecasting tools that comply with strict regulations (EU AI Act, IEC 61508, ISA/IEC 62443, Cyber Resilience Act). Existing compliance solutions wrap around ML libraries instead of being natively integrated, making them brittle, hard to verify, and difficult to maintain. The authors aim to provide a forecasting library where compliance and security are built-in rather than bolted on.

Method: The authors design spotforecast2-safe around four mandatory code rules (no dead code, deterministic processing, fail-safe behavior, minimal dependencies) and aligned process rules (model cards, executable docstrings, CI workflows, CPE identifier, REUSE-compliant licensing, formal release pipelines). They deliberately exclude features like interactive visualizations, AutoML, deep learning, and LLM backends to reduce attack surface, nondeterminism, and reproducibility issues. They encode regulatory requirements in API contracts, persistence formats, and CI gates and build a bidirectional traceability matrix that links each legal requirement to concrete technical mechanisms.

Result: The outcome is an open-source Python library for point forecasting whose architecture, development workflow, and documentation are tightly coupled to regulatory and security standards. The traceability matrix and CI gates provide demonstrable evidence of compliance. A case study on electricity generation, transmission, and consumption forecasting in the European market shows the approach is practically applicable.

Conclusion: Embedding compliance and safety constraints directly into a forecasting library’s design and tooling is feasible and beneficial for safety-critical time-series applications. By sacrificing some flexibility and advanced ML features in favor of determinism, reduced attack surface, and traceability, spotforecast2-safe provides a structured path to meeting current and emerging regulatory requirements in the EU and industrial control contexts.

Abstract: With spotforecast2-safe we present an integrated Compliance-by-Design approach to Python-based point forecasting of time series in safety-critical environments. A review of the relevant open-source tooling shows that existing compliance solutions operate consistently outside of the library to be used - e.g. as scanners, templates, or runtime layers. spotforecast2-safe takes the inverse approach and anchors the requirements of Regulation (EU) 2024/1689 (the EU AI Act, in German: KI-VO), of IEC 61508, of the ISA/IEC 62443 standards series, and of the Cyber Resilience Act within the library: in application-programming-interface contracts, persistence formats, and continuous-integration gates. The approach is operationalised by four non-negotiable code-development rules (zero dead code, deterministic processing, fail-safe handling, minimal dependencies) together with the corresponding process rules (model card, executable docstrings, CI workflows, Common-Platform-Enumeration (CPE) identifier, REUSE-conformant licensing, release pipeline). Interactive visualisation, hyperparameter tuning and automated machine learning (AutoML), as well as deep-learning and large-language-model backends are deliberately excluded, because each of these components either enlarges the attack surface, introduces non-determinism, or impairs reproducibility. A bidirectional traceability matrix maps every regulatory provision onto the corresponding mechanism in the code; an end-to-end example of European-market electricity generation, transmission, and consumption forecasting demonstrates the application. The package is open-source and available under Affero General Public License (AGPL) 3.0-or-later.

TL;DR: ZenBrain is a neuroscience-inspired multi-layer memory system for AI agents that integrates 15 models to support consolidation, forgetting, reconsolidation, and predictive memory, outperforming flat memory baselines on long-term memory benchmarks with strong ablation evidence.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current AI agent memories are usually flat stores or engineering-inspired systems that ignore key neuroscience principles like consolidation, forgetting, reconsolidation, neuromodulation, and sleep-like restructuring. This limits robustness, efficiency, and long-horizon performance, especially under stress, noise, or long time spans. The paper aims to design an AI memory architecture that aligns more closely with how biological memory works, to achieve better stability, prioritization, and long-term recall while remaining efficient in token usage.

Method: The authors design ZenBrain, a seven-layer memory architecture (working, short-term, episodic, semantic, procedural, core, cross-context) inspired by human memory systems. They couple these layers with nine foundational algorithms including a Two-Factor Synaptic Model, a vmPFC-coupled FSRS scheduler, Simulation-Selection sleep, Bayesian confidence estimation, and others. On top of these they introduce six Predictive Memory Architecture components: a multi-channel neuromodulator engine, a prediction-error-driven reconsolidation engine, a TripleCopyMemory mechanism with divergent decay rates, a 4D priority map with a fast emotional pathway, a stability-protection mechanism analogous to biological constraint molecules, and a metacognitive bias monitor. They then evaluate the architecture via extensive ablation over all 15 algorithms, stress-testing performance, and benchmarking on long-term memory tasks (LoCoMo, MemoryArena, LongMemEval-500) against other memory systems, while tracking metrics like F1, NDCG@10, stability, storage cost, and token budget.

Result: Ablations show that many of the 15 algorithms contribute synergistically, especially under stress: 9 of them become individually critical with large quality drops (up to -93.7%) when removed. Simulation-Selection sleep significantly improves stability (37%) while reducing storage by almost half. TripleCopyMemory sustains high retention after 30 days (S(t)=0.912), and the PriorityMap nearly perfects ranking quality (NDCG@10=0.997). The multi-layer routing architecture outperforms a flat baseline by about 20% F1 on two benchmarks (LoCoMo and MemoryArena). On LongMemEval-500, ZenBrain achieves the best average rank across all judge configurations and significantly outperforms competitors (letta, a-mem, mem0) with medium effect sizes, and approaches oracle accuracy (91.3%) at a drastically reduced token budget (1/106 per query). The system is released with over 11k automated tests, supporting reproducibility.

Conclusion: Integrating biologically inspired mechanisms—such as layered memory types, synaptic-like learning rules, sleep-like consolidation, neuromodulation, and reconsolidation—into AI agent memory yields more stable, efficient, and high-performing long-term memory behavior than flat engineered stores. ZenBrain demonstrates that a cooperative network of memory algorithms is especially important under stress and long horizons, and that predictive, priority-based, and metacognitively monitored memory systems can substantially close the gap toward oracle performance while being resource-efficient. The open-source release plus 11,589 tests positions ZenBrain as a robust reference architecture for future agent memory research.

Abstract: Despite a century of empirical memory research, existing AI agent memory systems rely on system-engineering metaphors (virtual-memory paging, flat LLM storage, Zettelkasten notes), none integrating principles of consolidation, forgetting, and reconsolidation.
  We present ZenBrain, a multi-layer memory architecture integrating fifteen neuroscience models. It implements seven memory layers (working, short-term, episodic, semantic, procedural, core, cross-context) orchestrated by nine foundational algorithms (Two-Factor Synaptic Model, vmPFC-coupled FSRS, Simulation-Selection sleep, Bayesian confidence, and five more) plus six new Predictive Memory Architecture (PMA) components: a four-channel NeuromodulatorEngine, prediction-error-gated ReconsolidationEngine, TripleCopyMemory with divergent decay, four-dimensional PriorityMap with amygdala fast-path, StabilityProtector (NogoA/HDAC3 analogue), and MetacognitiveMonitor for bias detection.
  The 15-algorithm ablation reveals a cooperative survival network: under stress, 9 of 15 algorithms become individually critical (delta-Q up to -93.7%, Wilcoxon, 10 seeds, alpha=0.005). Simulation-Selection sleep achieves 37% stability improvement (p<0.005) with 47.4% storage reduction. TripleCopyMemory retains S(t)=0.912 at 30 days; PriorityMap reaches NDCG@10=0.997.
  Multi-layer routing beats a flat single-layer baseline by 20.7% F1 on LoCoMo (p<0.005) and 19.5% on MemoryArena (p=0.015). On LongMemEval-500, ZenBrain holds the highest mean rank on all 12 system-judge cells (4 systems x 3 LLM judges), three-judge mean J=0.545 vs letta=0.485, a-mem=0.414, mem0=0.394; all 9 pair-wise contrasts clear Bonferroni (alpha=0.05/18, min p=6.2e-31, d in [0.18, 0.52]). Under LongMemEval's binary judge, ZenBrain reaches 91.3% of oracle accuracy at 1/106th the per-query token budget. Open-source with 11,589 automated test cases.

### [150] [MarketBench: Evaluating AI Agents as Market Participants](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23897)
*Andrey Fradkin,Rohit Krishnan*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MarketBench, a benchmark to test whether AI agents can accurately assess their own chances of success and costs when participating in market-style coordination, showing that current LLMs are miscalibrated and that limited interventions only modestly improve this.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As multi-agent AI systems and market-based coordination schemes become more popular, there is a need to understand whether AI agents can reliably self-assess their likelihood of success and associated costs so that markets can allocate tasks efficiently. Without accurate self-assessment, the theoretical benefits of market mechanisms for coordinating AI agents may not materialize, motivating a dedicated benchmark to test these capabilities.

Method: The authors construct MarketBench using a 93-task subset of SWE-bench Lite, a software engineering benchmark. They have six state-of-the-art LLMs attempt these tasks and elicit from each model self-reported estimates of (a) the probability it will successfully complete each task and (b) the expected token usage (a proxy for cost). They then simulate auctions and compare the resulting task allocation against a full-information benchmark where true success probabilities and costs are known. They also test an intervention where models are given additional contextual information about their past performance to see if calibration improves, and they implement and evaluate a market-based scaffolding system that assigns tasks based on these market interactions.

Result: The examined LLMs are significantly miscalibrated: they misestimate both their own success probabilities and token usage on tasks. As a result, auctions based on their self-reported beliefs lead to task allocations that differ substantially from the theoretically optimal full-information allocation. Supplying models with extra context summarizing prior experimental results improves calibration somewhat but only partially closes the gap with the full-information benchmark. The market-based scaffolding system’s performance is also constrained by these miscalibration issues.

Conclusion: MarketBench reveals that current LLMs lack sufficiently accurate self-assessment of success probabilities and costs to support efficient market-based coordination in multi-agent systems. Even with additional information about past performance, calibration improves only modestly, indicating that self-assessment remains a major bottleneck. The findings suggest that better methods for eliciting and calibrating AI agents’ beliefs about their own capabilities and resource usage are crucial for realizing the promise of market-style coordination of AI agents.

Abstract: Markets are a promising way to coordinate AI agent activity for similar reasons to those used to justify markets more broadly. In order to effectively participate in markets, agents need to have informative signals of their own ability to successfully complete a task and the cost of doing so. We propose MarketBench, a benchmark for assessing whether AI agents have these capabilities. We use a 93-task subset of SWE-bench Lite, a software engineering benchmark, with six recently released LLMs as a demonstration. These LLMs are miscalibrated on both success probability and token usage, and auctions built from these self-reports diverge from a full-information allocation. A follow-up intervention where we add information about capabilities from prior experiments to the context improves calibration, but only modestly narrows the gap to a full-information benchmark. We also document the performance of a market-based scaffolding with these LLMs. Our results point to self-assessment as a key bottleneck for market-style coordination of AI agents.

Abstract: We instruct an AI agent to construct two separate agentic AI platforms: one for autonomous training of predictive ML models for human-human and virus-human PPI, and the other for inducing explicit general rules governing human-human and virus-human PPI. The first agentic AI platform for autonomous training of predictive ML models for PPI is designed to consist of five AI agents that handle autonomous data collection, data verification, feature embedding, model design, and training and validation on three-way protein-disjoint cross-fold datasets. For human-human and human-virus PPIs, the final three-way protein-disjoint ensemble achieves an accuracy of 87.3% and 86.5%, respectively. For cross-checking and interpretability purposes, the second agentic AI platform is designed to replace ML predictions with human-readable rules derived from protein embeddings, physicochemical autocovariance descriptors, compartment annotations, pathway-domain overlap, and graph contexts. For human-human PPI, it is defined by a two-rule induction, whereas human-virus is induced by a more complex set of weighted rules. The rules induced by the second agentic platform align with the SHAP-identified features from the predictive ML models built by the first agentic platform. Taken together, our work demonstrates the agentic AI's ability to orchestrate from data planning to execution, and from rule induction to explanation in ML, opening the door to various applications.

### [157] [Representational Curvature Modulates Behavioral Uncertainty in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23985)
*Jack King,Evelina Fedorenko,Eghbal A. Hosseini*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper links the geometric curvature of internal representations in LLMs to their token-level uncertainty, showing that straighter representational trajectories correspond to lower next-token entropy and can be used to modestly control model uncertainty.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: While prior work on temporal straightening in autoregressive LLMs showed that internal representations become progressively straighter across layers, it lacked a direct connection between this geometric property and token-level behavior such as prediction uncertainty. The authors aim to establish whether and how the geometry of representation trajectories—specifically their curvature over recent context—systematically influences next-token entropy, and whether this relationship is causal and can be exploited during training.

Method: The authors define contextual curvature as a geometric measure of how much the representation trajectory bends over recent tokens in the hidden-state space of autoregressive LLMs. They empirically measure curvature and next-token entropy across GPT-2 XL and Pythia-2.8B, and track how their relationship emerges during training. They run perturbation experiments where they manipulate hidden states in directions aligned or misaligned with the local trajectory to change curvature and observe the effect on entropy. Finally, they modify the training objective with a regularizer that encourages straighter (lower-curvature) trajectories and evaluate its effect on token-level entropy and validation loss.

Result: They find a robust positive correlation between contextual curvature and next-token entropy across layers and training checkpoints in both GPT-2 XL and Pythia-2.8B, and show that this correlation strengthens as training progresses. Trajectory-aligned perturbations that reduce curvature reliably reduce entropy, whereas geometrically misaligned perturbations have negligible effect, indicating a selective dependence on curvature rather than generic noise. Adding a straightness regularizer during training yields modest reductions in token-level entropy without harming validation loss, suggesting that curvature is a controllable aspect of the representation that affects behavioral uncertainty.

Conclusion: The study demonstrates that the curvature of representational trajectories in autoregressive LLMs is a meaningful, task-aligned feature: higher curvature corresponds to greater uncertainty in next-token predictions, and manipulating curvature in a geometrically principled way can causally modulate entropy. Regularizing for straighter trajectories can modestly reduce uncertainty without degrading performance, supporting the view that temporal straightening is not just a byproduct of training but functionally tied to how LLMs represent context for prediction.

Abstract: In autoregressive large language models (LLMs), temporal straightening offers an account of how the next-token prediction objective shapes representations. Models learn to progressively straighten the representational trajectory of input sequences across layers, potentially facilitating next-token prediction via linear extrapolation. However, a direct link between this trajectory and token-level behavior has been missing. We provide such a link by relating contextual curvature-a geometric measure of how sharply the representational trajectory bends over recent context-to next-token entropy. Across two models (GPT-2 XL and Pythia-2.8B), contextual curvature is correlated with entropy, and this relationship emerges during training. Perturbation experiments reveal selective dependence: manipulating curvature through trajectory-aligned interventions reliably modulates entropy, while geometrically misaligned perturbations have no effect. Finally, regularizing representations to be straighter during training modestly reduces token-level entropy without degrading validation loss. These results identify trajectory curvature as a task-aligned representational feature that influences behavioral uncertainty in LLMs.

</details>


### [158] [Failure-Centered Runtime Evaluation for Deployed Trilingual Public-Space Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.23990)
*M. Meng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Introduces PSA-Eval, a failure-centered runtime evaluation framework for trilingual public-space agents that reveals cross-language performance drift hidden by aggregate scores.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard evaluations focus on static input-output mappings and aggregate scores, which can miss important, structured failures in real deployed systems, especially in multilingual settings. There is a need for runtime-focused, failure-centered evaluation that can detect cross-language policy drift in public-space agents.

Method: Propose PSA-Eval, which reframes evaluation from scores to failures and extends the evaluation chain from Question->Answer->Score->End to a richer pipeline including Batch, Run, Failure Case, Repair, and Regression Batch. Use trilingual equivalent questions as controlled probes to evaluate a deployed trilingual public-space digital front-desk system in a pilot study, keeping the foundation model constant across languages to isolate cross-language drift effects.

Result: In a pilot with 81 samples grouped into 27 trilingual question sets, the system attains a high average score (23.15/24), but 14 groups show non-zero cross-language score drift, 5 groups have drift ≥3 points, and the maximum drift is 9 points, indicating substantial language-dependent behavior despite similar inputs and a single underlying model.

Conclusion: Failure-centered runtime evaluation surfaces structured cross-language deployment issues that aggregate high scores would otherwise hide, suggesting that PSA-Eval is a useful framework for evaluating and improving trilingual public-space agents and potentially other deployed LLM systems.

Abstract: This paper presents PSA-Eval, a failure-centered runtime evaluation framework for deployed trilingual public-space agents. The central claim is that, when the evaluation object shifts from a static input-output mapping to a runtime system, the basic unit of analysis should shift from score to failure. PSA-Eval extends the conventional chain Question -> Answer -> Score -> End into Question -> Batch -> Run -> Score -> Failure Case -> Repair -> Regression Batch, making failures traceable, reviewable, repairable, and regression-testable.
  The framework uses trilingual equivalent inputs as controlled probes for observing group-level cross-language policy drift. We conduct a pilot study on a real trilingual digital front-desk system deployed in the lobby of an international financial institution. The pilot uses a simplified single-foundation-model setting (MA = MB), so the observed drift should not be interpreted as an A/B foundation-model difference. The study contains 81 samples organized into 27 trilingual equivalent question groups. Although the system achieves an average score of 23.15/24, 14 groups show non-zero cross-language score drift, 5 groups show drift of at least 3 points, and the maximum drift reaches 9 points. These results provide initial evidence that failure-centered runtime evaluation can expose structured deployment signals hidden by aggregate scoring.

For evaluation, the same set of questions is posed to machine-generated reports, and the system’s answers are compared to the gold answers to compute an accuracy-based score, providing interpretable, attribute-level error analysis.

Result: Using CT-FineBench to evaluate CT report generation systems yields metric scores that align more closely with expert clinicians’ judgments than traditional metrics do. The benchmark demonstrates substantially higher sensitivity to fine-grained factual errors, detecting specific clinical inaccuracies that overlap-based metrics often miss.

Second, we systematically transform these attributes into a QA dataset, where questions probe for specific clinical details grounded in gold-standard reports. The evaluation protocol for CT-FineBench involves using this QA dataset to query a machine-generated report and scoring the correctness of the answers. This allows for a comprehensive, interpretable, and clinically-relevant assessment, moving beyond superficial lexical overlap to pinpoint specific clinical errors. Experiments show that CT-FineBench correlates better with expert clinical assessment and is substantially more sensitive to fine-grained factual errors than prior metrics.

### [160] [QED: An Open-Source Multi-Agent System for Generating Mathematical Proofs on Open Problems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24021)
*Chenyang An,Qihao Ye,Minghao Pan,Jiayaun Zhang*

  <summary>Details</summary>

The authors aim to understand why current systems fail and how to design AI architectures that can overcome these obstacles to contribute real mathematical advances.

Method: The authors perform systematic experiments with frontier LLMs on research-level proof tasks and analyze their behavior, identifying seven characteristic failure modes in how they attempt proofs: context contamination, citation hallucination, hand-waving on crucial steps and misallocated effort, unstable proof planning, unfocused verification, problem modification, and reliance on a single model. Conclusion: The study suggests that the main obstacle to AI-generated research-level proofs is not purely model capability but the surrounding system design. By explicitly addressing common failure modes via a multi-agent architecture, AI systems like QED can produce original, nontrivial proofs to open problems. This demonstrates a concrete path toward AI systems that can contribute to mathematical research, and the authors release QED as open-source software to facilitate further work.

Abstract: We explore a central question in AI for mathematics: can AI systems produce original, nontrivial proofs for open research problems? Despite strong benchmark performance, producing genuinely novel proofs remains an outstanding challenge for LLMs. Through systematic experiments with frontier LLMs on research-level proof tasks, we identify seven failure modes that prevent reliable proof generation, including context contamination, citation hallucination, hand-waving on key steps and misallocation of proof effort, unstable proof plans, unfocused verification, problem modification and single-model bottleneck. We argue that the gap between benchmark success and research-level proving is primarily one of system design, due to those failure modes. We present QED, an open-source multi-agent proof system in which each architectural decision directly addresses a specific failure mode. Evaluated on five open problems in applied analysis and PDEs contributed by domain experts, QED produces correct proofs for three problems, each verified by the contributing experts as original and nontrivial. QED is released as open-source software at https://github.com/proofQED/QED.

### [161] [AgentPulse: A Continuous Multi-Signal Framework for Evaluating AI Agents in Deployment](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24038)
*Yuxuan Gao,Megan Wang,Yi Ling Yu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AgentPulse is a continuous, multi-signal framework for evaluating AI agents in the wild, capturing adoption and ecosystem dynamics beyond static benchmark scores.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Static benchmarks only show how AI agents perform at one point in time and fail to capture how they are actually adopted, maintained, and experienced in real-world deployment. There is a need for a systematic way to continuously measure real-world impact and uptake of agents across tools, platforms, and communities.

Method: The authors construct AgentPulse, a continuous evaluation framework that scores 50 AI agents across 10 workload categories. It aggregates 18 real-time signals from GitHub, package registries, IDE marketplaces, social media, and benchmark leaderboards into four factors: Benchmark Performance, Adoption Signals, Community Sentiment, and Ecosystem Health. They then analyze correlations among these factors, and run a circularity-controlled predictive test of a Benchmark+Sentiment sub-composite against external adoption proxies (GitHub stars, Stack Overflow questions, VS Code installs). They also compare AgentPulse-based rankings to benchmark-only rankings on the subset of agents with SWE-bench scores.

Result: The four composite factors are largely complementary, with low to moderate correlations (maximum ρ=0.61, most |ρ|≤0.37), indicating they capture different aspects of agent status. The Benchmark+Sentiment sub-composite, which excludes GitHub-derived signals, significantly predicts external adoption proxies it does not aggregate: GitHub stars (ρ_s=0.52, p<0.01), Stack Overflow question volume (ρ_s=0.49, p<0.01), and VS Code installs (ρ_s=0.44, p<0.05, though based on only 11 agents). For the subset of 11 agents with SWE-bench scores, composite vs benchmark-only rankings are weakly correlated (ρ_s=0.25), with most agents shifting by at least two ranks, due to a negative correlation between Adoption and Capability for some closed-source, high-capability agents.

Conclusion: AgentPulse successfully captures deployment and ecosystem signals that static benchmarks miss, demonstrating that benchmark performance alone is not a reliable proxy for real-world adoption and community engagement. The framework should be viewed as a methodology for multi-factor, continuous evaluation rather than as a definitive ranking of agents. All data, scores, and the evaluation harness are openly released under CC BY 4.0 to support further research and iteration.

Abstract: Static benchmarks measure what AI agents can do at a fixed point in time but not how they are adopted, maintained, or experienced in deployment. We introduce AgentPulse, a continuous evaluation framework scoring 50 agents across 10 workload categories along four factors (Benchmark Performance, Adoption Signals, Community Sentiment, and Ecosystem Health) aggregated from 18 real-time signals across GitHub, package registries, IDE marketplaces, social platforms, and benchmark leaderboards. Three analyses ground the framework. The four factors capture largely complementary information (n=50; $ρ_{\max}=0.61$ for Adoption-Ecosystem, all others $|ρ| \leq 0.37$). A circularity-controlled test (n=35) shows the Benchmark+Sentiment sub-composite, which contains no GitHub-derived signals, predicts external adoption proxies it does not aggregate: GitHub stars ($ρ_s=0.52$, $p<0.01$) and Stack Overflow question volume ($ρ_s=0.49$, $p<0.01$), with VS Code installs ($ρ_s=0.44$, $p<0.05$) reported as illustrative given that only 11 of 35 agents have non-zero installs. On the n=11 subset with published SWE-bench scores, composite and benchmark-only rankings are nearly uncorrelated ($ρ_s=0.25$; 9 of 11 agents shift by at least 2 ranks), driven by a strong negative Adoption-Capability correlation among closed-source high-capability agents within this subset. This is precisely why we rest the framework's validity claim on the broader n=35 test rather than the SWE-bench overlap. AgentPulse surfaces deployment signal absent from benchmarks; it is a methodology, not a ground-truth ranking. The framework, all collected signals, scoring outputs, and evaluation harness are released under CC BY 4.0.

</details>


### [162] [A2DEPT: Large Language Model-Driven Automated Algorithm Design via Evolutionary Program Trees](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24043)

Abstract: Designing heuristics for combinatorial optimization problems (COPs) is a fundamental yet challenging task that traditionally requires extensive domain expertise. Recently, Large Language Model (LLM)-based Automated Heuristic Design (AHD) has shown promise in autonomously generating heuristic components with minimal human intervention. However, most existing LLM-based AHD methods enforce fixed algorithmic templates to ensure executability, which confines the search to component-level tuning and limits system-level algorithmic expressiveness. To enable open-ended solver synthesis beyond rigid templates, we propose Automated Algorithm Design via Evolutionary Program Trees (A2DEPT), which treats LLMs as system-level algorithm architects. A2DEPT explores the vast program space via a tree-structured evolutionary search with hybrid selection and hierarchical operators, enabling iterative refinement of complete algorithms. To make open-ended generation practical, we enforce executability with a lightweight program-maintenance loop that performs feedback-driven repair. In experiments, A2DEPT consistently outperforms representative LLM-based baselines on both standard and highly constrained benchmarks. On the standard benchmarks, it reduces the mean normalized optimality gap by 9.8% relative to the strongest competing AHD baseline.

### [163] [Grounding Before Generalizing: How AI Differs from Humans in Causal Transfer](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24062)
*Liangru Xiang,Yuxi Ma,Zhihao Cao,Yixin Zhu,Song-Chun Zhu*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper evaluates whether large language models and vision-language models can learn and transfer abstract causal structures across contexts, finding that they lack human-like, decontextualized causal schemas and depend on environment-specific grounding instead.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Humans can quickly infer latent causal structures (like common-cause and common-effect patterns) and transfer this knowledge to new situations with minimal data, something classical reinforcement learning systems struggle with. It is unknown whether modern large-scale AI models share this human capability for abstract causal structure learning and transfer, especially in interactive, sequential tasks. The authors aim to fill this gap by rigorously testing LLMs and VLMs on a controlled causal learning paradigm and comparing them to human performance.

Method: The authors use the OpenLock paradigm, an interactive task requiring agents to discover and exploit Common Cause (CC) and Common Effect (CE) causal structures by sequentially trying combinations to open locks. They evaluate a range of LLMs and VLMs under different input modalities: text-only, image-only, and combined text-and-image. They analyze models’ learning curves, transfer performance across environments, and asymmetries in handling CC vs CE structures, and compare these quantitatively to human learners who perform the same tasks.

Result: Models show delayed or absent transfer of causal structure compared to humans. They typically need an initial phase of environment-specific trial-and-error mapping—“environmental grounding”—before any efficiency improvement appears, whereas humans use prior structural knowledge immediately. In text-only conditions, some models match or surpass human efficiency in discovering solutions, but adding visual information (image-only or text-plus-image) generally worsens performance. Models also display systematic asymmetries between handling Common Cause and Common Effect structures that do not appear in human data, indicating biased heuristics rather than neutral causal abstraction.

Conclusion: Current large language models and vision-language models do not form decontextualized, human-like causal schemas that support immediate analogical transfer across environments. Instead, their transfer depends on environment-specific grounding and is fragile, particularly when visual information is involved. The observed CC/CE asymmetries and degradation with vision suggest that these systems rely predominantly on symbolic pattern processing rather than integrated multimodal causal reasoning. This grounding-dependent transfer is identified as a fundamental limitation of present LLMs and VLMs for human-like causal and analogical reasoning.

Abstract: Extracting abstract causal structures and applying them to novel situations is a hallmark of human intelligence. While Large Language Models (LLMs) and Vision Language Models (VLMs) have shown strong performance on a wide range of reasoning tasks, their capacity for interactive causal learning -- inducing latent structures through sequential exploration and transferring them across contexts -- remains uncharacterized. Human learners accomplish such transfer after minimal exposure, whereas classical Reinforcement Learning (RL) agents fail catastrophically. Whether state-of-the-art Artificial Intelligence (AI) models possess human-like mechanisms for abstract causal structure transfer is an open question. Using the OpenLock paradigm requiring sequential discovery of Common Cause (CC) and Common Effect (CE) structures, here we show that models exhibit fundamentally delayed or absent transfer: even successful models require initial environmental-specific mapping -- what we term environmental grounding -- before efficiency gains emerge, whereas humans leverage prior structural knowledge from the very first solution attempt. In the text-only condition, models matched or exceeded human discovery efficiency. In contrast, visual information -- in both the image-only and text-and-image conditions -- overall degraded rather than enhanced performance, revealing a broad reliance on symbolic processing rather than integrated multimodal reasoning. Models further exhibited systematic CC/CE asymmetries absent in humans, suggesting heuristic biases rather than direction-neutral causal abstraction. These findings reveal that large-scale statistical learning does not produce the decontextualized causal schemas underpinning human analogical reasoning, establishing grounding-dependent transfer as a fundamental limitation of current LLMs and VLMs.

The improvement is more pronounced in higher-entropy (more uncertain) settings, indicating a nonlinear attenuation of uncertainty effects when internal structural factors are included in the stability measure.

Conclusion: The proposed framework does not claim to uncover a physical law or complete ethical theory but offers a compact, interpretable modeling perspective that unifies uncertainty, performance, and internal structure into a single evaluative lens. It appears particularly informative under high-uncertainty conditions and is positioned as a complement—not a replacement—to existing benchmarks, aiming to inform debates and practices in AI safety, reliability, and governance.

Abstract: As large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed in high-stakes and operational settings, evaluation strategies based solely on aggregate accuracy are often insucient to characterize system reliability. This study proposes a thermodynamic inspired modeling framework for analyzing the stability of LLM outputs under conditions of uncertainty and perturbation. The framework introduces a composite stability score that integrates task utility, entropy as a measure of external uncertainty, and two internal structural proxies: internal integration and aligned reective capacity. Rather than interpreting these quantities as physical variables, the formulation is intended as an interpretable abstraction that captures how internal structure may modulate the impact of disorder on model behavior. Using the IST-20 benchmarking protocol and associated metadata, we analyze 80 modelscenario observations across four contemporary LLMs. The proposed formulation consistently yields higher stability scores than a reduced utilityentropy baseline, with a mean improvement of 0.0299 (95% CI: 0.02470.0351). The observed gain is more pronounced under higher entropy conditions, suggesting that the framework captures a form of nonlinear attenuation of uncertainty. We do not claim a fundamental physical law or a complete theory of machine ethics. Instead, the contribution of this work is a compact and interpretable modeling perspective that connects uncertainty, performance, and internal structure within a unied evaluation lens. The framework is intended to complement existing benchmarking approaches and to support ongoing discussions in AI safety, reliability, and governance.

Conclusion: The framework offers a thermodynamics- and information-geometry-based paradigm for AI safety and ethical alignment, replacing ad hoc rules with a stability notion rooted in physical work and entropy; systemic and ethical violations are thus reframed as physically quantifiable deviations from a theoretically grounded manifold, supporting more principled anomaly detection in autonomous systems.

Abstract: This study introduces the Kerimov-Alekberli model, a novel information-geometric framework that redefines AI safety by formally linking non-equilibrium thermodynamics to stochastic control for the ethical alignment of autonomous systems. By establishing a formal isomorphism between non-equilibrium thermodynamics and stochastic control, we define systemic anomalies as deviations from a Riemannian manifold. The model utilizes the Kullback-Leibler divergence as the primary metric, governed by a dynamic threshold derived from the Fisher Information Metric. We further ground this framework in the Landauer Principle, proving that adversarial perturbations perform measurable physical work by increasing the system's informational entropy. Validation on the NSL-KDD dataset and unmanned aerial vehicle trajectory simulations demonstrated that our model achieves effective real-time detection via the FPT trigger, with strong performance metrics (e.g., high accuracy and low FPR) on benchmark datasets. This study provides a rigorous physical foundation for AI safety, transitioning from heuristic, rule-based ethical frameworks to a thermodynamics-based stability paradigm by grounding ethical violations in quantifiable physical work and entropic information.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In decentralized factories, production machines and transportation resources (like AGVs) must be scheduled together. Multi-agent RL is promising, but most work assumes that jointly training all agents is always beneficial. Joint training is more complex and costly, and it is unclear under what concrete conditions it provides real gains over simpler modular training. Practitioners need guidance on which training strategy to use in different environments.

Method: The authors model a job-shop scheduling problem that includes both processing machines and transportation resources. They formulate it as a multi-agent reinforcement learning task, with separate agents for job scheduling and AGV scheduling. They compare two training paradigms: joint training, where agents are trained simultaneously and cooperatively, and modular training, where each agent is trained independently and then combined. They perform a systematic sensitivity analysis over key environment parameters, especially resource scarcity and temporal dominance (whether processing or transport is the main bottleneck). They define and measure a “coordination gap” as the performance difference between joint and modular training across these settings.

Result: Joint training generally achieves better performance than modular training and classical dispatching-rule combinations, demonstrating its ability to capture richer coordination between production and transport. However, the performance advantage (coordination gap) shrinks markedly in bottlenecked scenarios, especially when one resource type (processing or transport) is severely constrained and dominates the system’s behavior. In such cases, the joint coordination learned through multi-agent training adds little marginal value over modular training.

Conclusion: The necessity of joint multi-agent RL training for job-shop scheduling with transport is context-dependent. When both production and transportation resources jointly influence system performance, joint training yields meaningful coordination benefits. But in environments dominated by a single bottleneck resource, modular training can match joint training sufficiently well and is therefore a practical and simpler alternative. The paper offers actionable guidance: choose joint training in balanced or coordination-critical settings, and opt for modular training in strongly bottlenecked or single-task-dominant settings to reduce complexity without sacrificing much performance.

Abstract: Efficient job-shop scheduling with transportation resources is critical for high-performance manufacturing. With the rise of "decentralized factories", multi-agent reinforcement learning has emerged as a promising approach for the combined scheduling of production and transportation tasks. Prior work has largely focused on developing novel cooperative architectures while overlooking the question of when joint training is necessary. Joint training denotes the simultaneous training of job and automatic guided vehicle scheduling agents, whereas modular training involves independently training each agent followed by post-hoc integration. In this study, we systematically investigate the conditions under which joint training is essential for optimal performance in the job-shop scheduling problem with transportation resources. Through a rigorous sensitivity analysis of resource scarcity and temporal dominance, we quantify the coordination gap -- the performance difference between these two training modalities. In our evaluation, the joint training can produce superior performance compared to the best-performing combinations of dispatching rules and modular training. However, the coordination gap advantage diminishes in bottleneck environments, particularly under severe transport and processing constraints. These findings indicate that modular training represents a viable alternative in environments where a single scheduling task dominates. Overall, our work provides practical guidance for selecting between training modalities based on environmental conditions, enabling decision-makers to optimize reinforcement learning-based scheduling performance.

### [168] [Right-to-Act: A Pre-Execution Non-Compensatory Decision Protocol for AI Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2604.24153)
*Gadi Lavi*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper proposes a deterministic pre-execution control layer, called the Right-to-Act protocol, that decides whether AI-generated actions are even allowed to be executed, focusing on structural permission rather than optimizing decisions themselves.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As AI systems increasingly trigger real-world actions, existing safety and governance methods mostly validate or certify outputs after they are produced, assuming any generated decision is in principle executable. This creates risks for premature, irreversible, or illegitimate actions. The authors are motivated to define a framework that explicitly governs whether a decision is allowed to be acted upon at all, independent of model accuracy or confidence.

Method: The authors conceptually define and formalize a Right-to-Act protocol as a non-compensatory, deterministic decision layer that sits between AI outputs and real-world execution. They distinguish compensatory vs. non-compensatory decision regimes, specify a pre-execution legitimacy boundary, and use a scenario-based case study to show how the same AI output can be blocked, deferred, or allowed depending on whether it passes this structural check.

Result: They show, via conceptual analysis and scenario-based examples, that enforcing non-compensatory structural constraints before execution leads to different, often safer, outcomes than traditional compensatory approaches. The framework preserves reversibility, blocks premature or irreversible actions when key conditions fail, and operates independently of model architecture or training pipeline.

Conclusion: The paper concludes that AI control should not only focus on improving or validating model decisions, but also on governing their admissibility through a protocol-level abstraction. The Right-to-Act protocol provides a general, architecture-agnostic way to enforce strict pre-execution legitimacy checks, preventing action when any required condition is unmet and thus strengthening AI governance and safety in action-taking systems.

Abstract: Current AI systems increasingly operate in contexts where their outputs directly trigger real-world actions. Most existing approaches to AI safety, risk management, and governance focus on post-hoc validation, probabilistic risk estimation, or certification of model behavior. However, these approaches implicitly assume that once a decision is produced, it is eligible for execution. In this work, we introduce the Right-to-Act protocol, a deterministic, non-compensatory pre-execution decision layer that evaluates whether an AI-generated decision is permitted to be realized at all. Unlike compensatory systems, where high-confidence signals can override failed conditions, the proposed framework enforces strict structural constraints: if any required condition is unmet, execution is halted or deferred. We formalize the distinction between compensatory and non-compensatory decision regimes and define a pre-execution legitimacy boundary. Through a scenario-based case study, we demonstrate how identical AI outputs can lead to divergent outcomes when evaluated under a Right-to-Act protocol, preserving reversibility and preventing premature or irreversible actions. The proposed approach reframes AI control from optimizing decisions to governing their admissibility, introducing a protocol-level abstraction that operates independently of model architecture or training methodology.

  <summary>Details</summary>
